PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In an original action, a motion was filed by plaintiffs, a number of highway motor carriers, for a temporary injunction enjoining defendants, as constituting the New York State Tax Commission, from enforcement of a road use tax known as the Weight Distance Tax, 1951 N.Y. Laws, ch. 74, based on vehicle tonnage and miles travelled, and a cross-motion was filed by defendants for a judgment on the pleadings.


OVERVIEW: A number of highway motor carriers sought a declaration that such tax was unconstitutional and sought an injunction restraining its enforcement. Among the highway motor carriers' claims were that the taxing statute imposed an undue and unreasonable burden upon interstate commerce, that it denied them equal protection, and that it constituted an illegal delegation of legislative powers in violation of the Constitution of the State of New York. The court found that, for a determination of such motions, it was necessary first to look at the statute. The court held that the tax, which was imposed upon road use measured by a ton-mile formula and which was applicable alike to interstate and intrastate commerce in an amount not shown to be unreasonable, did not constitute a violation of the interstate commerce clause of the United States Constitution. It was held that, in determining whether a classification set forth by the taxing statute was valid, judicial function was limited to an inquiry whether a classification had a rational basis. If it had, one affected by statute would not be heard to complain that another, who might also have been taxed, had been passed by.


OUTCOME: The court denied plaintiffs' injunction and granted defendants' motion for a judgment on the pleadings.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- weight-distance tax on highway motor carriers -- (1) weight-distance tax on highway motor carriers constitutional; enforcement not restrained; judgment on pleadings granted in favor of State Tax Commission -- (2) since tax is compensation for road use, although formula inexact, and is applicable to interstate and intrastate commerce, it does not violate commerce clause of Federal Constitution (art. I, § 8) -- (3) exclusion of vehicles of 18,000 pounds or less from tax based on legislative findings, proper -- (4) since omnibuses subject to another tax, exclusion of omnibuses from weight-distance tax proper -- (5) exception in favor of trucking vehicles of farmers proper -- (6) exemption of vehicles used exclusively within limits of city, village or commercial zones proper -- (7) by exempting vehicles used within limits of zones established by Public Service Commission, Legislature referred to presently  [***2] existing, well-defined zones established by said commission; law therefore does not contain unconstitutional delegation of legislative functions to administrative body -- (8) exemption of such zone traffic was not unconstitutional incorporation of statute by reference.

1. The weight-distance tax imposed upon interstate and intrastate public and private highway motor carriers by chapter 74 of the Laws of 1951 (adding sections 501-515, incl., to Tax Law), is constitutional, and a motion of plaintiffs, highway motor carriers, for a temporary injunction to restrain its enforcement should be denied and a motion of defendants, constituting the State Tax Commission, for judgment on the pleadings should be granted.

2. The State has the power to levy a tax upon vehicles as compensation for the use of highways, and such power is not barred by the commerce clause of the United States Constitution (U. S. Const., art. I, § 8). The tax must be laid upon road use rather than the privilege of doing interstate business, it must not discriminate against interstate commerce, and it must not exceed reasonable compensation for the privilege granted. The formula employed by the State to exact reasonable [***3] compensation is sufficient if it is a rough approximation equating the tax to the privilege conferred. Where the tax is truly compensatory of road use, a carrier cannot complain that the proceeds are commingled with the general funds of the State. The court cannot say that a tax upon road use measured by a ton-mile formula, applicable alike to interstate and intrastate commerce and in an amount not shown to be unreasonable, constitutes a violation of the interstate commerce clause of the United States Constitution.

3. The exclusion of vehicles of 18,000 pounds or less from the tax is rational. While there is a difference of opinion whether the true measure of road damage is gross weight or axle load, the legislative decision thereon may not be judicially disturbed. There was evidence available to the Legislature that smaller vehicles, by their license fees and gasoline taxes, meet their just share of the burden of highway maintenance to an extent not borne by heavier vehicles. It has not been shown that the line drawn at 18,000 pounds lacks substantial basis.

4. Since another statute taxes carriers of persons on the basis of gross receipts (L. 1951, ch. 601), the Legislature [***4] could properly exclude omnibuses from the operation of the weight-distance tax, which is more appropriate to cargo carriers. The existence of another tax, especially where the same taxes carriers of persons as distinguished from carriers of goods, is a good ground for exemption.

5. The exception in favor of trucking vehicles of farmers was proper. The use of highways by farmers in connection with their agricultural products in contrast to that of commercial carriers may be infrequent and incidental. Moreover, the Legislature is authorized to make a distinction in favor of farmers to the end that fiscal burdens may be borne in accordance with its conception of policy and justice.

6. The exemption of vehicles used exclusively within limits of city, village or commercial zones is proper. Such exemption is not accorded for mileage within such limits if such mileage is part of a larger use. Moreover, the Legislature may well have determined that local taxes and regulations amply cover such traffic. Its conclusion not to interfere with or duplicate municipal efforts in this field cannot, therefore, be said to be without reasonable basis. The fact that traffic within such zones uses [***5] city streets primarily, and State highways but slightly, and the practical difficulty of keeping records in such cases warrants upholding such exemption.

7. The law (Tax Law, § 503, subd. 2, par. [a]) exempts vehicles "used exclusively within the limits of any zone established by the public service commission". The zones so referred to are those which have been defined and were in existence at the time of the enactment of the law and were delineated pursuant to article 3-B of the Public Service Law. The zones had been published in the New York Official Compilation of Administrative Codes, Rules and Regulations and were well-defined geographical units. In referring to them as areas of exemption the Legislature did not unconstitutionally delegate to the Public Service Commission any of its legislative functions.

8. The exemption of zone traffic does not constitute legislative incorporation by reference, forbidden by section 16 of article III of the New York State Constitution. It is permissible to refer to another statute -- what is forbidden is to incorporate another statute. In referring to the zones in question, the Legislature took cognizance of existing geographical entities.  [***6] There was no violation of the State Constitution in so doing.

 

2821 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


New York v. Feit


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Kings County


200 Misc. 998; 110 N.Y.S.2d 425; 1951 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2791



December 21, 1951 



DISPOSITION: Motion is denied. Settle order.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff City of New York filed motions for an order pursuant to N.Y. R. Civ. Prac. 113(6), striking out two answers by defendant property owner, for summary judgment, and for judgment on the pleadings in the City's action in rem to foreclose unpaid tax liens pursuant to New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code ch. 17, tit. D.


OVERVIEW: The City filed an action to foreclose unpaid tax liens on two parcels of the owner's property. The owner filed objections and affirmative defenses, answering separately for each parcel. The court denied the City's motions for summary judgment and judgment on the pleadings, holding that the moving affidavit failed to set forth that the City believed that there was no defense to the action, and the City had failed to reply to the owner's counterclaim. However, if the procedural defects were not fatal, the owner's position could not have been sustained on the merits. The mere general denials contained in each answer were ineffective to raise a triable issue. Neither the answer nor the affidavits read in opposition to the motion affirmatively alleged that the taxes had been paid or that the property was not subject to tax. Moreover, the answer to the owner's objection with respect to the absence of a statutory provision for a public judicial sale was that knowledge of the applicable statute was imputed to the defaulting property owner. The right of redemption existed only as permitted by statute. Furthermore, taxes were not subject to counterclaim or setoff on the part of the taxpayer.


OUTCOME: The court denied the motions and discussed defenses presented for the guidance of the owner.


HEADNOTES

Taxation -- tax liens, tax sales and tax titles -- (1) in action in rem by City of New York to foreclose unpaid tax liens, defendant alleges as defense that Administrative Code of City of New York (ch. 17, tit. D) providing for foreclosure in rem is unconstitutional, and counterclaims for use and occupation of her property by plaintiff; plaintiff's motion for summary judgment denied where moving affidavits fail to set forth plaintiff's belief that there is no defense to action and where no reply to counterclaim filed -- (2) general denials in answers insufficient to create issues of fact in view of presumption of validity contained in Administrative Code (§ D17-11.0) -- (3) title D of chapter 17 of Administrative Code constitutional; defense challenging constitutionality stricken -- (4) counterclaim against city stricken; no setoff on part of taxpayer exists against claim for taxes.

1. In an action in rem by the City of New York to foreclose unpaid tax liens pursuant to title D of chapter 17 of the Administrative Code of the City of New York, defendant,  [***2] in addition to general denials, alleges as an affirmative defense that title D of chapter 17 is unconstitutional in that it does not provide for a judicial sale and in that it deprives an owner of his property without due process of law, and alleges a counterclaim to recover for the reasonable use and occupation of her property by plaintiff city in storing thereon vehicles and other equipment. Plaintiff is not entitled to summary judgment where the moving affidavit fails to set forth, as required by rule 113 of the Rules of Civil Practice, that plaintiff believes that there is no defense to the action and where plaintiff has failed to reply to defendant's counterclaim.

2. Were the procedural defects not fatal, summary judgment should be granted, for section D17-5.0 of the Administrative Code provides for the filing of a verified list of delinquent taxes in the office of the County Clerk containing the information required by the section. Such filing constitutes and has the same force and effect as the filing and recording in such office of an individual and separate notice of pendency of action and as the filing in the Supreme Court of such county of an individual and separate complaint [***3] by the city against the real property therein described to enforce the payment of delinquent taxes. Moreover, section D17-11.0 contains a presumption of validity and then states that a defendant alleging any jurisdictional defect must particularly specify in his answer such jurisdictional defect and must affirmatively establish such defense. Accordingly, the mere general denials contained in each answer were ineffective to create triable issues. Neither the answers nor the affidavits read in opposition to the motion affirmatively allege that the taxes have been paid or that the property was not subject to tax.

3. The provisions of title D of chapter 17 of the Administrative Code are analogous to the provisions of title 3 of article VII-A of the Tax Law which has been held constitutional. Accordingly, title D of chapter 17 is constitutional and a defense challenging its constitutionality should be stricken out.

4. The counterclaim for reasonable use and occupation of defendant's premises against the City of New York is insufficient, for taxes are not subject to counterclaim or setoff on the part of the taxpayer. The taxpayer may litigate any claims against the municipality by [***4] separate independent proceedings.

 

2822 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Brous v. Smith


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Suffolk County


109 N.Y.S.2d 289; 1951 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2666



December 14, 1951 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, the owner of numerous lots, sought to build homes and applied to respondent, the town building and zoning inspector, for a permit to build but was refused because roads were not installed in accordance with N.Y. Town Law § 280-a. The owner brought a proceeding in mandamus to compel the inspector to grant the permit, and alleged that § 280-a was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The owner alleged that § 280-a deprived him of his property without due process of law and denied him equal protection of the law. The court held that § 280-a was constitutional and dismissed the petition. Restrictions upon the use of property, within reasonable limits for the common good, were valid. In a time of emergency, such as sickness, accident, fire or other catastrophe, a road over which automobiles and fire apparatus could travel safely, had to always be available, otherwise great suffering, property damage and even loss of life could result. Thus, § 280-a was substantially related to the public health, safety and general welfare of the community wherein it conditioned a building permit upon a street or highway giving access to such proposed structure being duly placed on the official map or plan and being suitably improved to the satisfaction of the town board or planning board.


OUTCOME: The petition was dismissed and the statute was declared constitutional.

 

2823 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Blair v. Du Mond


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Albany County


200 Misc. 1036; 108 N.Y.S.2d 738; 1951 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2586



November 23, 1951 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff dog owner sought a judgment declaring that N.Y. Agric. & Mkts. Law § 115, pertaining to the night quarantine of dogs, was unconstitutional and restraining its enforcement against her. Defendants, the Commissioner of Agriculture and Markets of New York and the Chairman of the Columbia County Board of Supervisors, moved for judgment on the pleadings dismissing the complaint.


OVERVIEW: Pursuant to § 115, which provided that the Commissioner could by order require that all dogs in any town, city or county be securely confined between sunset and one hour after sunrise, a commissioner's order was made applicable to all of Columbia County. The dog owner contended that § 115 violated the due process clauses of the federal and state Constitutions and unlawfully delegated legislative power. The court held that: (1) due process did not preclude a use of the inherent police power of the State to protect the health and welfare of the many, even at the expense of a few; (2) the regulation of dogs under the police power had long been recognized and sustained; (3) the Commissioner's powers under § 115 to quarantine dogs at night was entirely constitutional; (4) the dog owner's argument that the portion of § 115 permitting destruction of any dog in violation of such order was obnoxious to due process was unavailing because the statute provided for notice of the quarantine, which was sufficient; and (5) the statute only conferred authority and discretion in its execution, which was not an unlawful delegation of legislative power.


OUTCOME: The court denied the dog owner's motion for a temporary injunction and granted defendants judgment on the pleadings.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- night quarantine of dogs -- (1) Agriculture and Markets Law (§ 115) authorizing Commissioner of Agriculture and Markets to order night quarantine of dogs in any county is constitutional exercise of police power -- (2) due process not violated by authorization to peace officer to shoot on sight dog at large in violation of order; notice of quarantine is sufficient -- (3) no unconstitutional delegation of legislative power given commissioner by permitting him to fix time and place where quarantine to be effective.

1. Section 115 of the Agriculture and Markets Law provides for the night quarantine of dogs and authorizes the Commissioner of Agriculture and Markets by order to provide for such quarantine in any county. Pursuant to the section, the commissioner issued an order applicable to Columbia County. The power of the commissioner under section 115 is constitutional. Dogs are property subject to the police power. The night quarantine is a valid and reasonable exercise of the police power.

2. The portion of section 115 which authorizes a peace [***2] officer to kill on sight any dog at large is not a violation of due process. The statute provides for notice of the quarantine and such notice is sufficient. A strange dog in the nighttime bent upon mischief is an elusive animal, and the Legislature in its wisdom cannot be said to have acted arbitrarily and capriciously in mandating destruction rather than capture.

3. The statute does not unconstitutionally delegate legislative power to the commissioner. The statute is complete on its face and does not provide for rules and regulations to be made by anyone. It merely leaves to the commissioner the fixing of time and place -- when and where -- the statute shall become effective. The statute confers discretion in its execution and that is not an unlawful delegation of legislative power.

American Civil Liberties Union, Inc. v. Cortlandt


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Westchester County


109 N.Y.S.2d 165; 1951 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2641



November 23, 1951 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff group of attorneys challenged the constitutionality of Town of Cortlandt, N.Y., Permit Ordinance (September 29, 1949) and Town of Cortlandt, N.Y., Prohibitory Ordinance § 2(5), (6) (October 18, 1949), contending that the ordinances infringed on the First Amendment right to freedom of assembly.


OVERVIEW: The ordinances created municipal discretion in the granting of permits by defendant town for parades, demonstrations and gathering while prohibiting activities that would result in the "break down" of law enforcement. The court found the ordinances to be unconstitutional because they were too vague and granted too much discretion to the town in determining whether to issue permits and what constituted a break down in law enforcement.


OUTCOME: The court granted judgment on the pleadings in favor of the group, finding the ordinances to be unconstitutional.

 

2825 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Crenshaw


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Bronx County


202 Misc. 179; 117 N.Y.S.2d 202; 1951 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2162



November 19, 1951 



DISPOSITION: As against the foregoing, the indictment filed against the defendants herein cannot stand and must be and hereby is dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants filed an application to dismiss the indictment against them. One defendant was charged with the possession of a revolver after his previous conviction of a crime, while his codefendant was likewise charged with the unlawful possession of the same firearm, in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 1897(4) and N.Y. Penal Law § 1898-a.


OVERVIEW: Defendants, along with their girlfriends, while sitting in an automobile owned by one defendant, were approached by a police officer and questioned about a complaint filed about a woman screaming. One of the girlfriends accused one defendant of getting fresh with her. The officer drove defendant's car to the police station with the girls inside, while defendants followed in a police car. After questioning defendants for 45 minutes, the police searched defendant's car and discovered the revolver. The court determined that defendant's car was left unattended in the police station's lot with the windows rolled down during the 45 minute preceding the discovery. The basic issue involved was one of law in which the circumstance of the entire case created a hypothesis of innocence that was equally consistent with that of guilt. Where the proof fell below the standard of rebutting the presumption of innocence, and of proving guilt beyond a reasonable doubt to warrant a conviction, as required by N.Y. Code Crim. Proc. § 389, a question of law was presented and the case could not be submitted to the jury for a verdict. The court was required to direct a verdict of acquittal.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the indictment filed against defendants.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- illegal possession of firearms -- presumption of guilt from finding revolver in automobile -- (1) Penal Law (§ 1898-a), providing that presence in automobile of weapon is presumptive evidence of its illegal possession by persons found therein at time weapon found, is constitutional -- (2) presumption not applicable to defendants who were not in physical occupancy of automobile at time weapon discovered -- (3) hypothesis of defendants' innocence from facts in case would require court to direct acquittal; indictment dismissed.

1. Section 1898-a of the Penal Law which provides that the presence in an automobile of a weapon shall be presumptive evidence of its illegal possession by the persons found in the automobile at the time the weapon is found is constitutional.

2. Such presumption is not applicable to defendants where they were not in physical occupancy of the car at the time the weapon in question was discovered by the police some forty minutes after defendants had been brought into the police [***2] precinct house. Defendants were not in constructive occupancy of the automobile.

3. The circumstances of the entire case create an hypothesis of innocence that is equally consistent with that of guilt. Under the facts of this case, as agreed upon by the defense and prosecution, upon a trial of the indictment a trial jury would not be permitted to speculate on the guilt or innocence of defendants when their guilt could not possibly be proved by the People beyond a reasonable doubt, and it would be the court's duty to direct a verdict of acquittal (Code Crim. Pro., § 389). The indictment against defendants should be dismissed.

 

2826 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Styler v Commonwealth Sanitation Co.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


City Magistrates Courts, City of New York, Municipal Term, Bronx County


107 N.Y.S.2d 982; 1951 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2459



October 20, 1951 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a motion for acquittal after being charged with violating N.Y. Sanitary Code § 181.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was a corporation and undertook a rat extermination project on federal property. In the course of the project, defendant used a very powerful poison, which was placed in paper cups. When the fluid in the cup evaporated, some poison crystals were left in the bottom of the cups. An employee of defendant failed to transport the used cups to defendant's plant and, instead, burned the cups on the open beach on federal property. Defendant was thereafter charged with violating § 181. Defendant filed a motion for acquittal, arguing that it could not be liable for the employee's acts, that the acts were not committed within the jurisdiction of the state, and that the statute was vague and indefinite. The court rejected the arguments, denied the motion, and found defendant guilty. The court first held that the state had jurisdiction because the employee's acts caused danger to life in the state. Second, the court held that §181 provided enough information to be reasonably applicable to varying fact situations. Third, the court held that the fact that the employee departed from the usual routine in the disposition of the dangerous poison cups was not a defense for defendant.


OUTCOME: The court denied the motion for acquittal and found defendant guilty.

 

2827 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Tursellino v. Paduano


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Kings County


202 Misc. 74; 107 N.Y.S.2d 839; 1951 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2433



October 17, 1951 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a consolidated action, defendant, Commissioner of Water Supply, Gas, and Electricity, filed a motion to dismiss the complaint that was filed by plaintiff corporations alleging that the Commissioner wrongfully charged them for the use of the sewer system.


OVERVIEW: The corporations initiated the declaratory judgment action and contended that the Commissioner illegally assessed charges to them for the use of the city's sewer system. The court held that the corporations were required first to appeal to an administrative body prior to initiating the declaratory action. The court determined that because the social aspects of a water supply system and sewer service system were analogous and because enabling legislation of both followed the same pattern, the legal aspects of rental charges for such services were pari passu. The court held that charges for both water and sewer service fees were valid levies placed by the Commissioner. Under N.Y. Civ. Prac. Act § 473, an action for declaratory judgment was a discretionary remedy that could have been withheld if existing forms of action were reasonably adequate. The court found that the corporations had an adequate remedy at law and that their prayer for relief failed to comply to the requirements of N.Y. R. Civ. Prac. 211. The court granted the motion to dismiss which was filed by the Commissioner.


OUTCOME: The Commissioner's motion to dismiss was granted.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- sewer rent -- (1) declaratory judgment is proper remedy for determining constitutionality of local laws imposing sewer rent on plaintiffs, ice manufacturers -- (2) ice manufacturer whose premises are mortgaged has no standing to complain that lien of sewer rent superior to mortgages previously recorded where mortgagees not parties to action -- (3) claim that sewer rent and method of fixing such rent unreasonable, overruled; question one of contract; if sewer used, plaintiffs must pay rental fixed -- (4) whether city should have chosen better method of raising funds for construction of sewage treatment plants presents question of legislative wisdom, and not judicial question -- (5) allegation that yield of sewer rentals will exceed purpose of enabling statute is conclusion; law accomplishes legitimate purpose and is constitutional -- (6) complaint alleges consumption of 95% of water and return into sewers of 5%; rental based on one third of water tax, claimed unfair to plaintiffs; local laws, rules and regulations provide special administrative and judicial procedure for industrial users such as  [***2] ice plants; such procedures give plaintiffs opportunity to be heard and for judicial review; allegation in complaint that no adequate remedy at law exists negated; declaratory judgment not needed where such procedure affords relief -- (7) since complaint lacks prayer that sewer rental be 5% of regular rent and, although complaint contains allegations sufficient to raise issue that sewer rent improperly applied, issue not litigated; complaint dismissed.

1. An action for judgment, declaring that Local Laws, 1950, No. 67 (Administrative Code, § 82d9-9.1) and No. 102 of the City of New York, which impose a sewer rent on plaintiffs, who are manufacturers of ice, are void and unconstitutional, is a proper remedy. Although rule 212 of the Rules of Civil Practice vests discretionary power in the courts, where the constitutionality of a statute is involved the remedy of relief by declaratory judgment is especially applicable.

2. Since the mortgagees of the real property belonging to one of plaintiffs are not parties to the action, said plaintiff has no standing to claim that the imposition of the sewer rental created a lien senior to mortgages executed earlier and that such was an impairment [***3] of the obligation of contract in violation of section 10 of article I of the United States Constitution. Whether it is a lien senior to the mortgages does not concern plaintiff.

3. Plaintiffs' claims, that the particular charges fixed by the commissioner, the method of fixing them, and that any charge at all are unreasonable, are overruled. There is no element of deprivation here or even of taxation but merely one of contract into which plaintiffs might or might not enter. They could use the sewer or not, as they chose. If they used it, they were required to pay the rental fixed by the city.

4. The argument that the city might have chosen a different and better method for raising the funds necessary to defray the cost of constructing sewage treatment and disposal plants, presents a question of legislative wisdom in the choice of a plan and not of its legality which alone is the subject of judicial interpretation.

5. The allegation that the dollar yield of the rent charge will be far in excess of what is required contrary to the limited purposes of the enabling statutes is conclusory, is unsupported by anything in the pleadings, and is prophesy rather than fact. The law is designed [***4] to accomplish a legitimate purpose. There is no constitutional infirmity in the statutes on any of the grounds raised by plaintiffs.

6. The complaint alleges consumption by one plaintiff of 95% of water as an industrial user with a return of only 5% of the water to the sewer. It is claimed that one sixth instead of one third of the water tax would be a fair and reasonable basis for fixing the sewer rental charge. The law, rules and regulations of the commissioner of water supply, gas and electricity together make special provision for industrial users such as ice manufacturers, whose manufacturing processes use up the water and return only the unconsumed part to the sewer. The commissioner is authorized to make such investigation and to hold hearings as he deems necessary in order to find a proper basis for the rental charge to be made to industrial users, and to notify the user of his determination. The user may then demand a hearing, after which hearing the commissioner shall notify the user of his final determination. His determination may then be reviewed under article 78 of the Civil Practice Act. These procedures insure plaintiffs an opportunity to be heard. The allegation [***5] of the complaint that plaintiffs have no adequate remedy at law is completely negated. Under such circumstances the court should not take jurisdiction. It is undesirable to permit a party to seek a declaratory judgment before his case is heard by an administrative agency.

7. Since the prayer for relief does not seek a determination of the issues as to whether plaintiffs' sewer rent should be 5% or some other percentage of the standard sewer rent of one third of the water tax and even though there still remain in the complaint sufficient allegations to state a cause of action on whether the standards laid down by the statute have been applied properly or fairly, an issue which plaintiffs have a right to raise, it cannot be litigated in this action for declaratory judgment. This is especially so since the plaintiff has no adequate remedy at law and the prayer of the complaint for relief does not encompass the request for judgment on this issue. The complaint should be dismissed.

 

2828 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Royal China v. Regal China Corp


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court, Special Term, New York County, Part III


107 N.Y.S.2d 901; 1951 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2442



October 11, 1951 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff corporation sought to enforce amendments to its Articles of Incorporation and by-laws, and defendant stockholder challenged the amendments before the court as illegal under both Ohio and New York law.


OVERVIEW: The stockholder claimed that the amendments were against both Ohio and New York public policy and that they illegally attempted retroactively to alter vested rights belonging to the stockholder at the time the amendments were adopted. The court held that the stockholder did not substantiate its contentions that the amendments violated either Ohio or New York law. The court pointed out that the very case the stockholder cited for the contention that restrictions imposed on the stock's alienability were invalid was authority to the contrary. The court noted that the questioned by-law interposed no permanent impediment to transfer, providing only for an option in the directors for 30 days as a reasonable opportunity to dispose of stock to persons deemed desirable as holders. The court concluded that the corporation's pre-emptive right could be exercised only in a case where the stockholder desired or was required to sell and, in that event, he was guaranteed the fair cash value of the stock as determined by a court. The court rejected the stockholder's contentions that the by-laws violated the Uniform Stock Transfer Act and that the penalty provided by an Ohio statute was exclusive.


OUTCOME: The court denied the stockholder's motion to dismiss the complaint with leave to answer within 10 days from the service of a copy of the order with notice of entry.

 

2829 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Gorman v. New York


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court, Special Term, New York County, Part I


109 N.Y.S.2d 539; 1951 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2708



October 5, 1951 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff police officers initiated a declaratory judgment against defendants, City of New York, New York, and Board of Trustees of the Police Pension Fund, contending that a local law relating to their pensions was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The police officers alleged that the law violated N.Y. Const. art. V, § 7. The City and the Trustees contended that the complaint was insufficient in law and filed a motion to dismiss. Under the constitutional provision, membership in any pension or retirement system of the state or of a civil division was to be a contractual relationship, the benefits of which were not to be diminished or impaired. The court held that, under the local law, a retiring member was required to give 30 days of notice of his impending retirement and was required to file his application with the Trustees. The court also found that the Trustees had the authority to deny a pension if the retiring member's term of office was forfeited prior to the retirement date. The court determined that it was not permitted to construe the words of the constitution in exactly the same manner as it would construe the words of a will. The court held that it was required to seek the meaning, which the words would have conveyed to an intelligent, careful voter. The court denied the motion to dismiss and held that the complaint was legally sufficient to state a cause of action.


OUTCOME: The motion to dismiss that was filed by the City and the Trustees was denied.

 

2830 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Householder v. Grand Island


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Erie County


36 Misc. 2d 862; 114 N.Y.S.2d 852; 1951 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1459



July 12, 1951 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff property owner initiated a complaint against defendant town and sought to have an ordinance declared unconstitutional and unenforceable on the ground that it was confiscatory as it prevented the property owner from building a structure on his property.


OVERVIEW: The property owner sought to have an ordinance declared unconstitutional and unenforceable because it restricted the use of his property and prevented the building of any structure. The court held that the property owner was entitled to judgment as demanded because the evidence was insufficient to show that the ordinance was needed for the protection of public morals, public safety, or public health. The ordinance was an unwarranted intrusion on the private ownership of property and deprived the property owner of the appropriate use and enjoyment of his land. The court concluded that the ordinance was confiscatory and unenforceable because it was an attempt to accomplish a "set-back ordinance" without compensation, which could only be accomplished by the exercise of the power of eminent domain.


OUTCOME: The court held that the property owner was entitled to judgment as demanded.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Municipal corporations -- zoning -- setback so deep that it left property owner with unusable lot is void -- moreover, location of dedicated roadway from which setback runs is uncertain, hence setback is unenforcible.

1. A zoning ordinance imposing a 90-foot setback, which would leave plaintiff property owner with a usable building lot only 7 1/2 feet deep at one end and only 1/2 foot deep at the other end, is confiscatory, unconstitutional and void as to his property.

2. Furthermore, the 90-foot setback imposed by the Town Board runs "from center of right of way", yet the Town Board itself cannot locate the center line of this dedicated roadway; hence the ordinance is too uncertain to be enforcible, particularly so since the ordinance imposes a $ 100 penalty for each day that a violation continues.

 

2831 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Lang


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


COUNTY COURT OF NEW YORK, NASSAU COUNTY


106 N.Y.S.2d 829; 1951 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2234



July 10, 1951 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a conviction by the Police Justice Court of the Village of Hempstead (New York) for a violation of the parking meter ordinance, Village of Hempstead, N.Y., Ordinance § 363 (April 1, 1950). He argued that the ordinance was unconstitutional because the provision making it a violation when the expired sign showed after a portion of the maximum allowed parking period was an arbitrary exercise of police power.


OVERVIEW: Defendant contended that the signs posted by the meters did not comply with statutory requirements. The court disagreed and affirmed the conviction. Parking ordinances were generally upheld as constitutional. Statutes were presumed to be constitutional. The constitution only demanded that the statute should not be unreasonable, arbitrary, or capricious, and that the means selected should have a substantial relationship to the object sought to be attained. The court found that the ordinance at issue was reasonable and that its enforcement method did not conflict with guaranteed constitutional rights. The ordinance limited parking to one hour to keep traffic moving, to minimize traffic congestion, and to eliminate traffic dangers. To allow parking for a shorter period than the one-hour maximum encouraged motorists to limits their parking. Parking on city streets was a privilege and the ordinance fixed reasonable limitations, along with reasonable enforcement methods. The ordinance did not usurp any constitutional rights.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment.

 

2832 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Application of Bohlinger


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


199 Misc. 941; 106 N.Y.S.2d 953; 1951 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2267



June 25, 1951 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner New York State Superintendent of Insurance filed a complaint with the court that requested the dissolution of respondent fraternal benefit society's corporate existence pursuant to N.Y. Ins. Law § 511(e) on the basis that the corporation's further transaction of business would have been hazardous to its policyholders, to its creditors, and to the public because the society was dominated by Communists and had revolutionary goals.


OVERVIEW: The court found that the right of the Communist party to function as a political party was not an issue before the court. The court held that the issue was whether the society, which was a political group, could properly organize itself in the guise of a fraternal benefit society and carry on its political activities using the facade of its insurance functions. The court found that the corporate existence of the society could not properly continue because when the insurance function of the corporation was subjected to a competing political objective, a hazard was created to the corporation's insurance functions. The court held that immediate insolvency of the corporation was not required for liquidation under N.Y. Ins. Law § 511(e). The court found that other financial hazards qualified the corporation for liquidation under § 511(e). The court found that the Communist party's goals and activities were a sufficient financial hazard to the corporation because the political affiliation raised a serious threat of decline in membership and a serious threat to its financial structure. The court held that the political climate was an important factor in its determination.


OUTCOME: The court held that the Superintendent of Insurance was entitled to an order of dissolution and liquidation because the continuity of the corporation was hazardous to its policyholders.


HEADNOTES

Insurance -- fraternal benefit society -- dissolution of corporate existence -- (1) respondent fraternal benefit society found on basis of evidence to have violated its charter in that it is engaged in political activity acting as front for Communist party; accordingly since further operations "hazardous" to its policyholders and public, corporate existence ordered dissolved (Insurance Law, § 511, par. [e]) -- (2) action of United States Attorney General in listing respondent as subversive organization not considered; such action not due process -- (3) writings of respondent's officers in its official publications have high probative value -- (4) respondent's officers, nine out of ten of whom were members of Communist party, were acting in said party not solely in personal capacity but as officers of respondent -- (5) insurance and social reform activities of respondent served as lure to bring workers into respondent and subject them to communistic teachings -- (6) language of Insurance Law (§ 511, par. [e]) not confined to imminent financial insolvency  [***2] but to any hazard respecting management, practices and personnel -- (7) statements in respondent's charter indicating that its purposes were those of fraternal benefit society constituted fraud upon State since its publications show purposes were political; thus filing of charter violated Insurance Law (§ 463) -- (8) Superintendent of Insurance need not await conviction of respondent for violation of Federal Smith Act before proceeding for order of dissolution -- (9) violation of Federal law subjects respondent to dissolution -- (10)requirements of due process fully met in these proceedings -- (11) Statute of Limitations no bar to proceedings -- (12) superintendent not required first to effect rehabilitation of respondent or to request removal of untrustworthy officers -- (13) superintendent not estopped from bringing present proceeding, by alleged prior forbearance to institute proceeding -- (14) respondent's constitutional guarantees of freedom of speech, press, and assembly not abridged -- (15) on dissolution, interest of policyholders protected by court's supervision -- (16) stay of dissolution granted on condition respondent diligently proceed with appeal; meantime respondent  [***3] directed to deliver its books, etc. to superintendent.

1. Respondent corporation was organized in 1930 as a fraternal benefit society under the Insurance Law. Although solvent and carrying on fraternal, social and other altruistic purposes for the benefit of its members, it has so far departed from its charter as to become a political organization acting as a front for the Communist party of the United States and its charter should be revoked, its business liquidated, and its corporate existence dissolved pursuant to paragraph (e) of section 511 of the Insurance Law since it is in such condition that "its further transaction of business will be hazardous to its policyholders, or to its creditors, or to the public". (Pp. 966, 967, 968, 970.)

2. In connection with the hearing which was had before the court, the Superintendent of Insurance offered in evidence the action of the Attorney General of the United States in listing respondent as a subversive organization. The court will not consider such listing as having any bearing upon the determination of the issues involved in this litigation since the action of the Attorney General did not constitute due process. (P. 946.)

3.  [***4] Writings of most of respondent's officers printed in the official publications of respondent have a high probative value since their credibility is not open to challenge on the ground of personal or ideological bias or financial interest. (P. 948.)

4. The corporate officers, nine of the ten presently in office having been identified as members of the Communist party, were acting in the Communist party not solely in a personal capacity but were acting as officers of respondent. The evidence showed that these activities of the officers represented no individual frolic of the officers, no sporadic excursion into social activities, no intermittent campaigns for social betterment, but did constitute an essential part -- a persistent and a consistent part -- of the corporate activity during the period in question. The corporation cannot disavow such conduct nor responsibility for it on the ground that it was not authorized by formal resolution of the governing body. (Pp. 960, 963, 965.)

5. The overwhelming weight of evidence indicates that the insurance and social reform activities of respondent served to lure with calculated design into the society a great mass of workers to expose [***5] them to communist teachings and doctrines and bring them within the sphere of the Communist party. (Pp. 964-965.)

6. The language of section 511 directing the Superintendent of Insurance to apply for an order dissolving a fraternal benefit society on the ground that its further transaction of business will be "hazardous" to its policyholders, creditors, or to the public was not intended to refer solely to imminent or actual insolvency arising out of the insurance operations. The word "hazardous" in the statute, broadly construed, extends not only to the solvency of the society but to the details of the management, internal organization, practices and personnel. Since it appears that respondent is dominated and controlled by the Communist party, that its responsible officers are active members of the Communist party, and that its assets are liquid, if the time arrives when there is a conflict between the interests of this country and the world of communism, it is not beyond the realm of reasonable probability that the funds of the society will be expropriated. This then is a substantial financial hazard. Moreover should respondent be prosecuted under the Smith Act (U.S. Code,  [***6] tit. 18, § 2385) for its active participation in the program of the Communist party with full knowledge of its nature, a serious threat to respondent would arise by reason of a decline in membership and its effect upon respondent's financial structure. (Pp. 968, 970, 972.)

7. Respondent was organized as a fraternal benefit society but the evidence shows that it exceeded or abused its powers. In setting forth in its charter the usual purposes of a fraternal benefit society, respondent was perpetrating a fraud upon the State since the official publications announcing its formation indicated that it was a revolutionary group with a clearly prescribed political program. Accordingly, respondent's charter does not contain a true statement of its purposes and the original filing was in violation of section 463 of the Insurance Law. (P. 974.)

8. The Superintendent of Insurance need not await conviction of respondent for violation of the Federal Smith Act or of the Criminal Anarchy Act of New York (Penal Law, §§ 160-166) before proceeding under the Insurance Law. (P. 975.)

9. Violation of a Federal law subjects respondents to action by the Superintendent of Insurance, for under the [***7] Federal Constitution (art. VI, cl. 2) a Federal law is as much a law of the State as any law enacted by the State itself. (P. 976.)

10. In these proceedings, the court sits as a trier of the facts as presented by petitioner and respondent. It is not limited to the allegations in the report of the examiner of the Department of Insurance or to allegations adduced at the hearing before the superintendent or to the superintendent's conclusions or recommendations. Any valid objection to the content of the examiner's report on the ground of inclusion of material of questionable probative value was met by the amendment of the report after the hearing before the deputy superintendent. No evidence was considered which did not strictly conform to the rules of evidence. All of the requirements of due process have been met. (Pp. 976-977.)

11. The Statute of Limitations is no bar since there was a course of conduct continuing up to the very commencement of these proceedings and since fraud was involved. (P. 977.)

12. It is no objection to liquidation that the superintendent has not first requested removal of the untrustworthy officers of respondent or applied for an order of rehabilitation.  [***8] Section 513 of the Insurance Law provides that the superintendent may apply for liquidation "regardless of whether or not there has been a prior order directing him to rehabilitate such insurer". In this case rehabilitation would not eradicate respondent's affiliation with the Communist party nor could it be done by the removal of its officers who are Communist party members. The virus has spread too far and has polluted the entire corporate body. It cannot be reduced to a simple fraternal benefit society as required by its charter. (P. 977.)

13. The superintendent did not estop himself, by his prior failure to institute the proceedings against respondent. No past course of forbearance can affect the superintendent's right and duty to proceed for a liquidation when such a proceeding is indicated. Moreover, many of the wrongs complained of are not past history but exist to date. (Pp. 977-978.)

14. No unconstitutional abridgement of freedom of speech, press or assembly is involved in these proceedings. Such constitutional guarantees were never intended as a license for illegality or an invitation for fraud. (P. 978.)

15. In the dissolution and liquidation of respondent every [***9] legitimate interest of the policyholders will be safeguarded and maximum protection will be afforded to all interested parties by supervision and direction by the court. (P. 978.)

16. A stay of dissolution and liquidation will be granted on condition that respondent proceed with due diligence to prosecute an appeal from the order to be entered herein. Meantime the officers and other officials of respondent are directed to deliver forthwith to the Superintendent of Insurance all its books, papers and documents. (P. 978.)

 

2833 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


In re New Rochelle's Tax Liens


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


COUNTY COURT OF NEW YORK, WESTCHESTER COUNTY


110 N.Y.S.2d 392; 1951 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2790



May 31, 1951 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff city brought an action under N.Y. Tax Law § 165 et seq. for the foreclosure of tax liens by action in rem against defendant mortgagee. The city made a motion for summary judgment. The mortgagee opposed the motion by asserting that N.Y. Tax Law tit. 3 was unconstitutional and violated due process because it failed to provide for adequate notice of foreclosure to mortgagees and that there was no proof of notice.


OVERVIEW: The city had commenced a prior action against the mortgagee for foreclosure of the same parcels. The mortgagee asserted that this prior action precluded the current action. The court granted the city's motion and held as follows: (1) the provisions of the tax law were not unconstitutional because an in rem foreclosure of a tax lien was an action to enforce the payment of taxes, in which the same stringent requirements for notice were not necessary as in ordinary judicial proceedings; (2) notice of foreclosure in the action was properly published, posted and mailed, as required by N.Y. Tax Law § 165-b; (3) the mortgagee was not entitled to have notice mailed specifically to her because she did not give to the receiver of taxes the prior notice provided for § 165-c; and (4) the prior action did not bar the current action because it constituted a separate and distinct cause of action on the parcels.


OUTCOME: The court granted the city's motion for summary judgment against the mortgagee.

 

2834 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Franklin Nat'l Bank


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Nassau County


200 Misc. 557; 105 N.Y.S.2d 81; 1951 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1871



May 29, 1951 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The States brought an action against defendant national bank to enjoin defendant from the use of the word "saving" or "savings" in its publicity and from holding itself out as a savings bank and to determine the constitutionality of N.Y. Banking Law § 258(1).


OVERVIEW: The State brought an action against defendant national bank in which plaintiff contended that N.Y. Banking Law § 258(1) prohibited defendant from using "saving" or "savings" or their equivalent in its business or in soliciting or receiving deposits as a savings bank. Defendant had been using "saving" and "savings" in its business and it had solicited accounts by forms of publicity in which it used the words "saving" and "savings." The State demanded a permanent injunction against defendant restraining it from using those words in its advertising and its business. The court tested the constitutionality of the statute against the statute that created defendant, the Federal Reserve Act, 12 U.S.C.S. § 371. The court found the state statute was restrictive and it defeated the purposes for which defendant was created. Therefore, the court held N.Y. Banking Law § 258(1) to be unconstitutional. The court dismissed The State's complaint and entered judgment in favor of defendant.


OUTCOME: The court held that a portion of the New York Banking Law was unconstitutional because the restrictive nature of the section defeated the purposes for which Congress created defendant under the Federal Reserve Act.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- supremacy of Federal law over conflicting State statute -- right of national bank to do "savings" bank business -- (1) Banking Law ( § 258, subd. 1) prohibits national bank from using word "savings" in banking business and in advertising; complaint by People to enjoin national bank from using word "savings" in publicity and to enjoin said national bank from holding itself out as savings bank, dismissed -- (2) Federal Reserve Act (U. S. Code, tit. 12, § 371) empowers national banks to solicit "savings deposits"; since State Banking Law ( § 258, subd. 1) is in conflict with Federal law, State statute unconstitutional -- (3) to prove public state of mind as to significance of words "thrift account" and similar words, as compared with words "savings account", evidence of poll taken by competent poll taker properly admitted as original evidence and not hearsay -- (4) ban in State statute against use of word "savings" in advertising, etc., restricted grant by Congress -- (5) Banking Law ( § 258, subd. 1) not  [***2] exercise of police power -- (6) under Federal Reserve Act, power to receive "savings deposits" implies power to advertise that fact -- (7) contention made that amendment of Federal Reserve Act to give national banks power to receive "savings deposits" at time when "time deposits" could be received was futile; Congress intended to remove doubt as to right of national bank to receive savings deposits.

1. Subdivision 1 of section 258 of the Banking Law provides that no national bank shall use the word "saving" or "savings" in its banking business or in any advertisement, and that no corporation other than a savings bank should receive deposits as a savings bank. The section further provides that any national bank violating the section should forfeit to the People of the State for every offense $ 100 for every day that the offense continues. In an action by the People to enjoin a national bank from using the word "saving" or "savings" in its publicity and from holding itself out as a savings bank, the complaint should be dismissed.

2. Section 371 of title 12 of the United States Code (Federal Reserve Act) provides that a national bank has power to solicit and receive "savings deposits".  [***3] Since subdivision 1 of section 258 of the Banking Law is not in harmony with section 371 of title 12 of the United States Code, the State law must yield to the Federal law and the State law is declared unconstitutional (U.S. Const., art. VI).

3. To prove that the public did not understand that the words "thrift account", "compound interest", and "special interest" had the same meaning as "savings account" and that "savings account" was understood for what it really was, defendant offered testimony as to a sample poll. Such testimony of the poll was properly received. It is original evidence and not hearsay. A party endeavoring to establish the public state of mind on a subject, which state of mind cannot be proved except by calling as witnesses so many of the public as to render the task impracticable, may offer evidence of a poll. The evidence should include calling the planners, supervisors, and workers (or some of them) as witnesses so that the court may see and hear them; and should also include the work sheets, reports, surveys and all documents used in or prepared during the poll taking and those showing its results. Such evidence of the poll in this instance draws conclusions [***4] worthy of consideration.

4. The evidence shows that defendant national bank could not function without "savings deposits". Crippling objections placed by a State statute against defendant bank amounts to impairment of defendant's banking business. Thus the prohibition of the statute against signs, printing, advertising or publicity containing the word "saving" or "savings", restricts defendant in obtaining savings deposits and curtails the power granted it by Congress.

5. Subdivision 1 of section 258 of the Banking Law is not an exercise of police power in view of the fact that deposits (up to $ 10,000) in national banks are insured Federally the same as savings banks' deposits are insured (up to $ 10,000).

6. Under the Federal Reserve Act the authority to receive "savings deposits" implies the power to advertise its right to receive savings deposits.

7. The contention that when Congress amended the Federal Reserve Act to empower a national bank to receive "savings deposits" such amendment was futile because national banks were receiving what actually were savings deposits by virtue of their authority to receive "time deposits" is overruled. Congress intended by the amendment [***5] to remove any doubt as to the right of national banks to receive savings deposits.

 

2835 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Beverly Hills Cemetery Corp. v. Rush


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Westchester County


201 Misc. 534; 114 N.Y.S.2d 793; 1951 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2135



May 18, 1951 



DISPOSITION: Judgment granted in favor of defendants dismissing the complaint, with costs. Settle decision and judgment on notice.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff cemetery corporation filed an action in equity against defendants, town board of assessors and county treasurer, to remove a cloud on its title to real property occasioned by the issuance of assessments, pursuant to N.Y. Tax Law § 4(6-a), as amended by 1939 N.Y. Laws 291.


OVERVIEW: The corporation filed an equity action to remove a cloud on its title to cemetery property located in the town that was occasioned by assessments made under an unconstitutional statute. The court granted judgment for the town board and county treasurer dismissing the complaint because the corporation failed to prove its right to a property exemption. The court held that § 4(6-a), which was conditioned on a designated census, had a valid and reasonable basis of protecting taxpayers in a sparsely populated county where there was no need to reserve excessively large acreage for cemetery purposes. The court applied the maxim of unclean hands to deny equitable relief because the corporation's financial manipulations demonstrated a lack of good faith and its activities were tainted with commercialism. The court found that the cemetery was a guise to cover an undertaking for profit, given evidence of the corporation's issuance of certificates of indebtedness for a palpably inadequate consideration, intimate relationship with profit-making corporations, and failure to follow the requirements of the New York Membership Corporations Law.


OUTCOME: The court granted judgment for the town board and county treasurer dismissing the corporation's action that challenged the constitutionality of the assessment statute.


HEADNOTES

Cemeteries -- exemption from taxation -- (1) action in equity by cemetery corporation against board of assessors to remove assessments as cloud on title proper, since assessments claimed to be unconstitutional; remedy of certiorari not exclusive remedy -- (2) in prior certiorari proceeding, assessment on property held to be illegal; findings in former proceeding not res judicata on issue of constitutionality where statute changed since prior proceeding -- (3) Legislature validly anchored tax exemption for cemetery property to population of a county as determined by 1930Federal census -- (4) evidence establishes that plaintiff was profit-making corporation; plaintiff not entitled to exemption from taxation; complaint dismissed.

1. An action in equity by the owner of cemetery property located in Putnam County against the board of assessors of a town to remove, as a cloud on title, allegedly illegal assessments on the property, is a proper remedy. Apart from the claim that plaintiff is a [***2] legitimate nonprofit-making cemetery corporation entitled to tax exemption, plaintiff maintains that the assessments were made pursuant to an unconstitutional statute and that consequently the assessments are illegal. Under such circumstances the remedy of certiorari under the Tax Law ( § 290 et seq.) is not the sole and exclusive remedy.

2. An earlier assessment against the cemetery property had been held to be improper in a certiorari proceeding. The findings in said proceeding are not res judicata here on the issue of constitutionality inasmuch as a substantial change in the statute occurred in 1939 between the date of the former proceeding and this one.

3. Subdivision 6-a of section 4 of the Tax Law, as amended in 1939, protects the taxpayers of a sparsely populated county, such as Putnam County, from a reservation of excessively large acreage for cemetery purposes by taking away, from the owner of cemetery property in such a county, exemption from taxation. Such statute is constitutional. It was reasonable for the Legislature to grant exemption to property in a county having a specified population according to the 1930 Federal census, irrespective of later population [***3] changes in the county.

4. The evidence establishes that plaintiff was organized for the purpose of realizing pecuniary profit for the corporation, its directors, officers and transferees. The issuance and reissuance of certificates of indebtedness for inadequate consideration, the relation existing between plaintiff and other profit-making corporations, with interlocking directors and officers, the failure of plaintiff to follow the requirements of the Membership Corporations Law governing conduct of such corporations establish that the cloak assumed by plaintiff was a pretense to cover a commercial undertaking. Plaintiff has failed to sustain the burden of establishing its right to a property exemption by clear and convincing proof that it is not profit-making in character. Plaintiff comes into court with unclean hands and is entitled to no affirmative equitable relief. The complaint should be dismissed.

 

2836 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


In re Estate of Harbord


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Surrogate's Court of New York, Westchester County


201 Misc. 358; 105 N.Y.S.2d 123; 1951 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1877



April 30, 1951 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant executors sought review of an order of the New York State Tax Commission that fixed the estate tax to be paid for a decedent's estate.


OVERVIEW: When fixing the state taxes to be paid by the executors on a decedent's estate the Commission included in the gross taxable estate the assets of an inter vivos trust established by the decedent. The executors appealed, and the court affirmed. It was an established legislative policy of New York to conform its estate tax law, so far as possible, to the provisions of the federal estate tax law. In determining, therefore, the effect of provisions of the New York estate tax law similar to those of the federal estate tax law, it was the policy of the court to give great weight to the federal decisions construing comparable provisions of the federal statutes. A federal case construing a similar federal law pertaining to the taxability of trusts held that a trust fund was required to be included in a decedent's gross taxable estate, and the right to receive the income from the trust property reserved by the grantor was a property right which passed to the remaindermen upon the grantor's death.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order fixing the estate tax.


HEADNOTES

Taxation -- estate tax -- inclusion of irrevocable trust made in 1930 in gross taxable estate -- (1) decedent created irrevocable trust in 1930, income of which payable to wife and, upon her death, income payable to decedent; upon death of survivor, principal was to be distributed to others, not including decedent or his estate; decedent died in 1947; New York estate tax appraiser in his report included trust assets in gross taxable estate; appeal of executors from pro forma order fixing estate tax denied -- (2) Tax Law (art. 10-C) governs trust created prior to enactment of article 10-C where settlor died after statute applicable -- (3) Supreme Court in 1949 in Commissioner of Internal Revenue v. Estate of Church (335 U.S. 632, 69 S. Ct. 322, 93 L. Ed. 288) decided that such trust should be included in gross taxable estate; Congress, by Technical Changes Act of 1949, relieved estate from Federal tax because of hardship; amendment to Tax Law (§ 249-r) conformed New York law to Federal law, but was made applicable to estates of persons dying after April 30, 1950; application of Church decision would require holding that  [***2] tax on trust became due on date of death in 1947; amended section 249-r inapplicable, decedent having died prior to April 30, 1950.

1. Decedent in 1930 executed an irrevocable trust agreement which gave the income to his wife for life and, upon her death, made such income payable to decedent during his life and directed that upon the death of the survivor, the principal of the trust was to be distributed to various individuals, not including decedent or his estate. Decedent died on August 20, 1947. The New York estate tax appraiser in his report included the assets of said trust in the gross taxable estate. The appeal of the executors from the pro forma order fixing the estate tax should be denied and the order upheld.

2. Decedent having died in the year 1947, article 10-C of the Tax Law, by its express terms, is applicable to this trust, created in 1930.

3. In 1930, the United States Supreme Court in May v. Heiner (281 U.S. 238, 74 L. Ed. 826, 50 S. Ct. 286) held that a transfer by a decedent in trust reserving income to her, with remainder to her children, their distributees or appointees, was not a gift to take effect in possession or enjoyment at her death,  [***3] and such trust was therefore held not taxable under the Federal Revenue Act. Thereafter the Federal Revenue Act was amended to provide that a trust which reserved to the trustor for life the income from the property should be included in the gross estate. New York enacted a comparable amendment to section 249-r of article 10-C of the Tax Law (eff. March 10, 1931). Under such amended New York statute, the New York courts held that the legislation was prospective only and refused to include, in the gross taxable estate, assets of irrevocable inter vivos trusts with reserved life estates to the donors created prior to March 10, 19 31. In January, 1949, in Commissioner of Internal Revenue v. Estate of Church (335 U.S. 632, 69 S. Ct. 322, 93 L. Ed. 288), the United States Supreme Court reversed itself and held that such a trust wherein the donor reserved the income for life should be included in the gross taxable estate. The court also held that possession or enjoyment took place upon death. By the Technical Changes Act of 1949 (63 U.S. Stat. 894), in order to prevent hardship upon persons who had relied upon the May v. Heiner interpretation, Congress exempted [***4] the estates of persons creating such trusts who diedbefore January 1, 1950. Decedent herein having died in 1947, there was refunded to his estate, under the Technical Changes Act, the amount of the Federal tax on the inter vivos trust which had been included in the gross taxable estate under the Church decision. New York thereafter amended section 249-r of the Tax Law by chapter 683 of the Laws of 1950. Section 249-r, as amended, was intended to grant relief similar to that contained in the Federal Technical Changes Act, except that its application was limited to estates of decedents dying after April 30, 1950, in view of section 8 of article VII of the State Constitution which prevents the giving of money of the State to any private undertaking, and has been construed as forbidding any retroactive reduction of tax liability already accrued. The transfer here in question made in 1930, took effect in possession or enjoyment at the time of decedent's death in 1947, and the tax on this estate accrued at the time of decedent's death in 1947. The assets of the trust are therefore taxable by New York.

 

2837 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Childs v. Brooklyn Edison Co.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Kings County, Part I


105 N.Y.S.2d 503; 1951 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1933



April 27, 1951 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, two brokerage companies, moved to enjoin defendant utility corporation from making operative and effective service classifications to require them to make effective and applicable service classifications with regard to utility rate changes. The utility corporation cross-moved to dismiss the complaint upon the ground that the court did not have jurisdiction of the subject and that the same was insufficient in law.


OVERVIEW: The brokerage companies owned commercial buildings and resold to their tenants the electrical service supplied to them by the utility corporation. The revised rate schedule became effective under orders of the public service commission. The companies contended that the new rates were not as profitable as the prior rate, that they were prohibited from obtaining additional customers, and that this prohibition was violative of their constitutional rights. The court held that the companies did not set forth any contractual obligation on the part of the utility corporation to continue supplying them with electricity at the lower rates. The utility corporation was required by federal and state law to charge the rates prescribed by the effective rate schedules. Further, it was not a requirement of due process that there be a judicial inquiry before the discretion of an administrative body was exercised. The companies' grievance related to the reasonableness of the rates and that issue was one to be determined by the public service commission. Therefore, the application for injunctive relief was denied and the corporation's cross-motion to dismiss was granted.


OUTCOME: The court denied the application for injunctive relief and the corporation's cross-motion to dismiss was granted in an action involving utility rate changes.

 

2838 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Franmor Realty Corp. v. Le Boeuf


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Nassau County


201 Misc. 220; 104 N.Y.S.2d 247; 1951 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1728



April 18, 1951 



DISPOSITION: Application for a review granted and on such review the decision of the board of appeals is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner gasoline station owner filed for review of the decision of respondent Board of Zoning Appeals of the Village of Old Westbury, which denied the appeal of the gasoline station owner from the denial by the village clerk of a permit for the use of the property as a gasoline service station.


OVERVIEW: The gasoline station owner had used the property as a gasoline service station as a continuous use for many years until the president of the gasoline station owner was in military service for several years. After the end of the war, the gasoline station owner attempted to re-rent or to sell the property for use as a gasoline station, but the property had become dilapidated. The gasoline station owner applied for a building permit to erect a gasoline service station. The village clerk denied the application because the area was zoned for residential single family dwellings under Village of Old Westbury, N.Y., Zoning Ordinance § 301. The gasoline station owner appealed to the board, which denied the appeal after a hearing. On appeal, the court affirmed. The court found that normally for a nonconforming use to be resumed after a period of nonuse, the use must not have been abandoned. The court found that there was no affirmative act on the part of the gasoline station owner to constitute abandonment. The court concluded, however, that under § 900(4), there was no requirement for abandonment and the court found the section to be constitutional.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the board, which affirmed the denial by the village clerk of a permit to erect a gasoline service station on the property of the gasoline station owner.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- zoning -- nonconforming use -- (1) village zoning ordinance provided that nonconforming use which was discontinued for twelve months should not be resumed; gasoline service station operated in residential district as nonconforming use discontinued by petitioner by reason of war restrictions on gasoline; after war, petitioner attempted again to use premises as gasoline station; denial by board of zoning appeals of petitioner's application to use premises as nonconforming use, affirmed -- (2) ordinance abolishing nonconforming use after "nonuser" for twelve months constitutional; period of twelve months reasonable; here nonuser was for total period of eight years.

1. A zoning ordinance of a village provided that a nonconforming use which had been discontinued for a period exceeding twelve months should not be resumed. Prior to 1942 petitioner had operated a gasoline service station as a nonconforming use in a residential area. In 1942, by reason of the restrictions imposed on the [***2] use of gasoline by the war, petitioner and its tenants were unable to operate the station. After the war petitioner attempted again to use the land for a gasoline station. A hearing disclosed that the station has not been operated as such since 1942. On this application by petitioner for an order directing the board of zoning appeals to issue a license to it to use the premises as a gasoline station as a nonconforming use, the denial of such use by the board of zoning appeals should be affirmed.

2. The provisions of the zoning ordinance which abolish a nonconforming use after "nonuser" of twelve months is valid and constitutional, and the period of twelve months of nonuser is a reasonable one. Here the actual period of nonuse has been five years since the end of the war and eight years altogether.

 

2839 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Suburban Tire & Battery Co. v. Mamaroneck


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Westchester County


104 N.Y.S.2d 850; 1951 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1824



April 16, 1951 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner landowner instituted a proceeding under N.Y. Civ. Prac. Act art. 78 for an order of mandamus to compel respondent Village of Mamaroneck to issue an occupancy permit authorizing the use of the landowner's premises as a gasoline service station.


OVERVIEW: The landowner acquired the premises long after the Village adopted a zoning ordinance that prohibited gasoline service stations in the Village's business district or within 200 feet of the exit or entrance to a playground. The landowner applied to the Village's board of zoning appeals for a variance. After the variance was denied, the landowner sought a mandamus order on the ground that these provisions of the zoning ordinance were unconstitutional. The court determined that the Village's board of trustees had the power and authority to divide the Village into districts for zoning purposes and that the exclusion of gasoline service stations from business districts was a matter within the Village's police power because they presented an increased fire hazard. Noting a presumption that the ordinance was constitutional and that courts could not pronounce an ordinance invalid unless able to see that there was no real, substantial evil of public interest to be guarded against, the court declined to substitute its judgment for that of the Village's board of trustees and ruled that the challenged provisions of ordinance were not unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court denied the landowner's application for an order of mandamus and dismissed the proceeding.

 

2840 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Mazzotti v. Swezey


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Suffolk County


199 Misc. 987; 103 N.Y.S.2d 956; 1951 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1697



April 2, 1951 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, a candidate for election, filed an application under N.Y. Civil Prac. Act art. 78 for an order cancelling an action of a village board of trustees that declared the candidate ineligible for election and restraining the board from taking any further action in relation to the election. The candidate sought an order that restrained respondent, a trustee-elect, from taking part in the matters and affairs of the board as a member.
