OVERVIEW: The village trustees filed a motion to dismiss the petition and a motion to strike two paragraphs of the petition. The candidate alleged that at a village election for village trustees, he was one of the three highest candidates out of six who contended for the three offices. He alleged that following the election the board met as a board of canvassers and that, in addition, the board declared that the candidate was ineligible for election because he was not the owner of record of real property within the village. He alleged that he was an owner of record of real property within the village and that the trustee-elect was declared elected in his place. The court held that the trustees had no power to nullify the election of the candidate nor declare the election of the trustee-elect because the election returns did not show that the trustee-elect was elected and because the vacancies arose as a result of the expiration of the terms of the predecessors as village trustees. The court also held that the trustees' declaration that the candidate was ineligible could not be sustained because the court could not determine that the candidate was not eligible except by quo warranto.


OUTCOME: The court denied the village's motion to dismiss. The court granted the candidate's motion.


HEADNOTES

Elections -- trustees of village -- eligibility of candidate for office -- (1) after election for three vacancies in office of village trustee, board of trustees met, and although petitioner was one of three receiving highest votes, board declared petitioner ineligible as not being property owner and declared one of respondents elected; duties of board of trustees acting as canvassers were ministerial; board lacked power to declare petitioner ineligible and to declare respondent elected -- (2) petition alleges that petitioner was owner of property; such allegation sufficient to show that petitioner "owner of record" of real property within Village Law (§ 42); question of eligibility of petitioner under section 42 cannot be determined in this proceeding but only in quo warranto proceedings -- (3) action of trustees in declaring petitioner ineligible not justified under Public Officers Law (§ 5) -- (4) court has power to direct board of trustees to perform duties as board of canvassers.

1. A board of trustees of a village after an election wherein six candidates competed for three vacancies for the office of village [***2] trustee declared petitioner ineligible for the office of trustee although petitioner received more votes than one of respondents and declared one of respondents elected to said office. Under section 53 of the Village Law, the trustees of the village became a board of canvassers and had the duty of listing all votes cast for each office, the name of each candidate, the number of votes cast for each, and of determining which person by the greatest number of votes was elected to each office. In so acting, the board of canvassers acts in a ministerial capacity only. The board of canvassers had no power to nullify the election of petitioner and to declare respondent elected. The Legislature by fixing time limitations in the Village Law (§§ 53-59) intended that the question of eligibility could not be determined at the meeting of the board of trustees held "not later than eight o'clock in the evening of the next day after the election", but only after the village clerk "within three days after the election" has notified such person of his election and his requirement to file his oath of office and furnish proof of his eligibility.

2. The board of trustees was unauthorized to declare [***3] petitioner ineligible under section 42 of the Village Law on the ground that he was not a property owner, in the face of the paragraph in the petition which alleged that petitioner prior to and after the date of the election was the owner of property within the village. While the petition does not allege that petitioner was at the time of his election "an owner of record" of title to real property in the village, such paragraph in the petition may be construed to mean just that. The failure of the Village Law to state how record ownership of real property may be evidenced, which the Town Law (§ 23-a) does, indicates a legislative intention that evidence of record ownership under the Village Law should not be of the same character as that required under the Town Law. Accordingly in this proceeding, the court cannot determine that petitioner was not eligible to the office of village trustee. In the absence of any definition in the Village Law of what constitutes "record ownership" the assessment rolls may be deemed to be public records. The question of eligibility under the Village Law (§ 42) cannot be determined in this proceeding, but only in quo warranto proceedings.

3. The [***4] action of the trustees in declaring petitioner ineligible may not be justified under section 5 of the Public Officers Law which provides for holding over after expiration of term by an incumbent until his successor is designated or chosen. Since there were three incumbents whose terms expired and six candidates contested for the three offices, it cannot be said that respondent was the incumbent of the office for which petitioner received a majority vote. There being three vacancies, the court cannot determine who the incumbent is.

4. Since the trustees failed to perform the duty enjoined upon them by law as canvassers, this court must direct them to perform. The court has jurisdiction both under article 14 of the Election Law and article 78 of the Civil Practice Act to compel election officers to perform their duties.

 

2841 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Pitts v. McGoldrick


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Nassau County


200 Misc. 150; 103 N.Y.S.2d 875; 1951 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1687



March 27, 1951 



DISPOSITION: The plaintiff's motion is in all respects denied, except as hereinbefore set forth, and defendant's motion for judgment on the pleadings is granted. Settle order on notice.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In an action of plaintiff, an apartment owner, for a declaratory judgment as to N.Y. State Residential Rent Law § 7, the apartment owner filed a motion to strike out first and second affirmative defenses, as insufficient in law, in response to an answer filed by defendant, the State Rent Administrator, as Temporary State Housing Rent Commission, and in response to a cross-motion filed by defendant for an order granting judgment on pleadings.


OVERVIEW: The apartment owner argued that a state rent law exempting from control all housing accommodations built after February 1, 1947 was unconstitutional and void in so far as it may have applied to a vacant apartment in his house constructed prior to February 1, 1947. The court noted that the Federal Rent Control Statutes enacted in 1947 exempted accommodations completed after February 1, 1947 from controls of statutes. It was determined that the state government thereafter assumed duties of housing rent control, in a 1950 state statute, fixing maximum rental on houses and apartments unoccupied or occupied, which retained exemption based on February 1, 1947. The court found this was reasonable protection to landlords who built houses in reliance on the Federal Act and was not arbitrary and discriminatory. The Administrator's defense reciting necessity in public health and emergency for law and the appropriateness of law to meet the situation was necessary to show the reason for the Act and was a sufficient defense as against the motion to strike.


OUTCOME: The court denied the motion to strike of the apartment owner and granted the motion for judgment on pleadings of the State Rent Administrator.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- landlord and tenant -- rent regulation -- (1) vacant apartments, as well as occupied ones, are validly subject to rent control (L. 1950, ch. 250) -- (2) housing accommodations erected after February 1, 1947, are validly exempted from State rent control (by L. 1950, ch. 250) -- (3) since landlord seeks judgment declaring statute unconstitutional, she need not go through procedure provided in statute for protest to State Rent Administrator against regulation; complaint dismissed.

1. Vacant apartments, as well as occupied ones, are validly and constitutionally subject to rent control during the declared public emergency in housing (L. 1950, ch. 250).

2. Housing accommodations erected after February 1, 1947, are validly and constitutionally exempted from State rent control (by L. 1950, ch. 250). When, by that act, the State took over Federal rent control in 1950, housing [***2] erected after February 1, 1947, was exempt under the Federal Housing and Rent Act of 1947; and the Legislature doubtlessly had in mind the protection of those landlords who in good faith had built houses in reliance on the Federal exemption.

3. Judgment on the pleadings is granted to defendant, the State Rent Administrator, dismissing the complaint in this action brought by a landlord for a judgment declaring the State Residential Rent Law unconstitutional in the above respects. The defense that the statute is a reasonable exercise of the State's police power is sufficient. However, since plaintiff attacked the statute itself, it was not necessary for plaintiff first to go through the procedure which the statute provides for a protest to the Administrator against a regulation.

 

2842 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Skottedal


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


COUNTY COURT OF NEW YORK, SUFFOLK COUNTY


104 N.Y.S.2d 583; 1951 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1780



March 27, 1951 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the ruling of the Court of Special Sessions of the Village of Amityville, New York, which convicted her of distributing circulars to the public, in violation of Amityville, N.Y. Village Ordinance No. 26. The circulars handed out by defendant sought to sway public opinion to favor defendant's political views.


OVERVIEW: Defendant contended that her conviction required reversal, arguing that the village ordinance under which she was convicted was unconstitutional on its face in that it violated the provisions of the Federal and State Constitutions relating to the right of free speech. Defendant further argued that, if the ordinance was constitutional, its provisions did not apply to the circular she distributed. The court overturned defendant's conviction, holding that: (1) the intent of the framers of the ordinance was to prevent the littering of the village streets with circulars, cards, and handbills; (2) the true intent of the legislature in enacting the ordinance was to prohibit the distributing upon the streets of only handbills, circulars, or cards containing commercial advertising matter and the distributing upon the streets of any commercial advertising matter through any means whatsoever; (3) the ordinance in and of itself was valid; (4) in publishing and distributing the circular, defendant attempted to bring attention to her views on local politics; and (4) the ordinance did not apply to the circulars distributed by defendant, as they could not be considered advertising material.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment of conviction and dismissed the information against defendant.

 

2843 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Holmes Electric Protective Co. v. New York


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


200 Misc. 708; 103 N.Y.S.2d 307; 1951 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1615



March 1, 1951 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff company brought an action for declaratory judgment to determine whether the company was obligated to pay the utility tax imposed by defendants, a city and its comptroller. Under previous laws, the company brought a successful action in which it was held that the company was not engaged in the sale of electric, telephone, or telegraphic service with the meaning of the then-existing tax laws.


OVERVIEW: About 90 percent of the company's gross income was attributable to certain protective and alarm services it provided through the use of electric wires and impulses. The company brought an action to determine whether such income was thus taxable under N.Y. Admin. Code § Q41-1.0. The company sought to recover a sum of taxes paid under protest, with interest, and to obtain declaratory and injunctive relief such that the company would not be required to make any further payments on the utility excise tax and to restrain defendants and their successors from attempting to enforce such tax against the company. The court held that in order to determine whether the services furnished by the company came within the meaning of the words "telegraph service," as defined under § Q41-1.0, the evidence regarding the devices and facilities used by the company tended to demonstrate the extent to which the services sold and furnished by the company required the use of electric or telegraph wires in the transmission of messages, signals, and alarms. The court thus concluded that the company was literally within the class of business activities subject to the utility excise tax.


OUTCOME: The court denied the company's request for declaratory and injunctive relief, dismissed the complaint, and entered judgment for defendants. The court, however, granted the company's motion to strike out certain testimony offered by defendants and denied all other motions made at the trial upon which decision was reserved.


HEADNOTES

Taxation -- municipal sales and utility excise taxes -- (1) local law (Administrative Code of City of New York, § Q41-2.0) imposes utility excise tax on "telegraph service"; "telegraph service" defined by section Q41-1.0 as requiring use of electric or telegraph wires for transmission of signal or alarm; plaintiff engaged in business of protecting customers' premises against burglary by means of wires in premises connected to plaintiff's office through wires some of which maintained under city's streets; service sold by plaintiff requires use of electric or telegraph wires for transmission of signals and alarms; plaintiff's business subject to utility excise tax -- (2) 1938 amendment of Local Law intended to impose taxes upon plaintiff -- (3) plaintiff, by its use of city streets for running its wires, is utility or quasi-utility subject to utility excise tax by local law -- (4) utility excise tax not invalid as containing arbitrary classification -- (5) General City Law (§ 20-b) did not freeze types of enterprises to be taxed; immaterial that prior to 1938, plaintiff under existing law, not subject to taxation; city could properly include  [***2] plaintiff as utility subject to taxation by 1938 amendment of local laws.

1. A local law was am ended in 1938 to impose a utility excise tax (Administrative Code of City of N. Y., § Q41-2.0) upon persons furnishing or selling "telegraph service". The local law (Administrative Code of City of New York, § Q41-1.0) defines "telegraph service" as "any service requiring the use of electric or telegraph wires * * * in the transmission of messages, signals, alarms, * * *." Plaintiff's business is the protection of its customers' premises against burglary or unauthorized entry. It provides electric protective service in which plaintiff wires the premises to be protected so that on disturbance of the circuit an electric alarm signal is sent out to plaintiff's offices from which a guard is dispatched to ascertain the cause of the disturbance. A portion of the connecting wires either leased or owned by plaintiff runs under the streets of the city of New York. Plaintiff's income is measured by the number of units wired for protection. The services sold by plaintiff necessarily require the use of electric or telegraph wires for the transmission of messages, signals and alarms, and therefore [***3] plaintiff is literally within the class of business activities subject to the utility excise tax.

2. Using propriety and justice as guides to the true legislative intent, since plaintiff has long enjoyed special rights and privileges in the use of public streets of defendant city for its electric wires, the 1938 local law was enacted by the legislature of the city to require corporations such as plaintiff to pay such utility excise tax.

3. The definition of "telegraph service" adopted in 1938 was intended to bring within the scope of the utility excise tax, businesses such as that conducted by plaintiff. In view of the purpose and pattern of the utility excise tax to include those enterprises, whether utility or otherwise, which are in the nature of utilities because they profit from the use of public property, franchises, facilities or other special privilege, plaintiff, being in the nature of a utility, properly comes within that class of taxpayers defined by the utility excise tax as utilities and quasi-utilities.

4. The tax is not invalid as based on an arbitrary classification. Plaintiff's enterprise by enjoying special benefits and use of property belonging to the public [***4] can hardly complain that a tax premised upon this characteristic is an arbitrary and invalid classification.

5. Section 20-b of the General City Law (added by L. 1937, ch. 321) empowered municipalities to adopt local laws imposing a tax such as was imposed by section 186-a of the Tax Law. Section 186-a of the Tax Law authorized a State utility tax in language identical with that contained in the New York City utility excise tax law prior to its amendment in 1938. Plaintiff contends that since the courts have held that the local law prior to 1938 did not impose a utility tax upon it, section 20-b of the General City Law prevented the city of New York from adopting an ordinance which would extend its tax to an enterprise such as plaintiff's. A review of the historical background leading to the adoption of section 186-a of the Tax Law and section 20-b of the General City Law shows that it was not intended and it was nowhere stated that the State's enactment served to freeze as of 1937 the tax on enterprises which could be taxed by municipalities.

 

2844 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Bibber v. Weber


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Queens County


199 Misc. 906; 102 N.Y.S.2d 945; 1951 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1575



February 15, 1951 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant grantor filed a motion to dismiss pursuant to N.Y. R. Civ. Prac. 112 regarding a judgment on the pleadings and N.Y. R. Civ. Prac. 113 regarding summary judgment plaintiff grantee's complaint that the grantor breached the covenants contained in a deed conveyed to the grantee. The grantor asserted that the deed stated all covenants and restrictions of record, the grantee had knowledge of the existing incumbrance and the public records.


OVERVIEW: The grantor conveyed title to certain premises by deed to the grantee which allegedly contained certain restrictive covenants. Part of the building on these premises encroached upon a street laid out on a final city map but which did not yet exist. The grantee filed suit to recover damages for breach of covenant and the grantor filed a motion to dismiss. The grantor asserted that the deed was subject to all covenants and restrictions of record, that the grantee had knowledge of the existing incumbrance, and the grantee was charged with knowledge of the public records. The court granted summary judgment in favor of the grantee. It held that the easement of the public highway was neither a covenant or restriction of record, that neither party claimed to have actual knowledge of the easement, and that the grantee did not have constructive notice from the physical appearance because the street was not physically open, visible, and in use, and that the grantee was not charged with knowledge of prior grants or easements of record.


OUTCOME: The court granted summary judgement in favor of the grantee finding the grantor breached the covenants contained in a deed conveyed to the grantee.


HEADNOTES

Covenants -- breach -- (1) deed conveyed premises encroaching on part of "paper" street to plaintiff under warranty deed; in action for damages for breach of warranty, no permit obtained from board of appeals under General City Law ( § 35) so that building violates section; constitutionality of section 35 may not be attacked since neither party applied for permit mentioned in section 35 -- (2) no rights acquired under building permit issued by department of housing and buildings in violation of law -- (3) deed provided that it was subject to covenants and restrictions of record not violated by existing building; said clause no help to defendants since easement not covenant or restriction of record, and even if it were, it is violated by existing building -- (4) immaterial that plaintiff not evicted; easement impairing value of premises is breach of covenant even in absence of physical ouster -- (5) title not made marketable by reason of title company's insuring interest of mortgagee -- (6) plaintiff  [***2] not charged with knowledge of existence of street because city map was public record -- (7) plaintiff entitled to summary judgment; damages are difference between value of land without servitude and with it; question of damages referred to Official Referee or to court and jury.

1. Defendants sold a parcel of real property, including a building thereon which encroached upon the street as laid out on a final map, under a full covenant and warranty deed. In an action to recover damages for breach of such covenants it appears that no permit was obtained from the board of appeals under section 35 of the General City Law and consequently the building as it now stands violates section 35. Defendants' contention that section 35 is unconstitutional insofar as it deprives landowners of their rights in the bed of the street while they remained owners of the fee thereof should be rejected. Since none of the parties has ever applied to the board of appeals for the permit mentioned in section 35, defendants are not in a position to question the constitutionality of the statute.

2. No rights may be acquired under the building permit issued by the city's department of housing and buildings [***3] since such permit was issued in violation of law.

3. A clause in the deed which provides that it was subject to the covenants and restrictions affecting said premises not violated by the existing building does not help defendants. The easement of the public highway pursuant to section 35 of the General City Law is neither a covenant nor restriction of record and even if it were, it is violated by the existing building.

4. It is immaterial that plaintiff has not been evicted. An outstanding easement which materially impairs the value of the premises conveyed and interferes with the use and possession of some portions thereof is a breach of the covenant although there has not been a physical ouster from the actual possession of any portion thereof.

5. A contention that the title is not unmarketable for the reason that a title company has insured the interest of a mortgagee is rejected. Marketability is not determined by title companies but by the courts and an easement is an incumbrance which renders a title unmarketable.

6. Although an open and visible public highway would not be considered a breach of the covenant, here neither of the parties had actual knowledge of the easement [***4] or constructive notice from the physical appearance of the premises. The street is not physically open. Plaintiff was not charged with knowledge of the public records including the official map of the city or with knowledge of prior grants or easements of record.

7. Plaintiff is entitled to summary judgment, her damages being the difference between the value of the land without the servitude and with it. The question of damages will be referred to an Official Referee or to a court and jury.

 

2845 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Bergerman v. Murphy


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


199 Misc. 1008; 102 N.Y.S.2d 622; 1951 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1535



January 30, 1951 



DISPOSITION: The petition, therefore, is granted to the extent that the Board of Trustees of the Police Pension Fund is directed to rescind the resolutions retiring defendants Furey and Collins as deputy police commissioners. Settle order in accordance herewith.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner private citizens applied for an order under N.Y. Civ. Prac. Act art. 78 to (a) compel respondent board of trustees to rescind the retirements of respondent detectives as deputy police commissioners and (b) direct the detectives to restore to the police pension fund payments that had been made to them after such retirement.


OVERVIEW: The detectives were members of the police force for more than 20 years when they each resigned their positions and were appointed deputy police commissioners. They each immediately applied for and were granted retirement with pensions for those new positions, pursuant to New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code § B18-4.0. The citizens seeking to overturn the retirements claimed that the pensions for retirement as deputy police commissioners were improper gifts of public funds. In granting the petition in part, the court ruled that, by allowing immediate retirement upon receiving a promotion and increase in salary, § B18-4.0(f) violated N.Y. Const. art. VIII, § 1, which prohibited a municipality from giving public funds in aid of any individual. The statute provided an impermissible "gift," that is, a voluntary transfer of money without consideration, and not a "pension," because payments could not be for past service or meritorious work where the retirement was immediate. However, the proper remedy for restoration of the funds paid out was a taxpayer's action under N.Y. Gen. Mun. Law § 51.


OUTCOME: The petition was granted in part and denied in part, and the board was directed to rescind the retirements of the detectives as deputy police commissioners.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- validity of pension provisions covering New York city deputy police commissioners -- (1) after twenty years' service, New York city first grade detectives resigned and immediately were appointed deputy police commissioners; each applied for retirement as commissioner and began to receive pension payments within 44 and 67 days respectively at half of salary of chief inspector which was more than full salary as first grade detective; pensions granted them pursuant to Administrative Code (§ B18-4.0, subd. f); subdivision funconstitutional since public funds given to individual in violation of State Constitution (art. VIII, subd. 1) -- (2) "gift" defined; "pension" defined -- (3) proceeding under Civil Practice Act (art. 78) proper since relief sought is to cancel invalid resolutions granting pension at half of chief inspector's salary.

1. First [***2] grade detectives resigned after service on the police force of the city of New York for more than twenty years and were appointed, pursuant to subdivision f of section B18-4.0 of the Administrative Code, as deputy police commissioners. Immediately upon being appointed, each applied for retirement and, pursuant to resolution of the board of trustees of the police pension fund, was granted an annual pension of $ 6,000, in one case within 44 days after promotion and in the other 67 days after promotion. Their salary as first grade detectives was $ 5,175 per annum. Subdivision f of section B18-4.0 of the Administrative Code provides that any member of the police force who shall have performed duty on the force for twenty years and subsequently served as deputy police commissioner shall be retired and granted a pension allowed to a chief inspector on such date. Subdivision c provides that any member of the force who shall have elected to contribute on the basis of retirement after twenty years as in the instant cases and who shall have performed service on the force for twenty years "shall" upon application be retired and granted an annual pension "of not less than one half his full [***3] salary" at the date of his retirement from service. Considering what has actually been done under subdivision f and what may reasonably be done under its authority, subdivision f is unconstitutional in that it violates subdivision 1 of article VIII of the State Constitution which prohibits a municipality from "giving" public funds to or in aid of any individual. The statute cannot be regarded as a pension statute, for where a deputy police commissioner resigns almost immediately after appointment the periodic payments are not for past services or for meritorious work but are gifts of public funds without any consideration therefor.

2. A "gift" is a voluntary transfer of money or property by one to another without any consideration or compensation. A "pension" is a periodic allowance made by a government to an individual or to those who represent him on account of past services or some meritorious work done by him.

3. Since petitioners pray for an order directing the board of trustees of the police pension fund to rescind the resolutions retiring the detectives as deputy police commissioners at pensions of $ 6,000 annually, the proper remedy would appear to be a proceeding under [***4] article 78 to compel the board of trustees to rescind the alleged illegal resolution especially where the question raised is one of public importance and concern. Any private citizen has such an interest in the property and lawful administration of the police pension system as to enable him to maintain such proceeding and not be relegated to a taxpayer's action to prevent waste of public funds under section 51 of the General Municipal Law. To the extent therefore that the petition seeks a rescission of the resolution, it is granted.

 

2846 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Joseph Burstyn, Inc. v. McCaffrey


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


198 Misc. 884; 101 N.Y.S.2d 892; 1951 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1480



January 5, 1951 



DISPOSITION: Motion granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff distributor of motion pictures filed an action to enjoin defendant Commissioner of Licenses of the City of New York from suspending the licenses of certain theatres and that sought a temporary injunction to prevent such action pending a trial.


OVERVIEW: The issues raised were whether the commissioner had any authority to suspending the licenses of certain theatres because motions pictures shown at the theatres were personally offensive, and if so, whether the authority was capriciously or arbitrarily exercised. The court held that peace officers had no duties with respect to the content of films that had been licensed. Further, the court held that the commissioner was not the protector from affronts of a large portion of New York citizens or even of all of them. The court held that citizens could protect themselves first by ignoring the exhibition, and secondly, any individual could seek to have the board of regents revoke a theatre's permit. If a citizen could show that the license was granted through an abuse of power, the court would grant relief against such an abuse.


OUTCOME: The court granted the motion for temporary injunction.


HEADNOTES

Theatres and shows -- revocation of license of theatre for exhibiting licensed motion picture -- (1) State Education Department exclusively has power to determine whether motion picture is sacrilegious; commissioner of licenses of city of New York lacks such power; suspension of license of theatre exhibiting licensed motion picture claimed to be sacrilegious by said commissioner vacated -- (2) peace officers must prosecute exhibitors of unlicensed films violating decency (Penal Law, § 1141) but have no duties with respect to content of films already licensed (Education Law, § 132).

1. Since the right to determine whether a motion picture is indecent, immoral or sacrilegious is vested exclusively in the State Education Department -- the Education Law (§§ 120-132) providing complete regulations for review and license -- the commissioner of licenses of the city of New York was without power to suspend a license of a theatre which exhibited a motion picture licensed by the Education Department but found by the commissioner to have been blasphemous. A local law which purports to give such municipal officer regulatory [***2] powers as to the content of films is unconstitutional.

2. Section 132 of the Education Law authorizes the Board of Regents to enforce part II of article III of the Education Law but does not relieve any State or local peace officer from detecting and prosecuting any violation of law. Although the Penal Law (§ 1141) forbids a criminal prosecution for the exhibition of a licensed motion picture, peace officers have duties in respect to films such as bringing to justice the exhibitor of an unlicensed film which offends against decency. They have no duties with respect to the content of films which have been licensed.

Suppus v. Bradley


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Bronx County, Part I


101 N.Y.S.2d 557; 1950 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2292



December 1, 1950 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff landlords sought judgment on the pleadings in their action against defendant statutory tenants, seeking a declaration that the Emergency Housing Rent Control Law of the State of New York (Act), N.Y. Unconsol. Law, §8581 et seq. (1950), was unconstitutional. Intervenor-defendants, the State Rent Administrator and the Attorney General, claimed the allegations did not state a cause of action.


OVERVIEW: The landlords sought to remove the statutory tenants from their rental properties and to remove their properties from the roles of available rental property under the Act. The attorney general and the state rent administrator intervened under N.Y. Exec. Law § 68 in support of the constitutionality of the Act. The court held that the landlords failed to state a cause of action. The court found that declaratory judgment was an appropriate remedy if the landlords' allegations were valid, but the court held that the Act was a proper exercise of the state's police power and that the landlords were not denied constitutionally protected rights. The court found that the Act did not violate due process, equal protection, or the contracts clause, and that the landlords were required by law to proceed in accordance with the procedure established in the Act for administrative relief. The court ruled that the Act did not contravene N.Y. Const. art. III, §1.


OUTCOME: The court denied the landlords' motion for judgment on the pleadings. The court granted the motion to dismiss filed by the attorney general and state rent administrator for failure to state a cause of action.

 

2848 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Dekrone v. Bussitil


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Queens County


199 Misc. 70; 102 N.Y.S.2d 291; 1950 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2395



November 30, 1950 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In plaintiff landlord's declaratory judgment action seeking to have declared unconstitutional the Residential Rent Law, 1946 N.Y. Laws ch. 274, defendant State Rent Administrator filed a motion to dismiss the complaint for legal insufficiency and for failure to state facts sufficient to justify the court's exercise of its discretionary jurisdiction. The landlord filed a cross-motion for judgment on the pleadings.


OVERVIEW: The landlord raised a number of constitutional objections to the statute and regulations. The court held that her objections were devoid of merit. The court found that the legislature, after finding that a serious public emergency existed in the housing of a considerable number of persons in the state, concluded that landlords had to suffer a temporary restraint upon the right to withdraw their property from the rental market. The court further found that the legislature delegated to the State Rent Commission the authority to prescribe the mechanics for implementing its conclusion and that neither the delegation of the power, nor the manner in which the delegated power was exercised was unconstitutional. The court further found that the conditions imposed by the State Rent Commission in its regulations on the right of a landlord to obtain a certificate permitting him to withdraw occupied housing accommodations from the rental market were not objectionable.


OUTCOME: The court denied the landlord's motion and directed judgment on the pleadings be entered for the State Rent Administrator.


HEADNOTES

Landlord and tenant -- rent regulation -- constitutionality -- (1) declaratory judgment proper remedy to decree constitutionality of State rent law since no questions of fact involved -- (2) no unconstitutional delegation of power to State rent commission granted by sections of State Residential Rent Law (§ 4, subd. 2; § 9, subd. 4) preventing landlords from withdrawing rental accommodations from market; manner of exercise of delegation of authority by commission not unconstitutional -- (3) judgment on pleadings granted in favor of State Rent Administrator on basis of motion therefor by plaintiff.

1. The remedy of declaratory judgment is appropriate in an action wherein plaintiff seeks to declare unconstitutional the State Residential Rent Law. A constitutional question is involved and there are no questions of fact.

2. Although a declaration of unconstitutionality of the entire law and regulations is sought, plaintiff's attack is principally against the provisions of the law and regulations preventing landlords [***2] from withdrawing certain housing accommodations from the rental market (§ 4, subd. 2; § 9, subd. 4; Regulations, §§ 12, 59). The delegation by the Legislature to the State rent commission of the authority to prescribe the mechanics for implementing the conclusion of the Legislature that landlords must suffer a temporary restraint upon the right to withdraw their property from the rental market renders neither the delegation of the power (§ 4) nor the manner in which the delegated power was exercised (§ 59) unconstitutional. The conditions imposed by section 59 of the regulations on the right of a landlord to obtain a certificate permitting him to withdraw occupied housing accommodations from the rental market are not objectionable.

3. The State Rent Administrator is entitled to judgment on the pleadings under section 476 of the Civil Practice Act and rule 112 of the Rules of Civil Practice on the basis of plaintiff's motion therefor.

 

2849 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Delro Estates v. Fredkin


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Municipal Court of the City of New York, Borough of The Bronx


198 Misc. 837; 100 N.Y.S.2d 419; 1950 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2131



October 26, 1950 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner landlord commenced a summary proceeding to recover possession of respondent tenant's apartment for nonpayment of rent. The landlord claimed the tenant owed the higher amount of rent established under the Federal Housing and Rent Act of 1947 while the tenant claimed that the lower amount was due under the N.Y. Residential Rent Law, codified at 1950 N.Y. Laws ch. 250.


OVERVIEW: The landlord brought a summary proceeding to recover possession of the apartment rented by the tenant for the tenant's nonpayment of rent. The landlord's action also demanded an award of the rent due by the tenant for the last month of the lease term. The landlord had applied for and received permission to raise the tenant's rent under the Act, and the landlord claimed the rental amount allowed under that application was due from the tenant. On the other hand the tenant asserted that the rent due was the lower amount authorized by the Law. The tenant had tendered that lower amount to the landlord, which the landlord refused to accept. The court entered judgment for the tenant on the issue of rent, finding that the lower amount was due and because the landlord had refused to accept that amount when tendered it was not longer entitled to the rent. The court held that under § 21 of the regulations promulgated pursuant to the authority granted by N.Y. Residential Rent Law § 4(2), 1950 N.Y. Laws ch. 250, where the maximum rent prescribed by the Act is higher than that set by the local laws of the city of New York, the lower ceiling shall prevail.


OUTCOME: The court held that the lower rent due under the state law prevailed over the higher amount due under the federal law. The court concluded that because the landlord refused to accept that lower amount as tendered by the tenant, a final order was entered for the tenant.


HEADNOTES

Landlord and tenant -- rent regulation -- maximum rent -- rent of tenant's apartment in New York City up to January 26, 1949, was $ 46 per month; by order of July 1, 1949, maximum rent of tenant's apartment increased by Federal Housing Expediter to $ 49.78, retroactive to January 26, 1949; despite invalidation of section 13-a of State Residential Rent Law, "freeze" date therein of March 1, 1949, not invalidated; since rent paid by tenant on that day was $ 46 per month, rent of said apartment for August, 1950, also $ 46; final order for tenant.

In summary proceedings to recover possession of an apartment for nonpayment of rent it appeared that tenant paid $ 46 per month up to January 26, 1949. On July 1, 1949, the Federal Housing Expediter increased the maximum rent to $ 49.78 effective retroactively to January 26, 1949. Although section 13-a of the State Residential Rent Law, which fixes the "freeze" date for New York City as March 1, 1949, was declared unconstitutional by the Court of Appeals (Teeval Co. v. Stern, 301 N. Y. 346, 93 N.E.2d 884), nevertheless under that decision [***2] the rents for housing accommodations in the city of New York are deemed rolled back to March 1, 1949. Moreover, such decision rejected laws dealing almost exclusively with the prohibition of the collection of increased retroactive rent. Since the petition in this proceeding seeks rent for the current month only, it is apparent that the maximum rent of tenant's apartment for the month of August, 1950, was $ 46. Since that amount was tendered and rejected by landlord, tenant is entitled to a final order.

 

2850 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Baldwin Gardens v. McGoldrick


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Nassau County


198 Misc. 743; 100 N.Y.S.2d 548; 1950 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2155



October 19, 1950 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner landlord brought an action under N.Y. Civ. Proc. Act art. 78 to restrain respondent New York State Rent Administrator from enforcing the maximum rent established by him pursuant to N.Y. Emer. Hous. Rent Control Law, 1950 N.Y. Laws ch. 250, on the ground that such law was unconstitutional insofar as it affected the landlord. The Administrator cross-moved to dismiss the petition both on jurisdictional and substantive grounds.


OVERVIEW: The landlord was aggrieved by an emergency rent control measure. His petition raised a number of claims, but the court did not consider them because the relief sought by the landlord was in the nature of prohibition, and prohibition was not the proper vehicle for the relief sought. The Administrator's enforcement of the law was an administrative task, and the duty of setting maximum rents was a legislative function. Thus, the main prerequisite for the issuance of prohibition, relief from judicial or quasi-judicial action, was absent. Furthermore, prohibition was available only for action not yet completed, yet the administrative determination that the landlord complained of had already been adopted and promulgated.


OUTCOME: The court did not pass on the merits of the case.


HEADNOTES

Proceeding against body or officer -- enjoining enforcement of State rent law -- proceeding in nature of writ of prohibition (Civ. Prac. Act, art. 78) does not lie to restrain State Rent Administrator from enforcing State rent law and from establishing maximum rents for petitioner's housing accommodations since his functions not judicial or quasi-judicial but administrative and legislative.

A proceeding under article 78 of the Civil Practice Act in the nature of a writ of prohibition will not lie against the State Rent Administrator to enjoin the enforcement of the prohibitions contained in chapter 250 of the Laws of 1950, and to prevent his establishing the maximum rents for petitioner's housing accommodations. Prohibition does not lie, except to restrain judicial or quasijudicial functions (Civ. Prac. Act, § 1284, subd 4). Enforcing the law is an administrative task and fixing maximum rents is a legislative function. Moreover, prohibition is available only for action not yet contemplated and here the administrative determination as to the maximum rents has already been adopted and promulgated. In addition prohibition [***2] is a remedy issued only in cases of extreme necessity where the applicant would be remediless without it and where the grievance cannot be redressed by ordinary proceedings at law or in equity.

 

2851 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Wolfe


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Kings County


199 Misc. 413; 102 N.Y.S.2d 12; 1950 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2371



October 14, 1950 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of murder in the first degree and sentenced to death. He sought a new trial on the grounds of newly discovered evidence.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with killing his wife. He advised his attorney not to assert an insanity defense at trial and was convicted. Subsequent to the jury's verdict, a defense attorney petitioned for a stay of proceedings so that defendant could be committed for a mental examination. The hospital found that defendant was insane and was incapable of understanding the charge and proceedings against him. Defendant was committed. When he was discharged, the trial court found that defendant was not presently insane and imposed the death penalty. Defendant sought a new trial on the ground of newly discovered evidence, asserting that during trial he was in such a state of idiocy or insanity as to be incapable of understanding the proceedings or making his defense. The State argued that the conviction was final. The court granted relief, declaring the murder trial a nullity. While defendant acted normally during trial, after the verdict he acted in such an unusual and rambling manner to place doubt that he was sane. Evidence from doctors showed that his condition was present during the trial and that the trial court therefore did not have authority to permit the trial to commence.


OUTCOME: The court ruled in favor of defendant, holding that his original trial for murder was a nullity.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- insanity as defense -- (1) defendant convicted of murder in first degree in 1944; after jury brought in verdict, defendant harangued court in rambling manner; defendant committed to hospital for examination and found at that time to be insane; order made committing defendant to State hospital for criminal insane; in 1950, superintendent of State hospital found defendant had recovered sanity, and defendant brought to court for passing of mandatory death sentence upon him; defense attorney then moved for new trial on ground that in 1944 defendant incapable of understanding charge against him and of making his defense thereto; in view of all facts, court grants motion for new trial -- (2) court has power to entertain such motion.

1. Defendant was convicted of murder in the first degree on November 1, 1944. Prior to the trial he expressly enjoined his attorney from interposing a defense of insanity. After the jury had brought in its verdict defendant made a long harangue to the court in an unusual and rambling manner which was one of the circumstances which caused the court to commit defendant to a hospital for an examination as to his [***2] sanity. The psychiatrists of said hospital found defendant to be insane. Thereafter, an order was made committing defendant to a State hospital for the criminal insane. Five years and two months after such order of commitment the superintendent of the State hospital certified that defendant was no longer so insane as to be incapable of understanding the charge against him. Defendant was then returned to the court. Thereafter and while hearings were being held to determine whether the court should confirm the report of the authorities of the State hospital, the doctors of the State hospital reversed their findings as to defendant's mental condition and once again found that he was insane. The court then appointed independent psychiatrists who found defendant to have suffered a manic depressive psychosis and also found that defendant had recovered sufficiently to enable him to understand the proceedings before the court. The court then found defendant presently capable of understanding the charge against him and of making his defense thereto. Defendant was brought before the court and the mandatory death sentence was pronounced upon him. On the next day defendant's attorney [***3] moved for a new trial on the ground that at the time defendant was placed on trial and all during the trial he was in such a state of insanity as to be incapable of understanding the proceedings against him or of making his defense. A review of all the facts leads the court to the conclusion that the motion should be granted. Defendant's behavior for a period of some six years and the opinions of the experts indicate that at the time of his trial his mental condition was such as to cast great doubt upon his capacity to defend himself properly. The question of defendant's responsibility for the crime under section 1120 of the Penal Law is for a trial jury to pass upon, if defendant elects to interpose a defense of insanity.

2. The Trial Court has the power to entertain a motion for a new trial on the ground that defendant was incapable of making his defense to the charge at the time he was tried. The determination whether or not an insane person is capable of understanding the proceedings or making his defense rests upon the court and the full responsibility for deciding whether or not such person should be placed on trial or sentenced to any punishment or punished for a crime,  [***4] is one that the court itself must assume.

 

2852 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Hess


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Police Justice's Court of New York, Village of Amityville


198 Misc. 913; 103 N.Y.S.2d 582; 1950 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2489



October 3, 1950 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with having violated Amityville, N.Y., Ordinance art. II, § 26 by distributing leaflets on the street.


OVERVIEW: Defendant passed political circulars on the street. Defendant was charged with violating Amityville, N.Y., Ordinance art. II, § 26, which prohibited the circulation of printed materials on the street. At the conclusion of the trial, defendant filed a motion to dismiss the information on the grounds (1) that the ordinance did not apply to defendant's admitted distribution of noncommercial, non-advertising leaflets, and (2) that, if the ordinance did so apply, it would be unconstitutional as violative of N.Y. Const. art. I, § 8 and the First and Fourteenth Amendments of the United States Constitution. The court found that the ordinance did not apply to non-advertising material. The court held that if the ordinance were interpreted to apply to political and other literature, it would violate the constitutional guarantee of freedom of speech because it would prohibit a person lawfully upon the streets to impart information or opinion either through speech or by the distribution of literature. Although the constitutional guarantee was not unlimited, defendant's actions did not result in littering or the blocking of streets, which were proper areas for the village to regulate.


OUTCOME: The court found that the ordinance did not apply to defendant's activities.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- freedom of speech -- village ordinance prohibiting distribution on streets of circulars or other advertising matter inapplicable to distribution of handbill containing political matter; should ordinance apply to other than advertising matter it must be held unconstitutional.

An ordinance of a village which forbids the distribution of handbills or other advertising matter on the streets of the village is inapplicable to the distribution by defendant of circulars prepared at the instance of a political party and containing matter political in nature. Should the ordinance apply to other than advertising matter it must be held unconstitutional as a violation of defendant's right of freedom of speech as guaranteed by the State Constitution (art. I, § 8) and Federal Constitution (1st and 14th Amendts.). Although there was no evidence as to the intent of the village board at the time the ordinance was enacted, it cannot be assumed that the board intended to enact an ordinance in violation of constitutional rights.

 

2853 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


New York v. Donner


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Domestic Relations Court of the City of New York, Children's Court, School Part, Kings County


199 Misc. 643; 99 N.Y.S.2d 830; 1950 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2038



September 25, 1950 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent fathers were before the court in a prosecution for their alleged violation of the State of New York's compulsory education law, N.Y. Educ. Law § 3212. The fathers' children attended a small, religious school that was not registered with nor recognized by the committee that supervised secular instruction in such parochial schools.


OVERVIEW: The children's primary curriculum was religious and they did not receive any formal or systematic instruction in the common branches of study required by N.Y. Educ. Law § 3204. The fathers claimed that their religious belief prohibited all systematic, secular education of their children, a concept in conflict with the provisions of § 3204. The fathers argued that, in view of their deep religious convictions, enforcement of the compulsory education law against them infringed on their religious liberty. The court noted that an infringement of the fathers' religious liberty did not necessarily mean that their constitutional rights had been violated. The court held that the fathers' religious convictions must yield to the public interest in compulsory education laws, which were universally enacted for the benefit of the country's children. The court concluded that the fathers' religious convictions could not interfere with the responsibility of the State to protect children's welfare.


OUTCOME: The court held that the fathers' constitutional rights had not been violated and, consequently, each of them was guilty of having violated the compulsory education law. The court directed the fathers to appear on a subsequent date for sentencing.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- freedom of religion -- respondents whose children attend religious school only, where they do not receive systematic instruction in courses of study required by Education Law (§ 3204) and where English is not language of instruction, are guilty of violation of Education Law (§ 3212); freedom of religion gives way to requirement of Education Law for common good.

Respondents whose children of school age do not attend schools in which secular subjects are taught but instead attend a religious school where they are taught by an instructor not possessing the minimum qualifications of a teacher for secular instruction required by the board of education of the city of New York and there do not receive any systematic instruction in the ten common branches and other courses of study required by section 3204 of the Education Law and where English is not the language of instruction, are guilty of violating the Compulsory EducationLaw (Education Law, § 3212). Respondents' failure to obtain secular education for their children constitutes conduct which is subject to regulation for the protection [***2] of society and although they oppose secular education on religious grounds they are not exempt from the obligations imposed by the Education Law. The secular law in such case of conflict takes precedence over the religious law where the interests of a democratic society clearly requires compliance with the secular law. Religious convictions of parents cannot interfere with the responsibility of the State to protect the welfare of children. The constitutional rights of respondents have not been violated.

 

2854 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Hoff v. Long Beach


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Queens County


198 Misc. 898; 99 N.Y.S.2d 397; 1950 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1946



August 18, 1950 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, owners and/or drivers of taxicabs, filed a motion for an injunction pendente lite to restrain the enforcement of an ordinance passed by defendant city and of certain rules and regulations promulgated by the police department of that city, which regulated the operation of taxicabs within the city.


OVERVIEW: The city enacted an ordinance which forbade taxicabs from cruising for passengers; the taxi drivers could pick up passengers that had called them, Long Beach, N.Y., City Ord. § 8(d), but driving an empty cab on the roadways of the city was presumptive evidence of cruising, Long Beach, N.Y., City Ord. § 2(e). Plaintiffs argued that the ordinance was null and void as an infringement of the State's exclusive control of the streets and highways and that rules and regulations of the police department, pursuant to the authority vested in it by the ordinance, were illegal or in contravention of State rule. The court disagreed and refused to grant an injunction. The court stated that there was no inherent or vested right to use public streets for hire. Such use was a privilege which could be granted by a city, subject to reasonable regulation, and was not in contravention of state rules. The court found that pursuant to the authority vested in it by N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 54, the city had the power to enact a reasonable ordinance regulating the operation of taxicabs. The court held that a prohibition against cruising was within that power.


OUTCOME: The court denied the motion.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- regulation of taxicabs -- (1) city enacted ordinance prohibiting taxicabs from cruising on streets of city; motion for temporary injunction to restrain enforcement of ordinance denied since clear right to injunction insufficiently established by factual proof -- (2) municipality had power to prohibit cruising on streets under its charter and Vehicle and Traffic Law -- (3) on papers before court such regulation of taxicabs not unreasonable -- (4) regulations of police department were enacted pursuant to authority delegated by Vehicle and Traffic Law -- (5) ordinance formulated sufficient standard for guidance of police department in enacting regulations -- (6) regulations restricting places from which community riding permitted and prohibiting taxicabs from picking up passengers from designated bus stop not ultra vires -- (7) section authorizing suspension of license during investigation of charges constitutional.

1. An ordinance of defendant city prevents cruising by taxicabs within such city. Plaintiffs' motion for a temporary injunction to restrain enforcement by the city of such ordinance and of the rules [***2] and regulations of the police department of the city should be denied since the factual proof of a clear right to an injunction advanced by plaintiffs is insufficient.

2. There is no inherent or vested right to use public streets for hire. Such use is a privilege which may be granted subject to reasonable regulation by a municipality pursuant to authority delegated to it by the State, here the charter of the city and the Vehicle and Traffic Law (§ 54, second par.). The prohibition against cruising was within that power.

3. On the papers before the court such regulation of taxicabs is not on its face unreasonable although after development of the facts on the trial it may be that such unreasonableness may be demonstrated.

4. The rules and regulations promulgated by the police department of the city pursuant to the ordinance are not illegal on the claimed ground that the charter of the city did not authorize the delegation of such authority to the police department. Although the authority to delegate is not contained in the charter, the Legislature gave such authority in the Vehicle and Traffic Law (§ 90, subds. 4, 6).

5. The ordinance authorizes the police department to promulgate [***3] such regulations "as they may deem necessary for the proper conduct of the taxicab business, and to that end may promulgate rules and regulations as to cruising, use of streets and use of equipment and such other regulations as they may deem necessary and proper". The standard therein set forth upon the basis of which the police department enacted the regulations was sufficient.

6. Regulations restricting the places from which community riding is permitted and prohibiting taxicabs from picking up passengers from a designated bus stop are not ultra vires but within the city's power to regulate the conduct of the taxicab business.

7. A section of the ordinance provides that the commissioner, the chief of police or acting chief of police may revoke a license for reasonable cause after a hearing at which the driver and owner may present proof and cross-examine witnesses. It is also provided that such officers may in their discretion suspend any license during an investigation of charges of improper conduct by a driver or owner of a taxicab license. Such section is not unconstitutional. No license may be revoked until after a hearing. The suspension of a license during the investigation [***4] of charges of improper conduct is not an unreasonable method of obtaining that continued control of such drivers which is essential to the protection of the public.

 

2855 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Brown v. New York State Tax Com.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Onondaga County


199 Misc. 349; 99 N.Y.S.2d 73; 1950 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1879



July 5, 1950 



DISPOSITION: The motion is, therefore, denied, with $ 10 costs to plaintiff.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff taxpayer brought an action against defendant, the New York State Tax Commission, for both declaratory and injunctive relief against the Commission with respect to an assessment of additional personal income tax against the taxpayer for the year 1946. The Commission filed a motion to dismiss the taxpayer's complaint for insufficiency.


OVERVIEW: The question arose whether the Commission, by virtue of the authority vested in it by N.Y. Tax Law § 373, could arbitrarily make an additional assessment against a taxpayer for the sole purpose of extending the three-year statutory period within which it was required to determine the amount of tax due from the taxpayer. The court held that wide discretion had to be given the Commission in making an additional assessment or revising a return, but it could not be seriously contended that the Legislature ever intended to permit such action without any basis for so doing and for the sole purpose of procuring an extension of the Commission's time in which to make a different assessment. The court determined that the action of the Commission in making an additional assessment against the taxpayer after the latter refused to consent to an extension for the tax determination, was unjustified and arbitrary as no authority was granted so to do by the section in question. Accordingly, the additional assessment against the taxpayer was invalid and void. The court concluded that the complaint stated a cause of action.


OUTCOME: The court denied the Commission's motion to dismiss.


HEADNOTES

Taxation -- income tax -- assessment of additional tax -- (1) complaint of taxpayer alleges that State Tax Commission without facts to support it and solely for purpose of extending three-year period provided in Tax Law (§ 373) within which amount of income tax might be corrected served notice ofassessment upon plaintiff; complaint further alleges that such action capricious and seeks declaration that Tax Law (§ 373) does not authorize such action by commission; Tax Law (§§ 374-375) provides for manner of reviewing determination of commission fixing tax and states that such manner is exclusive; complaint states cause of action -- (2) while commission had authority to make estimate of taxable income on basis of information in its possession, it had no authority to make assessment without information for purpose of extending commission's time in which to make different assessment; commission may not circumvent three-year limitation contained in statute by methods alleged in complaint -- (3) making of additional assessment against plaintiff invalid -- (4)  [***2] declaratory judgment proper remedy to determine authority of commission to make additional assessment under Tax Law (§ 373) -- (5) provision of Tax Law (§ 375) that said section furnishes exclusive remedy inapplicable since complaint alleges that commission not acting within section 373.

1. A complaint by a taxpayer alleges that the State Tax Commission in order to secure an extension of the time within which to determine the amount of an additional income tax which might be due from said taxpayer, sent taxpayer a notice of additional assessment purporting to have been made pursuant to section 373 of the Tax Law and alleges that such notice of assessment was not based on information or facts in possession of defendant but was capricious and done solely to procure said extension of time at a date more than three years after the tax return was made. Plaintiff therefore asks judgment declaring that the right asserted by defendant does not exist under section 373 of the Tax Law. Section 373 of the Tax Law provides that the tax commission may revise a taxpayer's return if incorrect or if a taxpayer fails to make a return and that the commission may estimate the taxable income and [***3] assess the taxes due the State. It then provides that the amount of tax due under any return must be determined within three years after the return is made. Section 374 provides for the revision and adjustment of a taxpayer's return and for a hearing to the taxpayer with respect thereto. Section 375 provides for the manner in which the determination of the tax commission shall be reviewed and it then provides that the remedy for review of a determination shall be the exclusive remedy to determine judicially the liability of a taxpayer for taxes. The complaint states a cause of action. Jurisdiction of this action for declaratory judgment should be retained.

2. While discretionary power is granted the commission to make an estimate of the taxable income of a taxpayer from any information in its possession, where it is alleged that the commission had no basis whatever for its estimate of plaintiff's taxable return -- and on a motion attacking the complaint, the facts therein are admitted -- the commission was not authorized by section 373 of the Tax Law to make an assessment without any basis for so doing and for the sole purpose of procuring an extension of the commission's time [***4] in which to make a different assessment. The three-year limitation in the Tax Law benefits and protects the taxpayer and the commission should not be allowed to circumvent this provision by the methods alleged in the complaint.

3. The action of the State Tax Commission in making the additional assessment was unjustified and such assessment is invalid and void.

4. Declaratory judgment is the proper remedy to stabilize the uncertain jural relations between the parties as to the commission's authority under section 373. Since it involves an interpretation of a section of the law not previously judicially construed, declaratory judgment is the proper remedy.

5. The provision contained in section 375 of the Tax Law stating that the section furnishes the exclusive remedy to a taxpayer does not apply unless the commission acts within the authority of section 373 of the Tax Law. Since plaintiff alleges that the commission is acting without jurisdiction in making the assessment, said provision does not apply.

 

2856 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Schrager v. Albany


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Rensselaer County


197 Misc. 903; 99 N.Y.S.2d 697; 1950 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2008



July 1, 1950 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff ice cream seller filed a motion for temporary injunction against defendant City of Albany and sought a judgment declaring that Albany, N.Y., Gen. Ordinance ch. 10, as amended, was invalid, void, and unconstitutional. The ice cream seller also sought to enjoin the enforcement of the provisions against him.


OVERVIEW: Gen. Ordinance ch. 10, § 1 required all peddlers to be licensed but permitted the issuance of a license only to a person who had been a resident of the City for at least six months. Albany, N.Y., Gen. Ordinance ch. 10, § 4 prohibited vehicles from remaining on the public street for longer than five minutes while offering goods for sale, and Albany, N.Y., Gen. Ordinance ch. 10, § 12 prohibited venders from engaging in business on certain streets within 300 feet of any school building. The seller contended that the enactment was discriminatory and unconstitutional in that the provisions discriminated against a nonresident who was not permitted to obtain a license thereunder and made an illegal distinction between a vender having a stated place of business in the City and one who had not such. The court held that regulations whose purposes were asserted to be in the public interest should be reasonable, adapted to cure a substantial evil and applicable to all persons similarly situated. Thus, it held that Albany, N.Y., Gen. Ordinance ch. 10, § 1 was unconstitutional and invalid since it worked a discrimination against nonresidents of the City in favor of residents of the City.


OUTCOME: The court granted the motion.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- enjoining void ordinance -- (1) court assumes jurisdiction of action to declare unconstitutional ordinance of City of Albany prohibiting plaintiff from conducting ice cream vending business from trucks on streets, and will enjoin enforcement since plaintiff's business rights affected -- (2) section 1 of said ordinance which provides for licensing of peddlers but permits issuance of licenses only to persons resident in city for six months unconstitutional -- (3) plaintiff's complaint and affidavits show such unconstitutionality of ordinance as to warrant temporary injunction.

1. A court of equity will assume jurisdiction of this action to declare an ordinance of the City of Albany unconstitutional and will enjoin the enforcement of its provisions against plaintiff who sells at retail ice cream from trucks on the streets of the city of Albany, where its enforcement will affect plaintiff's property rights and work irreparable injury. The criminality of the act performed by plaintiff will not bar such relief where an injunction is necessary for the protection of one's property rights (General Municipal Law, § [***2] 80).

2. Section 1 of said ordinance which requires peddlers to be licensed but permits the issuance of a license only to persons who have been residents of the city for at least six months is unconstitutional since it discriminates against nonresidents in favor of residents of the city.

3. Other provisions of the ordinance prohibit vehicles from remaining on the public street for longer than five minutes while offering goods for sale and prohibit the use of a horn or other instrument to announce the approach of a vehicle. Still another provision prevents ice cream venders from doing business on certain named streets and parkways within 100 feet of any street intersection and 300 feet of any school building. By his complaint and supporting affidavits plaintiff has made a sufficient showing to question the constitutionality of such provisions on the grounds: (1) that the enactment is not fairly adapted to cure a real or substantial evil; (2) that the enactment bears no reasonable relation between the evil and the attempted remedy; (3) that it discriminates between persons having a place of business in the city and those who do not and (4) that it is an attempt to regulate the use [***3] of public highways which is reserved wholly to the State. Such a showing justifies the granting, in the court's discretion, of plaintiff's motion for a temporary injunction to preserve the status quo until the trial.

 

2857 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Vestal v. Bennett


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Broome County


199 Misc. 41; 104 N.Y.S.2d 830; 1950 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2509



June 30, 1950 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff town board sought an injunction to restrain defendant junkyard operators from operating a junk yard in noncompliance with a town ordinance, enacted pursuant to the provisions of N.Y. Town Law § 136, requiring a six-foot fence around the junkyard if it was operated outside of a building. The operators had conducted business in the manner opposed prior to the enactment of the subject ordinance.


OVERVIEW: For approximately 10 years preceding the adoption of the town's ordinance, the operators had conducted a junk business on the land they owned, located on a triangular piece of land adjacent to two intersecting highways. Owing to the operators' refusal to erect a fence in conformance with the ordinance, they were denied a license, and the town sought to restrain their operations. In dismissing the town's complaint, the court found that the town was constitutionally empowered to adopt ordinances to regulate the conduct of a junk business in respect to matters that affected the public health, morals, safety, and general welfare. However, the court determined that aesthetic considerations were only to be given regard if recognized police power considerations were sufficiently present. In the instant case, the court concluded that the aesthetic considerations on which the regulations were based were insufficient to uphold the law's legality as a reasonable exercise of the town's police power inasmuch as the regulatory measure applied to the entire town. Thus, the enactment of the subject provisions of the ordinance was not within the town's power, rendering it unconstitutional and void.


OUTCOME: The court granted the operators a judgment and dismissed the town's complaint with costs.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- regulation of junk yards -- ordinance requiring junk yard to be in buildings or enclosed by six-foot-high solid board fence on all sides, unconstitutional as unreasonable under police power; considerations, including aesthetic ones, insufficient to uphold reasonableness of regulation.

A municipality cannot regulate junk dealers by adopting an ordinance under the guise of police power which is not reasonable and does not bear a relationship to the protection of public morals, health or general welfare. An ordinance enacted by plaintiff town which required that the premises of junk dealers be in enclosed buildings or enclosed with a solid board fence at least six feet high on all sides, is unconstitutional. The sole purpose of the ordinance is to hide unsightly premises from the view of persons traveling along adjacent highways. Aesthetic considerations may be regarded in connection with recognized police power considerations and, if the latter are sufficiently present, the fact that considerations of an aesthetic nature entered into the reasons for the regulation will not render it invalid.  [***2] The aesthetic considerations on which the regulation here is based are not sufficient to uphold their legality as a reasonable exercise of the police power.

 

2858 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Fara Investors, Inc. v. Lourie


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Bronx County


199 Misc. 288; 106 N.Y.S.2d 66; 1950 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2527



June 26, 1950 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff landlord brought an action for a declaratory judgment to challenge the constitutionality of § 203(b) of the Housing and Rent Act of 1949, 50 U.S.C.S. app. § 1894(b).


OVERVIEW: The landlord purchased an apartment building at a foreclosure sale, and the tenants had occupied apartments in the building under leases from the previous owner. The original leases had been filed with the Housing Expediter, but the leases expired at the end of the year of the purchase. New leases were executed at a higher rate. Section 203(b) of the Act fixed the maximum amount for rent at the rent set forth in the lease previously filed with the Housing Expediter. The landlord claimed that it was deprived of an increase that would have been permitted prior to the enactment of § 203(b) and that the provision was discriminatory and in violation of the Fifth Amendment. Although the provision might have seemed unsound, the court held that § 203(b) was constitutional. Pursuant to N.Y. Civ. Prac. Act § 440, the court found that the landlord was not entitled to declaratory judgment relief.


OUTCOME: The court entered a judgment in favor of the tenants.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- landlord and tenant -- rent regulation -- (1) tenants of apartments under leases made in 1947, expiring after December 31, 1948, and filed with Federal Housing Expediter were made parties to foreclosure action; plaintiff purchased apartment house as result of foreclosure sale; tenants' leases terminated by foreclosure and apartments decontrolled until April 1, 194 9, when Federal Housing and Rent Act of 1949 (§ 203, subd. [b]) took effect -- (2) subdivision (b) of section 203 constitutional despite fact that maximum rent of tenants' apartments reverted to rents in 1947 leases rather than higher rentals contained in 1948 leases.

1. Tenants of an apartment house had leases made in 1947 and expiring after December 31, 1948, which leases were filed with the Housing Expediter. Plaintiff purchased the apartment building at a foreclosure sale in January, 1948. As a result of said foreclosure, wherein tenants had been made parties, tenants' leases terminated. New leases at higher rentals were executed in 1948. The apartments occupied by tenants were decontrolled from February 1, 1948, to April 1, 1949, when the [***2] Federal Housing and Rent Act of 1949, took effect and recontrolled said housing accommodations fixing the maximum rent as that set forth in the leases filed in 1947 (§ 203, subd. [b]).

2. Said subdivision (b) of section 203 of the Housing and Rent Act of 1949 is constitutional despite the fact that plaintiff was deprived of the 15% increases that would have been permitted upon termination of leases on or after April 1, 1948. In this action for a declaratory judgment that the subdivision is unconstitutional, the relief sought must be refused.

 

2859 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Zorach v. Clauson


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Kings County


198 Misc. 631; 99 N.Y.S.2d 339; 1950 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1936
