June 19, 1950 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner parents filed an application pursuant to N.Y. Civil Practice Act § 78 for a trial to review the constitutionality of N.Y. Educ. Law § 3210 authorizing respondent Board of Education's (board) "released time program" for the dismissal of children from public school for religious instruction.


OVERVIEW: The board established a "released time program" which permitted the dismissal of children from public school for religious instruction under certain conditions. Respondent coordinating committee, an organization wholly independent of the school system or of any particular religious organization, prepared and distributed the program's registration cards. The parents challenged the constitutionality of the program and N.Y. Educ. Law § 3210 that authorized the program, claiming that a child whose parents did not choose any form of religious instruction could be classed as a dissenter and thereby humiliated. The court dismissed the parents' petition on the merits as a matter of law, holding that no issues existed in the pleadings that required a trial. The court determined that the statute and the program itself were constitutional under the First Amendment's freedom "of" religion, not freedom "from" religion. The parents' generalized allegation of the program's poor administration did not affect the constitutionality of the statute.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the parents' petition for a trial on the constitutionality of the board's "released time program" for the dismissal of children from public school for religious instruction.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- released time from public school for religious instruction -- (1) "released time program" of religious instruction in public schools created by Education Law ( § 625) and regulations of State Commissioner of Education, constitutional -- (2) no issue of maladministration of statute or regulations presented by petition; proceeding (Civ. Prac. Act, art. 78) to declare "released time program" unconstitutional dismissed.

1. The "released time program" of religious instruction now in practice in the public schools of the city of New York and elsewhere throughout the State, as created by former section 625 of the Education Law (L. 1940, ch. 305; now Education Law, § 3210) and by the regulations of the State Commissioner of Education promulgated pursuant thereto, is constitutional. To permit restraint as petitioner would have the court impose upon State and local educational agencies which are lawfully authorized to grant released time for those pupils who wish to take religious instruction would constitute a suppression of the right of religious freedom and would be a denial of a basic right guaranteed [***2] by the American form of Government. The present released time program contains none of the features found objectionable by the Supreme Court of United States in Illinois ex rel. McCollum v. Board of Educ. of School Dist. No. 71, Champaign Co. (333 U.S. 203, 92 L. Ed. 649, 68 S. Ct. 461).

2. If there are maladministrations in particular instances in such released time program, particulars of which are not recited in the petition, such practices respecting administrative details do not affect the constitutionality of the statute. No triable issues are created by the petition with respect to the administration of the statute. If any particular teacher violates the regulations, ample machinery to prevent a particular instance of maladministration exists. Maladministration is not an issue because no record has been presented of an arbitrary or capricious ruling by respondents respecting any particular act of maladministration. None of the regulations adopted by the Commissioner of Education is arbitrary or capricious or unreasonable in law or in fact. A petition (Civ. Prac. Act, art. 78) to declare the "released time program" unconstitutional is dismissed on the merits as a matter [***3] of law.

 

2860 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People ex rel. Rankin v. Ruthazer


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


198 Misc. 276; 98 N.Y.S.2d 104; 1950 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1743



June 2, 1950 



DISPOSITION: There being no further question of fact the writ is denied. Relator is remanded for surrender to the officer of the State of Michigan.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Relator, a parolee, filed for a writ of habeas corpus against respondent, the Warden of the City Prison of the City of New York. The parolee claimed that his extradition under N.Y. Correct. Law § 224 to Michigan for a parole violation was unlawful.


OVERVIEW: The parolee was in prison in Michigan, which extradited him to New York on a parole of custody certificate to be tried for murder. The New York indictment was dismissed. Two years later, the Michigan court issued a parole certificate. During the parole term, the Michigan court issued a parole violation warrant against the parolee, whom the New York police arrested. New York State prepared to extradite the parolee back to Michigan pursuant to N.Y. Correction Law § 224, which created a compact between the governor and all other states to provide for the residence of persons on probation or parole from one state in another. Upon compliance with its provisions, a harboring state was required to yield the parolee to the sending state. Whether the statute applied after the parolee had served his entire term was an issue to be determined by the Michigan courts. The parolee argued that the statute violated U.S. Const. art. IV § 2 and similar state provisions. The court explained that it was appropriate to have more than one means of extradition and that the Constitutional provision itself was in the nature of a compact among all of the states that required the state's recognition.


OUTCOME: The court denied the writ of habeas corpus.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- pardon -- parole -- agreement between States with respect to parolees -- (1) Correction Law (§ 224) providing for Governor to enter into compact with other States to provide for residence of persons on parole from one State in another and providing for supervision of parolees is constitutional -- (2) Correction Law (§ 224) sets forth compact between Michigan and New York and provides that accredited officers of Michigan may enter New York and apprehend person on parole; compact further provides that decision of Michigan to retake person shall be conclusive; relator concedes that officer seeking relator is accredited and that relator is person to be retaken; question whether relator is or is not on parole beyond power of New York to review; writ denied and relator remanded for surrender to Michigan.

1. Section 224 of the Correction Law authorizes the Governor on behalf of the State to enter into a compact with all other States which shall sign the same compact. The general purpose [***2] of the compact is to provide for the residence of persons on probation or parole from one State in another and provides the conditions of such residence, for supervision and the like. The section is constitutional. It does not violate section 2 of article IV of the Federal Constitution providing for extradition since the constitutional provision does not purport to provide the only procedure for extradition. The constitutional provision is in itself in the nature of a compact between all the States and is a provision that this State must recognize.

2. Section 224 of the Correction Law sets forth the compact between Michigan and New York and provides that duly accredited officers of a sending State (Michigan) may at all times enter a receiving State (New York) and there apprehend and retake one on probation or parole. It further provides that the decision of the sending State to retake a person on probation or parole shall be conclusive upon and not reviewable within the receiving State. It is conceded that the officer seeking relator is the duly accredited officer of the sending State and that relator is the person who is to be retaken. According to the statute therefore these [***3] are the only questions to be decided in the receiving State. The question of whether relator is presently on parole and has completely served his term in Michigan need not be reviewed within the receiving State. If the procedure indicated in the statute is followed, the receiving State must yield the individual. Beyond that the provision does not go. There being no further question of fact, the writ is denied and relator is remanded for surrender to the officer of the State of Michigan.

 

2861 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Gedney Estates v. White Plains


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Westchester County, Part II


99 N.Y.S.2d 111; 1950 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1892



May 31, 1950 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff landowners sought a declaratory judgment that certain ordinances enacted by defendant city's common council were void and unconstitutional and that a certain lot be zoned in either a residence A district or a district R-I. The landowners also sought a determination of the parties' rights to the action, which parties included defendants, a city, a hospital, and the buyers of the hospital's land.


OVERVIEW: The hospital owned land placed in a single-family residence district of the A class. The buyers entered into a contract with the hospital for the purchase of the land, and the common council passed an amending ordinance changing the land's designation to a residence B district to permit the erection of an apartment house. No action was taken by the buyers under the amending ordinance, and the common council enacted an entirely new overall ordinance, which repealed the amending ordinance. Thereafter, the land contracted for purchase by the buyers was designated as a grouped multi-family structure, and the landowners brought an action challenging the validity of the amending and the overall ordinances. The court held that the validity of the amending ordinance was no longer a justiciable issue as the total repeal of that ordinance made the issue of its validity academic, and no action of any kind in reliance upon it was taken before its repeal. The court found that the overall ordinance did not grant a special privilege to the buyers and was not brought about by any improper motive or intent. The court thus concluded that such an exercise of the city's police power was proper.


OUTCOME: The court granted the buyers' motion to dismiss the first and second causes of the landowners' action regarding the validity of the amending ordinance. The court also granted the buyers' motion for judgment on the third and fourth causes of action and declared the overall ordinance constitutional and valid.

 

2862 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Meyer v. Zimmer


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


City Court of New York, Rochester


197 Misc. 653; 97 N.Y.S.2d 457; 1950 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1657



May 17, 1950 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] On Reargument June 22, 1950; See 197 Misc. 894, 98 N.Y.S.2d 1003 



DISPOSITION: The tenant's motions are denied and the case is placed on the Summary Proceeding Calendar for May 23, 1950.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant tenant filed a motion for dismissal or stay of summary proceedings initiated by plaintiff landlord to recover possession of the real property.


OVERVIEW: The question raised involved the validity and effect of N.Y. Rent & Eviction Regs. § 51(4), which provided that no tenant was to be removed or evicted in any action or proceeding pending on May 1, 1950 except after compliance with the regulations. The proceeding was commenced on April 14, 1950, returnable on April 25, 1950, and the parties appeared on the return day and adjourned the same to May 5, 1950 when the motions were made. The landlord contested the validity of § 51(4) on grounds that the regulation was not authorized by the Residential Rent Law, 1950 N.Y. Laws ch. 250, and that the measure was retroactive and impaired a vested right. The court held that N.Y. Rent & Eviction Regs. § 51(4) was not authorized by the Residential Rent Law because the Residential Rent Law fell far short of containing an unequivocal declaration of an intent to confer power for the adoption of a retroactive regulation that impaired vested rights. Section 51(4) was invalid as an attempt to exercise power not conferred by law.


OUTCOME: The tenant's motion was denied.


HEADNOTES

Landlord and tenant -- rent regulation -- validity of regulations of State Rent Commission -- State Housing Rent Commission's regulations (§ 51, subd. 4) restricting removal of tenant in proceeding pending on May 1, 1950, except after compliance with regulations, and providing that proceeding to evict deemed to be pending if warrant of eviction not executed prior to May 1, 1950, invalid; State Residental Rent Law does not empower commission to require new certificate with respect to proceeding pending in courts prior to effective date of State Residential Rent Law, May 1, 1950, on certificate granted by Federal Housing Expediter.

Subdivision 4 of section 51 of the Rent and Eviction Regulations -- promulgated by the Temporary State Housing Rent Commission pursuant to the New York State Residential Rent Law (L. 1950, ch. 250) -- which provides that no tenant shall be evicted in any proceeding [***2] pending on May 1, 1950, except after compliance with the regulations and that a proceeding to evict is deemed to be pending if a warrant of eviction had not been executed on on before May 1, 1950, is invalid as an attempt to exercise power not conferred by law. The last paragraph of subdivision 1 of section 12 of the Residential Rent Law provides that the commission may adopt rules necessary or proper for the control of evictions and that it may require that a certificate be obtained from it "prior to the institution of any action or proceeding" for the recovery of possession of housing accommodations. Section 13 of the Residential Rent Law provides as to matters arising upstate that the commission may authorize the transfer to it of any "pending" application or proceeding initiated before the Office of Housing Expediter prior to May 1, 1950. Such sections of the law do not contain an unequivocal declaration of intent on the part of the Legislature to confer upon the Temporary State Housing Rent Commission power to adopt a retroactive regulation impairing vested rights. The power of the commission to require or issue certificates came into being on May 1, 1950, the effective date [***3] of the act. The Legislature confined its grant of power in relation to pending applications and proceedings only to those initiated before the Housing Expediter. The prior proceeding before the Housing Expediter ceased to be "pending" the moment the Expediter issued his certificate. Accordingly, where a landlord obtained from the Housing Expediter a certificate of eviction pursuant to which he commenced, prior to May 1, 1950, summary proceedings to recover possession of an apartment, it is unreasonable to require such landlord to procure a new certificate and thus subject him to the possibility of another waiting period.

 

2863 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Teeval Co. v. Stern


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Municipal Court of the City of New York, Borough of Manhattan


197 Misc. 406; 96 N.Y.S.2d 859; 1950 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1603



May 1, 1950 



DISPOSITION: Motion to dismiss as prayed for is granted solely on the ground that this court rules the statutes are constitutional.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant tenant filed a motion to dismiss plaintiff landlord's complaint for insufficiency. The landlord sought to recover the difference in rent between that allowed by 1950 N.Y. Laws 250 and regulations of a federal office of a housing expediter.


OVERVIEW: The landlord sought to recover an amount of rent owed in excess of the limit established under 1950 N.Y. Laws 250. On review, the court granted the tenant's motion to dismiss the complaint. The court rejected contentions that the state law deprived the landlord of property without due process of law; the law was not in conflict of the Supremacy Clause, U.S. Const. art. VI. The court held that, pursuant to principles expressed in common law, the rights of property and contract must have yielded to public convenience.


OUTCOME: The court granted the tenant's motion to dismiss the complaint.


HEADNOTES

Landlord and tenant -- State Residential Rent Law (L. 1950, ch. 250) constitutional.

Chapter 250 of the Laws of 1950 (the State Residential Rent Law) -- the effect of which is to invalidate an increase in rent allowed landlord by the Federal Housing Expediter on September 27, 1949, and which protects tenant from eviction by reason of failure to pay such increase -- is constitutional. The statute does not deprive landlord of property without due process of law (U. S. Const., 14th Amendt., § 1) and it is not in conflict with the Supremacy Clause of the Federal Constitution (art. VI). Accordingly, a complaint in an action to recover the difference in rent between that allowed by New York law and the increased rent granted by the Federal Housing Expediter is dismissed.

 

2864 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Denihan Enterprises v. O'Dwyer


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


197 Misc. 950; 97 N.Y.S.2d 326; 1950 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1641



April 26, 1950 



DISPOSITION: It follows that the complaint must be dismissed and the injunctive relief requested, denied. The motions are so determined.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff taxpayer instituted an action pursuant to the General Municipal Law § 51 and sought injunctive relief restraining defendant city from performance of the provisions of a contract made with the lessee.


OVERVIEW: An agreement provided that the city would acquire real property for street opening purposes and to effectuate the purposes of General Municipal Law § 72-j. The lessee would erect a parking garage. The taxpayer owned some of the property to be condemned and sought injunctive relief restraining the city from performing the provisions of the agreement, which was denied. The court found that to be constitutional, the land acquired under General Municipal Law § 72-j had to be taken for public purposes. As public necessity required that the multiplicity of parked cars be removed from the city streets, parking and storage garages were necessary. Undertaking the obligation of supplying the necessary garages was within the concept of a public use and the statute was constitutional. The court found that although the statute permitted the sale and leasing of the acquired land to private individuals, the public good was enhanced. The court found that the agreement was in no way at variance with the statute.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the taxpayer's complaint and denied the injunctive relief requested.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- eminent domain -- taking of land for garage to be leased to private corporation as public use -- (1) General Municipal Law ( § 72-j) providing for taking of land by municipality for parking and garage purposes and for maintenance thereof by municipality or private corporation under lease, constitutional; emergency with respect to parking exists and taking of land for such purpose is "public use" -- (2) defendant city made agreement with insurance company for city to condemn land and if city auctioned land for construction thereon of garage, insurance company would bid on stated terms and erect garage; since statute permits leasing of acquired land to private corporations for garage purposes, immaterial that private interest may be benefited since public good enhanced by agreement; city absolutely authorized to make agreement -- (3) degree of alleviation of conditions immaterial -- (4) that specifications in agreement limit prospective bidders to persons with large resources  [***2] no ground for enjoining performance of contract -- (5) agreement provides for creation of park on roof of garage; one half of park to be maintained by city as public park, other one half by insurance company; agreement does not contemplate exclusive use of insurance company's half of park by insurance company -- (6) renting of garage to residents of area and fixation of rents provided for in section 72-j -- (7) agreement and statute contemplate both storage and parking garages.

1. Section 72-j of the General Municipal Law (as amd. by L. 1949, ch. 453) authorizes a municipality to acquire land for the purpose of erecting parking garages or parking areas to be operated by said municipalities or for sale or lease to private individuals or corporations for such purposes. Section 1 of chapter 453 of the Laws of 1949, declares the use to be a "public purpose". Section 72-j is constitutional. While the Legislature has declared that an emergency exists in large cities with reference to parking and garage spaces and while a declaration with respect to public use is not binding on the courts, such a declaration is nevertheless entitled to great respect. The Legislature's pronouncement [***3] conclusively establishes the necessity. In view also of conditions which obtain today, the taking of privately owned land for public parking and garage areas with the resulting alleviation of the traffic problem constitutes a taking for a public use.

2. Defendant municipality entered into a contract with an insurance company requiring the city to condemn certain land to effectuate the purposes of section 72-j of the General Municipal Law. The agreement provides that the insurance company will bid on expressed terms for a lease if the condemned property is offered for public auction by the city. The agreement would require the insurance company to erect a garage thereon. The statute permits the sale and leasing of condemned land to private individuals or corporations for garage and parking purposes. The land is to be acquired by the city in its trust and not in it s proprietary capacity. It may not therefore be used for any other use without specific legislative provision and such legislative provision is contained in section 72-j. If upon completion of the project the public good is enhanced, it does not matter that private interests may be benefited. The authority of defendant [***4] municipality to make an agreement with the insurance company is absolute.

3. The degree of alleviation resulting from the improvement is immaterial so long as conditions are alleviated and then a public purpose is being served.

4. That the specifications contained in the agreement will limit prospective bidders to corporations and individuals with large resources does not warrant enjoining the performance of the contract. From the very nature of the improvement, it is desirable from the city's point of view that the lessee have ample means not only to erect but to maintain such an improvement.

5. The agreement provides for the creation of a park on the roof of the structure, the southern one half to be a public park maintained by the city and the northern part to be maintained by the insurance company. Although no provision is made for the use of the northern portion by the insurance company exclusively, it must be assumed that none is in contemplation since the taking for such use would not be one for the public benefit.

6. The agreement provides for the renting of space in the garage to residents of the area and to transients. Rents for the use of the garage are to be fixed.  [***5] The use by the public of the garage and the establishment of reasonable rents is provided for in section 72-j. The right to fix these rents within the limitation of what appears to be a fair return to the lessee is retained by the city. In computing these rents the return from the commercial space must be considered as part of the fair return to the lessee.

7. Subdivision 4 of section 72-j of the General Municipal Law defines "public garage"; the term elsewhere used in the section is "public parking garage". The definition is sufficiently broad to include not only the term "public garage" but also "public parking garage". Both storage and parking garages were in contemplation of the Legislature and the agreement has in contemplation both uses,giving broad latitude to the lessee with reference to the allocation of each use.

 

2865 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Metropolitan Life Ins. Co. v. New York


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


199 Misc. 181; 98 N.Y.S.2d 442; 1950 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1782



April 20, 1950 



DISPOSITION: The motions are, in all respects, denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant controller issued to plaintiff taxpayer a notice of a determination of a business tax deficiency. The taxpayer paid the tax and filed a written notice of protest. The taxpayer filed a complaint to recover the amount paid under protest and a petition under N.Y. Civ. Prac. Act art. 78 to review the comptroller's determination of the deficiency. The comptroller and the city filed motions to dismiss the complaint and petition.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayer filed the notice of protest within 10 days after the issuance of the deficiency notice. The taxpayer demanded a hearing and a refund. The comptroller denied the claim for a refund, but granted a hearing for the limited purpose of affording the taxpayer an opportunity to establish that it had complied with the statutory requirements for a review of the comptroller's determination. Following the hearing, the original determination was confirmed. A related test case was instituted as the result of an agreement between the City and the insurance companies affected, including the taxpayer. The proceeding resulted in an annulment of the comptroller's determination. The taxpayer filed the instant complaint and petition following that decision. The City and the comptroller conceded that the determination of the tax deficiency was illegal. However, the City contended that the taxpayer was not entitled to a refund because it failed to comply with certain provisions of the New York City Administrative Code. The court held that because the comptroller was without jurisdiction to issue the deficiency assessment, the exclusive remedy provisions of the Code were inapplicable.


OUTCOME: The court denied the motions to dismiss the complaint and the petition.


HEADNOTES

Taxation -- New York City business tax -- (1) comptroller of city of New York determined deficiency in business tax based on insurance company's receipts from annuity contracts; determination of comptroller annulled in test case; present action for declaratory judgment and to recover tax paid under protest proper despite "exclusive remedies" provisions of Administrative Code since comptroller was without jurisdiction -- (2) even if declaratory judgment prohibited by "exclusive remedies" provisions of Administrative Code, letter sent by insurance company to comptroller within statutory thirty-day period sufficiently manifested intention to effect rehearing within "exclusive remedies" provisions.

1. Plaintiff-petitioner, an insurance company, paid under protest a business tax deficiency imposed by the City of New York upon its receipts from annuity contracts for prior years. As a result of a test case the city comptroller's determination that such receipts constituted premiums was annulled. Although the provisions of [***2] the Administrative Code of the City of New York pertaining to the power of the comptroller to determine a tax deficiency provide for an exclusive method for the review of the determination, since the comptroller here was without jurisdiction or authority to issue the deficiency assessment, the "exclusive remedies" provisions of the Administrative Code are inapplicable and plaintiff properly brought this action for declaratory judgment and for money had and received.

2. Even assuming that such actions are prohibited by the "exclusive remedies" provisions of the Administrative Code, a letter sent by plaintiff to defendant constitutes an application for a hearing received by defendant within the statutory period within the Administrative Code (§ RR41-6.0), which is the "exclusive remedies" provision. Such section makes the comptroller's determination final unless the taxpayer shall within thirty days "apply * * * for a hearing". Such section does not prescribe the form in which the application is to be made, whether written or oral, nor does it specify the language to be used. Plaintiff's letter is sufficient if it may reasonably be deemed to be an application or request for a hearing [***3] on the comptroller's determination. The letter is in substance and effect an application for a rehearing. It was not necessary that the letter use the words "hearing requested". Plaintiff manifested its intention to contest its liability for the tax imposed and to apply for a hearing. Accordingly, defendant-respondent's motion to dismiss the complaint in the action and the petition in the proceeding under article 78 of the Civil Practice Act should be denied.

 

2866 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Consolidated Edison Co. v. Moore


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Albany County


197 Misc. 628; 98 N.Y.S.2d 981; 1950 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1866



March 31, 1950 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner company brought proceedings that sought to review the determination of respondent State Board of Equalization and Assessment that fixed the equalization rate applicable to the company's special franchise. The board filed a motion to dismiss portions of the company's petition.


OVERVIEW: The company claimed that 1949 N.Y. Laws 346 was unconstitutional. The expressed purposes to be carried out by the Board of Equalization and Assessment were "special purposes" within N.Y. Const. art. V, § 3 that provided that no new departments shall be created, but the Legislature shall not be prevented from creating "temporary commissions for special purposes" and the board as created to carry out such purposes is a "temporary" commission. Chapter 346 designated the board as a "temporary state commission." The court determined that the board was therefore not a new "department" within the ban of the New York Constitution. The court found that a provision within the chapter that set the jurisdiction, functions, and powers of the board did not violate either N.Y. Const. art. 1, § 3 or N.Y. Const. art. 3, § 16. The court determined that making the state comptroller a member of the board of did not violate N.Y. Const. art. 1, § 5. The court further determined that the company was not denied its due process rights.


OUTCOME: The court granted the board's motion to dismiss portions of the company's petition.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- act creating State Board of Equalization and Assessment -- (1) act creating State Board of Equalization and Assessment (L. 1949, ch. 346) constitutional -- (2) board is not new department banned by Constitution but is "temporary" commission for special purpose -- (3) no unlawful delegation of legislative power in act -- (4) Constitution (art. V, § 1) prohibiting Legislature from assigning to Comptroller administrative duties, excepting such as may be incidental to performance of his assigned functions, not violated by making Comptroller member of board -- (5) petitioner's rights not violated in conduct of hearing on grievance day and by board's determination -- (6) that State Tax Commission made tentative assessment does not entitle petitioner to final determination by such commission.

1. In a proceeding to review a determination of the State Board of Equalization and Assessment fixing an equalization rate applicable to a special franchise of petitioner, a utility company, for the year 1949-1950, petitioner challenges, by means of allegations in its petition, the constitutionality [***2] of chapter 346 of the Laws of 1949 which created said board. Respondents' motion to strike such allegations from the petition is granted. The statute is constitutional.

2. The expressed purposes to be carried out by the Board of Equalization and Assessment are "special purposes" within the Constitution (art. V, § 3) which provides that no new departments shall be created, but the Legislature shall not be prevented from creating "temporary commissions for special purposes" and the board as created to carry out such purposes is a "temporary" commission. The act itself designates the board as a "temporary state commission" and the time limit on its existence to April 1, 1952, stamps it as temporary. The board is therefore not a new "department" within the ban of the Constitution. The act neither abolishes a department nor creates a new one.

3. Section 3 of chapter 346 of the Laws of 1949 transfers to the State Board of Equalization and Assessment "all" the jurisdiction, functions or powers of the State Tax Commission, the State Board of Equalization, and the Department of Taxation and Finance "pertaining to or connected with the assessment and taxation of real estate". No unlawful [***3] delegation of legislative power in violation of section 1 of article III of the State Constitution is thereby made. The functions, powers and duties assigned had long been performed by the three existing agencies and were clearly defined and well delineated. The general description thereof buttressed as it is by the detailed specifications later enumerated in section 3, renders untenable any claim of unlawful delegation of legislative power by reason of ambiguity. Due consideration has been given by the court to petitioner's claims of vagueness and of the unconstitutional incorporation by reference of provisions of existing laws (State Constitution, art IV, § 16) in the last paragraph of section 3 of the act relating to the apportionment of taxes among counties under the certain articles of the Judiciary Law there enumerated. The Legislature was not required to set forth or repeat in the enactment the precise terms of all statutes which conferred the authority being transferred or to revise and amend each in order constitutionally to effect the change.

4. Section 1 of article V of the State Constitution as amended in 1925 provided that the Legislature should define the Comptroller's [***4] powers and duties "but shall assign to him no administrative duties, excepting such as may be incidental to the performance of these functions." In making the State Comptroller a member of the State Board of Equalization and Assessment the act did not violate said section. By that act, the Legislature created a board of three members to be appointed by the Governor and provided that any public officer should be qualified for appointment thereto. It is this board which must carry out the duties and functions prescribed and not the members thereof individually. There is, however, no existing inhibition, constitutionally or otherwise, against one occupying the office of Comptroller from performing other public services or from holding public office not incompatible with that of Comptroller.

5. Petitioner was not denied a hearing to which it was constitutionally entitled on grievance day. A hearing was had. It was conducted by two of the three members of the board. Petitioner submitted evidence in support of its complaint addressed to both valuation and rate of equalization. The board's final determination was made by all its members. Petitioner was not deprived of any rights [***5] by such procedure.

6. The contention of petitioner that its assessment having been made tentatively by the State Tax Commission, not alone on the evidence before it but also upon the personal knowledge, judgment and experience of the members thereof, it was entitled to the benefit of such personal knowledge, judgment and experience of the Tax Commission members at the time of the grievance day hearing, is rejected. Petitioner acquired no vested interest in the knowledge, judgment and experience of the State Tax Commission.

 

2867 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Application of Liberty Country Wear, Inc.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


197 Misc. 581; 96 N.Y.S.2d 134; 1950 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1519



March 29, 1950 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, a Massachusetts corporation, appearing specially, filed a motion to vacate service upon it by registered mail, alleging that the arbitration clause on the reverse side of its agreement with respondent, a New York corporation, did not bind petitioner and that petitioner had not consented to New York jurisdiction. Respondent filed a cross motion to direct arbitration.


OVERVIEW: The arbitration clause in question was on the reverse side of an order form printed by respondent. Just above the signature line on the front of the order was an express statement that the order was subject to the terms on the reverse. The clause specified that the parties agreed to arbitrate any disputes in the State of New York, subject to the arbitration rules of the National Federation of Textiles, Inc. Petitioner alleged that it was unaware of the clause. The court held that the reference was in clear type and that it incorporated in plain language the clauses on the reverse side, thereby committing petitioner to their acceptance. The court also reasoned that the method of service followed the applicable arbitration rules and complied with the service requirements of N.Y. Civ. Prac. Act § 1450. The court rejected petitioner's unsupported claim that § 1450 was unconstitutional, holding that the law merely gave more complete expression to what the parties themselves fairly intended, which was that when petitioner agreed to arbitrate in the State of New York, it consented to jurisdiction of its courts.


OUTCOME: The court denied petitioner's motion to vacate service of the notice of arbitration and granted respondent's cross motion to direct arbitration.


HEADNOTES

Arbitration -- effect of arbitration clause on foreign corporation -- (1) foreign corporation not doing business in State upon whom notice of arbitration served may, under its special appearance to set aside service of notice and staying arbitration, challenge efficacy of arbitration clause -- (2) order form signed by petitioner as buyer had printed clause above signature referring to arbitration clause on reverse side; arbitration clause part of agreement and binding on petitioner -- (3) rules of arbitration body provided for service by registered letter outside State; petitioner's consent to procedure gave service by registered mail outside State, validity -- (4) Civil Practice Act ( § 1450), providing that contract for arbitration in State deemed consent to jurisdiction of Supreme Court to enforce contract, constitutional -- (5) notice of arbitration not vacated; arbitration directed.

1. Petitioner, a foreign corporation not doing business in this State, appears specially to vacate the service upon it by registered letter at its home office of a noticeof [***2] arbitration given by a trade association under an agreement between petitioner and respondent. Petitioner claims that the arbitration clause printed on the reverse side of the agreement is not binding and that in any case it has not consented to the jurisdiction of the New York courts over it. Petitioner in its papers has not specified relief other than the setting aside of the service of the notice and the staying of arbitration under it. Petitioner is not foreclosed from disclaiming an obligation under the arbitration clause. The only basis of jurisdiction over petitioner is the arbitration clause and, if it cannot be given effect, jurisdiction fails. Accordingly, petitioner is entitled to challenge the arbitration clause.

2. Petitioner signed an order on respondent's printed form for the purchase of gabardine from respondent. On the face of the form immediately above petitioner's signature it is stated that the order is given subject to terms on both sides of the order form "which are hereby accepted by the buyer". On the reverse side under the heading in bold faced type "Provisions of Contract Covering Purchase and Sale of Finished Goods", there is contained a clause which [***3] provides that "All controversies arising out of or relating to the contract, or any modification thereof, shall be settled by arbitration in New York in accordance with the Arbitration Rules" of the trade association. Respondent states that the order was the twentieth contract between the parties on the same form. Since the body of the order refers to the clauses on the reverse side and precedes the signature, such clauses are part of the agreement and bind petitioner, whether it knew of the clauses or not.

3. Since the clause provided for arbitration in New York in accordance with the trade association's rules and since the rules provided for service by registered mail without the State, petitioner consented to that procedure and to the form of service and such consent gave the service complete vitality.

4. Section 1450 of the Civil Practice Act by which a contract for arbitration in this State is deemed a consent to the jurisdiction of the Supreme Court to enforce the contract is constitutional. The jurisdiction conferred by the section is not compulsory. Initially it springs from the voluntary act of the nonresident who by written contract has obligated itself to arbitrate [***4] a controversy in this State. When parties agree to arbitrate in a named jurisdiction they submit themselves to any procedure in that jurisdiction necessary to bring the arbitration to an effective conclusion. The agreement then is a consent to the jurisdiction of the courts.

5. Petitioner's motion to vacate the service of the notice of arbitration should be denied and respondent's cross motion to direct arbitration granted.

 

2868 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Lederman v. Board of Education


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Kings County


197 Misc. 183; 96 N.Y.S.2d 461; 1950 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1558



March 6, 1950 



DISPOSITION: Accordingly, the motion to punish for contempt is denied. Submit order.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, a president of a teachers unions, a union local, and others, filed a motion to punish defendants, the Board of Education of the City of New York (New York) and a school superintendent, for contempt for violating a final judgment that enjoined the implementation of the Feinberg Law, 1949 N.Y. Laws ch. 360, which implemented N.Y. Civ. Serv. Laws § 12a(c) and added N.Y. Educ. Law § 3022(2) and N.Y. R. Brd. Regents ch. XV-B, § 254 .


OVERVIEW: Petitioners sought to have respondents held in contempt of court and punished for disobeying a court order that invalidated certain legislation and enjoined its enforcement. Respondents sought to question a teacher as to whether he was associated with the communist party. The teacher stated that such question was generally in violation of his constitutional rights and particularly in violation the prior judgment. Respondents contended that such question was asked pursuant to N.Y. Educ. Law § 2523 rather than the invalidated legislation. The court denied the petition for punishment. The court found that respondents had the power to proceed against disloyal or unfit teachers notwithstanding the invalidity of the Feinberg Law. The invalidation of the Feinberg Law was not to be construed as in any way affecting the undoubted power of the State of New York and its administrative agencies to set up qualifications for school personnel. On the motion for contempt for violation of a prior judgment, the court could not adjudicate whether the question was proper under § 2523, as that issue was not before the court and required an independent adjudication.


OUTCOME: The court denied petitioner's motion to punish for contempt.


HEADNOTES

Schools -- teachers -- inquiry as to fitness of teacher -- (1) motion to punish board of education and superintendent of schools for contempt of court for violation of judgment of court declaring Feinberg Law (L. 1949, ch. 360) unconstitutional based on claim that teacher was questioned as to affiliation with Communist party, denied -- (2) action of superintendent under Education Law (§ 2523) was not subterfuge to circumvent judgment of court; machinery of Feinberg Law not invoked by superintendent -- (3) on motion to punish for contempt, legality of inquiry made by superintendent under Education Law (§ 2523) cannot be determined.

1. A motion to punish a board of education and a superintendent of schools for contempt of court for alleged violation of a final judgment declaring the Feinberg Law (L. 1949, ch. 360) unconstitutional is denied. The claimed violation is that after the entry of judgment,  [***2] the superintendent of schools commenced an inquiry of a teacher and asked of that teacher "Are you now or were you ever a member of the Communist party?" Movants have not sustained their burden of showing clearly that the specific provisions of the judgment of the court were disobeyed.

2. The superintendent of schools acted under section 2523 of the Education Law which provides that persons employed in teaching in the schools "shall hold their respective positions during good behavior and efficient and competent service." There was no subterfuge to circumvent the mandate of the court on the part of the superintendent in acting under section 2523. In any event, it cannot be held that the superintendent was acting under the invalidated Feinberg Law. The board of regents has not issued a list of organizations found to be subversive. No hearings have been held, nor has any other action been taken as required by the Feinberg Law. The machinery of that law has not been invoked and concededly the present inquiry by the superintendent was instituted independently of the Feinberg Law and under the general provisions of the Education Law.

3. On this motion to punish for contempt, the legality [***3] of the inquiry made by the superintendent under section 2523 of the Education Law cannot be determined since it is beyond the scope of this application. The superintendent's right to ask such questions under the Education Law must be the subject of an independent adjudication.

 

2869 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Broome County v. Bates


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Albany County


197 Misc. 88; 95 N.Y.S.2d 248; 1950 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1398



February 15, 1950 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff County of Broome (New York) instituted an action for a judgment declaring an amendment to the New York Judiciary Law, provided for by 1949 N.Y. Laws 814, unconstitutional and invalid. Defendant members of the State Tax Commission (New York) filed a motion for judgment on the pleadings.


OVERVIEW: The amendment added N.Y. Jud. Law §§ 144-a, 144-b, which provided for additional compensation to supreme court justices of the third and fourth departments and also provided for the apportionment of the additional compensation among several counties. The court noted that there was no provision in the New York Constitution that specifically fixed the salaries of supreme court justices nor were supreme court justices specifically named in the New York Constitution as state officers. The court determined that the restrictions imposed by N.Y. Const. art. XIII, § 12 did not control the compensation paid to supreme court justices and that N.Y. Const. art. VI, § 19 was the controlling mandate. The court further determined that the matter of compensation for justices of the supreme court was left for the legislature, which was curtailed only by the restriction in N.Y. Const. art. VI, § 19 that the compensation of justices could not be reduced during tenure of office. The court found that there was no violation of any constitutional provisions sufficiently established to warrant an adjudication that the legislation under review was unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court granted the motion for judgment on the pleadings and dismissed the complaint.


HEADNOTES

Judges -- compensation -- (1) Judiciary Law (§§ 144-a, 144-b) provides that each justice of the Supreme Court in third and fourth departments shall receive additional compensation equal to one-half amount of additional compensation received by justices of the Supreme Court resident in second and ninth judicial districts, to come from each county in said third and fourth departments; sections constitutional -- (2) State Constitution (art. XIII, § 12) prohibiting increase in compensation of State constitutional officers during terms of office inapplicable to Supreme Court justices -- (3) Constitution (art. VI, § 19) does not fix salary of judges but restricts Legislature from reducing their salaries during office; action of Legislature (Judiciary Law, § 144) in permitting City of New York to pay justices resident therein more than $ 18,000 paid by State, constitutional -- (4) Legislature granted $ 10,000 additional compensation to justices resident in ninth and tenth judicial districts in second department and imposed upon counties in said districts outside city of New York duty to pay judges resident in such counties such  [***2] additional compensation (Judiciary Law, §§ 145, 146); proper therefore for Legislature to require counties in third and fourth departments to pay their justices one-half amount of additional compensation paid to justices in first and second judicial departments -- (5) legislation not improper delegation to board of estimate of city of New York of power to fix salaries of justices outside city limits.

1. Chapter 814 of the Laws of 1949 added sections 144-a and 144-b to the Judiciary Law, which sections provide that each of the justices of the Supreme Court in the third and fourth judicial departments shall receive additional compensation equal to one-half the amount of the additional compensation received by each of the justices of the Supreme Court resident in the second and ninth judicial districts and that the State Department of Taxation and Finance shall pay the additional compensation in the first place and shall be reimbursed the amount thereof by counties in the third and fourth judicial departments. The statute is constitutional.

2. Section 12 of article XIII of the Constitution which prohibits the increase of the salary of a constitutional State officer during his term [***3] of office is inapplicable to compensation paid to Supreme Court justices, for such section applies to those officers specifically named in the Constitution as State officers and Supreme Court justices are not within such category.

3. Section 19 of article VI of the State Constitution is the controlling section. It does not fix a salary and restricts the Legislature from reducing the compensation of justices during their terms of office. Accordingly, when the Legislature (Judiciary Law, § 144) allowed the City of New York to pay additional compensation to justices of the Supreme Court who are resident of the city, such legislation contemplated that said justices may receive more than the $ 18,000 paid by the State of New York. Such legislation has never been regarded as unconstitutional.

4. Within the second judicial department are contained the ninth and tenth judicial districts and with respect to the justices in said districts residing without the city of New York, the Legislature (Judiciary Law, §§ 145, 146) has given salaries equal to those paid to the justices residing in Kings County, New York City. It was therefore not improper for the Legislature to grant additional [***4] compensation to the justices of the third and fourth departments. That the judges in the third and fourth departments have been allowed only one half the extra compensation is a matter within the Legislature's discretion and cannot be superseded by judicial pronouncement.

5. Such legislation (Judiciary Law, §§ 144-a, 144-b) does not empower the board of estimate of the city of New York to act beyond its existing powers, but recognizes that the board may act within the limits of the city. It takes cognizance of the fact that the exercise of the power by the city creates a situation that calls for legislative intervention as to matters beyond the jurisdiction of the board of estimate. Such legislation is proper. The fear of plaintiff, a county in the third judicial department, that its taxpayers will be thrust into bankruptcy because of generosity of the board of estimate of the city of New York toward the justices resident therein is without real foundation. For more than twenty years such board has not granted the justices any increase in compensation.

 

2870 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Weiss v. Beckmann


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Nassau County


197 Misc. 414; 96 N.Y.S.2d 66; 1950 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1513



February 7, 1950 



DISPOSITION: Motion is in all respects denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff business partners filed an action against defendant Commissioner of Police, Nassau County (New York), seeking to restrain the County's police department from enforcing the provisions of N.Y. Penal Law § 2147, a Sunday closing law. The partners filed a motion for a temporary restraining order. They asserted that § 2147 was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The partners were non-Christians; they did not observe Sunday as their day of Sabbath. They argued that § 2147 was unconstitutional as applied to them because it interfered with their freedom to practice their religion. The court disagreed. It noted that the constitutionality of § 2147 had previously been upheld. The freedom of religion argument had been considered by another court and rejected. New York's Sunday closing law did not interfere with the religious liberty of any person, but was a restraint upon civil liberty within the State of New York's police power. It was constitutionally valid because it advanced the physical and moral well-being of the people of New York by prescribing a day of general rest from labor. The court could not exercise its equitable jurisdiction to restrain police from enforcing a criminal statute unless a clear legal right to relief was established. The partners had not met this burden of proof.


OUTCOME: The court denied the partners' motion for injunctive relief.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- injunctions -- Sunday closing of business places -- since no clear legal right present, court of equity may not exercise its jurisdiction to restrain police department from enforcing section 2147 of Penal Law which deals with Sunday closing of business places.

Plaintiffs, who are of the Hebrew faith, seek to restrain the Police Department of Nassau County from enforcing the provisions of section 2147 of the Penal Law which deals with the Sunday closing of business places. Plaintiffs assert that the section is unconstitutional. Since a clear legal right is not presented, a court of equity may not exercise its jurisdiction to restrain the police from enforcing a criminal statute.

 

2871 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Wald v. Lenox Ave. & 140th Street Corp.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Municipal Court of the City of New York, Borough of Manhattan


197 Misc. 773; 94 N.Y.S.2d 793; 1950 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1370



January 25, 1950 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff attorneys brought an action to recover from defendant clients for legal services rendered and on an account stated. The clients asserted that a change in the law rendered the contract "frustrated" and that they should not be obligated to pay for the services received.


OVERVIEW: A firm of attorneys was hired by the clients to obtain from the Office of Housing Expediter the certificates needed to authorize an increase in the rents due from the clients' tenants. The attorneys procured the desired certificates but were not paid by the clients. The clients asserted that rents still could not be raised because of the recent enactment of the Sharkey Law, New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code § U41-7.0(c) (1949), which required an additional certificate from a temporary commission. The court found that the attorneys were entitled to judgment, ruling that they had completed performance under the contract. The court distinguished each case of "impossibility of performance" recited by the clients, noting that the contract was not impossible to perform merely because the clients now found the result less useful. The court found no conditions precedent or subsequent in the contract that would prevent payment to the attorneys.


OUTCOME: The court found in favor of the attorneys on their cause of action for the amount demanded from their clients for legal services rendered, dismissing the second cause of action for judgment on an account.


HEADNOTES

Landlord and tenant -- frustration of purpose of contract -- attorneys retained by owners of apartment houses to obtain certificates granting increases in rent from Federal Office of Housing Expediter; agreement required payment for services to be made upon receipt of orders from Housing Expediter increasing rentals; plaintiffs' efforts resulted in issuance of such certificates; New York City Local Laws had effect of requiring landlord to obtain additional certificate from temporary city rent commission before increased rents could take effect; local law not such intervening act of Government as to invalidate purpose of agreement; plaintiffs entitled to judgment based on contract.

Defendants owners of apartment houses retained plaintiffs, a firm of attorneys, one of whom by previous experience was especially qualified in the field, to obtain an increase of rent from the Federal Office of Housing Expediter and agreed to pay them for their services. The agreement then provided that "Payment of the minimum fee is not contingent upon success and is to be made upon the execution of this retainer; and the balance upon receipt of orders [***2] from the Housing Expediter increasing such rentals in said premises". Through the efforts of plaintiffs, the Office of Housing Expediter issued certificates authorizing defendants to increase rents retroactively to February 14, 1949. The Sharkey Law (Local Laws, 1949, No. 73 of City of New York), as validated by chapter 1 of the Laws of 1950, had the effect that, whereas at the time of the entry into the agreement only the certificate of the Office of Housing Expediter was necessary to enable the landlord to bring a proceeding to remove a tenant for nonpayment of increased rentals or to bring an action for such increased rentals, now the certificate of the New York City temporary city rent commission was necessary as well. The enactment of the Sharkey Law was not such a frustration of the underlying purpose of the agreement that plaintiffs are not entitled to their fee for the performance by them of their portion of the agreement. In this agreement, its essence did not depend on the happening of any event. Payment was to be made to plaintiffs on "receipt of orders from the Housing Expediter increasing such rentals in said premises", and plaintiffs were engaged "to obtain an increase [***3] of rent from the Office of Housing Expediter". Even assuming that the Sharkey Law was an intervening act of Government, it could not have been anticipated by defendants that it would make the procurement of an Office of Housing Expediter certificate of increase of rent illegal; it did not prohibit employment of counsel to facilitate issuance of such certificate; it did not prohibit defendants from accepting such services and paying for same and finally it did not render the certificate of the Office of Housing Expediter valueless. The facts that one of plaintiffs was especially qualified to prosecute the applications in question and the clarity and conciseness of the language of the agreement result in a finding that plaintiffs were retained to facilitate the procurement of such certificates and without implied condition whatsoever. Plaintiffs are entitled to judgment based on their contract.

 

2872 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Durand Realty Co. v. Stolman


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Trial Term, New York County


197 Misc. 208; 94 N.Y.S.2d 358; 1949 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3082



December 21, 1949 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, owners and lessees of certain premises, brought an action to recover damages from defendant, individually and under his business name, contractors, pursuant to N.Y. Lien Law § 39-a (1930), after the contractors had been unsuccessful in foreclosing a mechanics' lien.


OVERVIEW: The contractors were mechanics, lienors, having performed work and furnished materials pursuant to a contract made with the lessees of the premises. The contractors sought to foreclose their mechanics' lien. The referee decided that the contractors had willfully exaggerated the amount of their lien, and, pursuant to the provisions of N.Y. Lien Law § 39, he declared the lien void and cancelled it. Plaintiffs then brought the action to recover the damages allowed by N.Y. Lien Law § 39-a (1930), which added an additional protection to the owner from fictitious or fraudulent liens. Under § 39-a, plaintiffs could collect an amount equal to the difference by which the amount claimed to be due exceeded the amount actually due. The court disagreed with plaintiffs, who had argued that since the entire debt was cancelled, they were entitled to recover the full amount claimed in the notice of lien. Damages were limited to the amount by which the lien was willfully exaggerated. Because plaintiffs failed to establish the amount of the contractors' exaggeration, the claim was disallowed, but plaintiffs were entitled to reasonable attorney's fees.


OUTCOME: The court determined the reasonable attorney's fees each plaintiff was entitled to receive and entered judgments in those amounts, with taxable costs.


HEADNOTES

Liens -- damages for willful exaggeration -- (1) action under Lien Law (§ 39-a) to recover damage based on lienors' willful exaggeration of amount of lien; plaintiffs, owner and lessees, respectively, not entitled to full amount claimed in notice of lien but only amount by which lien is willfully exaggerated -- (2) since in action to foreclose lien, present plaintiffs did not establish difference between amount claimed in notice of lien and amount actually due, they are not entitled to damages for such difference; difference cannot be established in this action -- (3) plaintiffs entitled to award for attorney's fees paid in foreclosure action.

1. Section 39-a of the Lien Law allows damages to be recovered by an owner or contractor with respect to whose property a lienor has willfully exaggerated the amount of his lien and provides that the damages he shall be entitled to recover shall include "an amount equal to the difference by which the amount claimed to be due or to become due as stated in the notice of lien exceeded the amount actually due or to become due thereon". Defendants, mechanic-lienors, had [***2] filed liens against premises of which plaintiffs were the owner and lessees, respectively. In an action to foreclose the lien it was determined that said defendants had willfully exaggerated the amount of their lien and that the lien was void (Lien Law, § 39). In the present action to recover damages, plaintiffs are not entitled to recover the full amount claimed in the notice of lien regardless of whether or not the lien included some amounts which would support a recovery except for the willful exaggeration. Damages are limited to the amount by which the lien is willfully exaggerated.

2. Since in the foreclosure action present plaintiffs did not obtain an adjudication of the difference between the amount claimed and the amount actually due, they are not entitled to any damages in that respect. Such difference cannot be determined in this action.

3. Plaintiffs are entitled to the amount paid as attorney's fees in the foreclosure action which defendants concede to have been reasonable.

 

2873 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Philip Lewis Egg Products


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


City Magistrate's Court of New York, Borough of Manhattan, Municipal Term


197 Misc. 212; 93 N.Y.S.2d 453; 1949 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2964



December 19, 1949 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant corporation filed a motion to dismiss a complaint filed by the State charging it with a violation of New York City, N.Y., Sanitary Code § 148, reg. 27 relating to food unfit for human consumption. The motion was based on the ground that the State had failed to establish its guilt beyond a reasonable doubt.


OVERVIEW: A health inspector visited the egg-breaking establishment and found in the cooler 90 dozen check eggs. Upon candling, he found that 60 dozen were bad. There was evidence of use and nothing to indicate they were candled. The corporation's president, who was the candler, told him that the eggs had been delivered days earlier after he had finished for the day and that they were to be candled on the "next regular candling day," which was when the inspector came. The cooler temperature was lower than required by law. It was contended that the New York City, N.Y., Sanitary Code § 148, reg. 27 was unconstitutional because it contravened a state regulation, N.Y. Agric. & Mkts. Law § 160-b, official order 106 (1936), which permitted the sale of uncandled eggs under certain conditions. Further, mere possession of bad food was not a violation without proof of sale and no proof the food was bad existed. The court denied the motion, finding no inconsistency between the regulations. The municipality had a right to enact additional protective provisions to meet special conditions. There was a violation, which was not excused by lack of guilty intent or by mere good faith by the corporation.


OUTCOME: The court denied the motion to dismiss the complaint.


HEADNOTES

Food -- possession or sale of food unfit for consumption -- (1) New York City inspector found in defendant's egg-breaking establishment eggs which had been set aside for candling and which proved to be bad; defendant charged with violation of regulation issued under New York City Sanitary Cod e providing that food becoming unfit must be kept apart "properly denatured, marked 'Condemned' and removed daily"; motion to dismiss complaint denied; presence on premises of such eggs for five days constitutes clear violation of regulation -- (2) fact that bill accompanying eggs bore notice under State law permitting sale as uncandled eggs no defense in view of regulation adopted by city; municipality may enact additional protective provisions to meet special or peculiar conditions -- (3) intent to sell not essential since mere possession of impure food is violation under regulation -- (4) claim that regulation not violated until eggs found bad by candling rejected; otherwise dealer might readily excuse possession of impure food.

 [***2] 1. When an inspector of the New York City Department of Health visited defendant's egg-breaking establishment on August 9, 1949, he found three cases of eggs, two thirds of which upon candling proved to be bad, and he was informed that the eggs had been delivered on August 4th after candling for the day had been finished, and had been put in the cooler until the next regular candling day which happened to be August 9th. Defendant was charged with violation of regulation 27 of the General Regulations for the Conduct of Food Establishments, issued under section 148 of the Sanitary Code of the City of New York, providing that "Food which has become unfit for human consumption shall be kept separate and apart from other foodstuffs which are held, kept, and offered for sale properly denatured, marked 'Condemned' and removed daily." Motion to dismiss the complaint on the ground of failure to prove guilt beyond a reasonable doubt is denied. The presence on the premises of the eggs for five days in the face of the regulation for denature, marking and daily removal of such eggs constitutes a clear violation of regulation 27.

2. While a bill accompanying the eggs bore the notice required [***3] under the Agriculture and Markets Law to permit their sale as uncandled eggs, there is no inconsistency between that law authorizing such sale and the specific requirement of the Sanitary Code that unwholesome foodstuffs be denatured, "marked 'Condemned'" and removed daily from the food-handling establishments. A municipality has a right to enact additional protective provisions to meet special or peculiar conditions, and a city of eight million such as New York City operates under such "special conditions" as will justify the ordinance in question.

3. Defendant's further contention that mere possession of impure food is not a violation of the law but that it is necessary to prove an intent to sell, is without merit. The language of the regulation is quite specific and its directions are unmistakable. There is no need to go beyond the regulation itself to determine whether there is compliance with its requirements in any particular case.

4. The argument that since the only way that the existence of bad eggs may be determined is by candling, there is no responsibility on defendant's part until the candling is done, is also without merit. If this view were to prevail, it would [***4] be very easy for an individual violator to allow impure food to remain on his premises for several days and then try to excuse himself, if he should be visited by an inspector during that period by pleading lack of time to inspect or adherence to a schedule providing for inspection at infrequent intervals.

 

2874 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Lederman v. Board of Education


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Kings County


196 Misc. 873; 95 N.Y.S.2d 114; 1949 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3158



December 14, 1949 



DISPOSITION: Judgment upon the pleadings is granted to plaintiffs to the extent indicated. Submit orders and judgment accordingly.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, a group of teachers, taxpayers, and others, applied for a temporary injunction and judgment on the pleadings in a declaratory judgment action alleging that, inter alia, N.Y. Civ. Serv. Law § 12-a(c), the Feinberg Law, 1949 N.Y. Laws ch. 360, and rules promulgated thereunder, violated due process, free speech, and freedom of association. Intervenor challengers applied for leave to intervene as parties plaintiff.


OVERVIEW: The Feinberg Law was an administrative statute that implemented prior laws, including N.Y. Civ. Serv. Law § 12-a, and it required that a list of subversive organizations be promulgated. While § 12-a(a) and (b) disqualified teachers for personal advocacy of the violent overthrow of the government, § 12-a(c) disqualified teachers for mere membership in a listed subversive organization. In the challenge to these provisions, the court ruled that there was no justiciable controversy as to plaintiff teachers because the list had not yet been promulgated and no teacher had been accused of membership. The taxpayers did have standing, however, under N.Y. Gen. Mun. Law § 51, to prevent waste of public funds. In striking down the laws and regulations, the court ruled that teachers had a basic right of free speech, and due process did not permit them to be barred from public employment by conviction without judicial trial. Proceedings under the laws and regulations, as well as N.Y. Educ. Law §§ 310 and 2523, not only allowed nonjudicial conviction, but they required a finding of personal guilt by association, in violation of due process.


OUTCOME: The motion to intervene was denied, while the motion for a temporary injunction was not passed upon. Judgment upon the pleadings was entered declaring that the challenged provisions were unconstitutional.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- public schools -- teachers and other school employees -- subversive activities -- (1) teachers who have not been accused of being members of subversive organization, lists of which are required under Feinberg Law to be established by Board of Regents, may not bring action for judgment declaring Feinberg Law unconstitutional -- (2) General Municipal Law (§ 51) authorizes taxpayers to bring action -- (3) Feinberg Law requires Board of Regents to promulgate list of subversive organizations; at hearing against accused teacher because of his membership in organization deemed subversive, statute places burden upon teacher of showing innocence through "good faith"; no reasonable connection exists between fact presumed and fact supposedly "proved"; statute unconstitutional -- (4) statute ambiguous as to whether present membership is proscribed; though regents' rules interpret it as forbidding only present membership, rules create a presumption of continuance of past membership  [***2] in absence of showing that membership has been terminated "in good faith"; rule unconstitutional as ex post facto legislation -- (5) procedure under Feinberg Law amounts to conviction and punishment without judicial trial -- (6) Feinberg Law and Civil Service Law (§ 12-a, subd. [c]) embody doctrine of personal guilt by association which is unconstitutional -- (7) procedures in Feinberg Law and Civil Service Law (§ 12-a, subd. [c]) violate due process clause of United States Constitution (14th Amendt.).

1. In an action for a judgment declaring unconstitutional (a) the so-called Feinberg Law (L. 1949, ch. 360), (b) section 12-a of the Civil Service Law, as implemented by the Feinberg Law, and (c) the rules of the Board of Regents and the memorandum of the State Commissioner of Education promulgated thereunder, teachers who have not been accused of being members of a subversive organization listed as provided by the Feinberg Law have no right to maintain the action since there is no justiciable controversy until the law has first been violated.

2. As to taxpayers, since an elaborate system of investigation will have to be set up which will require personnel, material and supplies [***3] paid for from public funds, section 51 of the General Municipal Law authorizes them to bring this action. Since it is important to the public at large and the school system in particular that the issue be speedily determined, the court should not pause to consider whether the question is presented in appropriate proceedings.

3. The Feinberg Law provides that the Board of Regents shall promulgate a list of "subversive" organizations after inquiry and "such notice and hearing as may be appropriate". Should a teacher be charged with membership in a listed organization and a hearing held to dismiss such teacher, the organization is deemed to be subversive within the definition of section 12-a of the Civil Service Law, even though the finding was by an administrative body and as to the accused teacher the supporting evidence was hearsay and he had no opportunity to meet it. Under such circumstances there is no reasonable connection between the fact supposedly "proved" and the fact presumed. A presumption which establishes the major portion of a case against an accused and casts upon him the burden of disproving the charges against him is unconstitutional.

4. Moreover, the statute [***4] is ambiguous as to whether past as well as present membership in such organization is proscribed. Though the regents' rules interpret it as forbidding only present membership, the rules create a presumption of continuance of past membership "in the absence of a showing that such membership has been terminated in good faith". This rule well may be invalid under the constitutional ban on ex post facto legislation.

5. The law is unconstitutional in that the procedure under the Feinberg Law might be tantamount to conviction and punishment without judicial trial. A teacher discharged under the Feinberg Law who appeals to the Commissioner of Education loses his right to recourse to the courts; if he starts a proceeding under article 78 of the Civil Practice Act, the court's review (on the record alone) is inadequate, if not illusory; if he goes into court under subdivision (d) of section 12-a of the Civil Service Law he probably will be confronted with a court ruling that his proper remedy was under section 310 or section 2523 of the Education Law -- and this at a time when in all likelihood, the Statute of Limitations already may have run on these remedies.

6. An analysis of the Feinberg [***5] Law and of subdivision (c) of section 12-a of the Civil Service Law, as implemented, discloses that the statutes embody the doctrine of personal guilt by association which is unconstitutional. A person's nonguilt would be no defense under said statutes where the charge would be membership in a subversive organization.

7. The procedures set forth in the Feinberg Law and in subdivision (c) of section 12-a of the Civil Service Law, as implemented by the Feinberg Law, do not add up to those fundamental requirements of fairness which are the essence of due process (U. S. Const., 14th Amendt.) and therefore the statutes are unconstitutional. This determination in no way impairs the power of the Board of Regents under the other adequate provisions of existing law, to promulgate and enforce reasonable rules and regulations designed to rid the school system of teachers found to be unfit.

 

2875 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Roosevelt Field v. North Hempstead


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Nassau County


197 Misc. 621; 95 N.Y.S.2d 126; 1949 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3159



December 5, 1949 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, a town, members of the board of appeals, a water district, and its commissioners, filed two motions to dismiss plaintiff airport owner's complaint for a declaratory judgment regarding the construction of a water tower on the water district's property, pursuant to N.Y. R. Civ. Prac. 106(4), (5).


OVERVIEW: The airport owner's property was south of the water district's property and abutted the southern boundary of the town. The airport owner's complaint purported to state two causes of action and sought a declaratory judgment. The first cause challenged, on constitutional grounds, certain provisions of the town's zoning ordinance and the action of the board of appeals in granting the water district's application for the erection on its property of a water tower. The second cause challenged the jurisdiction of the board of appeals in acting directly upon an original application rather than on an appeal from the denial of an application. The airport owner's contention that the ordinance was subject to constitutional attack could not be sustained because of the power conferred upon the board of appeals under N.Y. Town Law § 267. However, the board of appeal's action was void for lack of jurisdiction because it acted upon an original application and not on an appeal from the denial of permission by the town building official. Where a public body acted entirely without jurisdiction, the rule against collateral attack did not apply. The remedy of a declaratory judgment was appropriate.


OUTCOME: The motions to dismiss were denied.


HEADNOTES
