Municipal corporations -- zoning -- (1) action for judgment declaring that zoning ordinance unconstitutional and that action of town board of appeals in granting application of water district to erect water tower on its property adjacent to plaintiffs, invalid; that another action pending between same parties for same cause in United States District Court not bar here -- (2) since plaintiffs attack validity of provisions of ordinance and jurisdiction of board of appeals in acting upon original application for variance rather than on appeal from denial of owner's application to board officials, remedy by declaratory judgment proper; determination of board of appeals acting upon original application not upon appeal (Town Law, § 267) void -- (3) second cause of action challenging action of board on ground that determination was arbitrary, insufficient; matter reviewable solely by certiorari -- (4) zoning ordinance not subject to constitutional attack since provisions circumscribed zoning board from making arbitrary determinations -- (5) since one cause of action sufficient, motions to dismiss addressed to two causes denied.

 [***2] 1. In an action for a judgment declaring that a zoning ordinance of defendant town is unconstitutional and that the action of the town's board of appeals in granting the application of defendant water district for the erection of a water tower on its property located north of plaintiffs' property is invalid, defendants have moved to dismiss the complaint for insufficiency. The fact that there is another action pending between the same parties for the same cause in a United States District Court does not bar the prosecution of the instant action. Such objection does not relate to actions pending in the courts of other States or in the Federal courts. Moreover, the fact of the pendency of another action does not appear on the face of the complaint.

2. The ordinance limits the height of any structure in an industrial A district to 35 feet and in an industrial B district to 60 feet. The water tower planned to be erected by defendant water district in an industrial district of the height of 168 feet, 6 inches, could not be permitted unless in the exercise of discretion the board of appeals having jurisdiction and acting under a valid ordinance has granted a variance. If a variance [***3] were thus granted, the exclusive manner of reviewing the determination of the board would be by certiorari under section 267 of the Town Law. Nevertheless, since plaintiffs are attacking the validity of certain provisions of the ordinance, pursuant to which the board of appeals purported to grant a variance and attacking the jurisdiction of the board of appeals in acting directly upon an original application rather than on an appeal from the denial of the owner's application to the boards' officials, this action for declaratory judgment lies. The determination of the board of appeals, acting upon an original application and not on an appeal from denial of permission by the town building official, is void for lack of jurisdiction. Section 267 of the Town Law grants to the board power to vary only "in passing upon appeals". No original jurisdiction in such cases is given by the statute or by the ordinance. Where a public body acts entirely without jurisdiction and in the absence of questions of fact the rule against collateral attack does not apply and even though certiorari were available, the remedy of declaratory judgment is appropriate.

3. A second cause of action in plaintiff's [***4] complaint which challenges the determination of the board of appeals on the ground that there were no facts before the board showing unnecessary hardship and practical difficulty and that there were no findings by the board -- rendering its determination arbitrary and capricious -- is insufficient since the matters alleged are matters reviewable by the exclusive remedy of certiorari.

4. Provisions of the zoning ordinance enable the board of appeals to vary the application of the ordinance in harmony with its general purpose and intent in cases of practical difficulty or unnecessary hardship, provided public health, safety and general welfare are secured. These provisions sufficiently circumscribe the zoning board from making arbitrary determinations. Accordingly the ordinance is not subject to constitutional attack because of failure to provide standards for the guidance of the board of appeals.

5. Since the motions to dismiss are addressed to the entire complaint rather than to each of the two causes of action and since there is one sufficient cause of action, the motions must be denied.

 

2876 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Hetenyi


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Monroe County


36 Misc. 2d 518; 235 N.Y.S.2d 164; 1949 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1643



December 3, 1949 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a challenge under N.Y. Code of Crim. Proc. § 362 to the panel of trial jurors that had been drawn for his trial for the crime of murder in the first degree.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was indicted for first degree murder. He challenged the panel of jurors that was drawn for his trial on the ground that it violated N.Y. Code of Crim. Proc. § 362, which governed the drawing of names from the jury wheel and the return of the panel on the part of the Sheriff. The court disallowed the challenge because a successful challenge to a panel of trial jurors drawn for the trial of the case could not be sustained because of alleged irregularities incident to the general selection of jurors. The court found that the method used to select the initial names was not a material departure to the prejudice of defendant within the meaning of § 362. The court declined to pass on defendant's claim that the panel was not a representative cross-section or that the panel was a blue ribbon panel.


OUTCOME: The court disallowed defendant's challenge to the panel of jurors chosen in his murder case.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Jury -- challenge to panel -- defendant's challenge to jury panel called to try him on indictment for murder was disallowed -- defendant failed to substantiate claim that drawing and return of panel was conducted in manner as to constitute material departure from forms prescribed by Judiciary Law; complaint as to selection of jury panel not encompassed by section 362 of Code of Criminal Procedure -- even if section 362 applied to selection, defendant failed to show material departure to prejudice of defendant.

1. Defendant's challenge to the panel of trial jurors drawn to try him upon an indictment for murder in the first degree was disallowed.

2. Defendant failed to substantiate his claim, made under section 362 of the Code of Criminal Procedure, that the drawing and return of the panel was conducted in such a manner as to constitute a material departure, to the prejudice of defendant, from the forms prescribed by the Judiciary Law. The testimony centered upon the method pursued by the Commissioner of Jurors in selecting the 10,246 registrants upon the jury list for jury service. The word "drawing" in section 362 pertained to the actual drawing of the names of eligible [***2] jurors from the so-called jury wheel. The words "return of the jury" had reference to the procedure followed by the Sheriff in summoning into court the jurors drawn. The word "selection" was not used in section 362 and, therefore, no successful challenge to a panel could be sustained because of irregularities incident to the general selection of jurors on the part of the Commissioner of Jurors.

3. Even if section 362 applied to the method of selecting jurors, defendant produced no evidence of a material departure to the prejudice of defendant.

 

2877 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Gordon v. Hempstead


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Nassau County


196 Misc. 954; 93 N.Y.S.2d 250; 1949 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2950



December 2, 1949 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff challenger applied for an injunction to restrain defendant applicant from constructing and maintaining a public parking place under a special exception permit issued by defendant building inspector for defendant zoning board of appeals, pursuant to Hempstead, N.Y., Building Zone Ordinance art. XIV, § G-11.0, prior to a 1949 amendment.


OVERVIEW: The pre-amendment ordinance allowed the zoning board to grant special exceptions to a general prohibition on public parking. While the application for a special permit was pending, the ordinance was amended to provide that only the town board had authority to grant special exceptions. The applicant was awarded a permit the day after the amended ordinance was adopted. The court ruled that, although the permit was issued after adoption of the amendment, the zoning board still had jurisdiction because the amendment did not take effect until later. Under N.Y. Town Law §§ 133 and 269, publication and posting for 10 days was required before an ordinance became effective. However, the pre-amendment ordinance was unconstitutional and void because it contained no standards, rules, or regulations to limit the discretionary power given to the zoning board. Thus, the permit issued under that ordinance was null and void.


OUTCOME: The court entered a judgment declaring that, prior to its amendment, the ordinance under which the special permit was granted was unconstitutional, and the court enjoined and prohibited the applicant from performing any act under that permit.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- zoning ordinance -- constitutionality of variance made by board of zoning appeals in its discretion -- (1) town zoning ordinance forbade public parking place "except as a special exception" by board of appeals; permit therefor granted by board of appeals on July 27, 1949; on July 26th, ordinance amended to require permit from town board rather than board of appeals; judgment granted plaintiff in action to restrain construction of parking place under permit and to declare said zoning ordinance and determination of board of appeals void -- (2) jurisdiction of board of appeals to issue permit in question unaffected by amendment of ordinance since latter ineffective until published as required (Town Law, § 269, third paragraph) -- (3) said third paragraph not restricted in application by remainder of section dealing with "Conflict with other laws"; board of appeals had jurisdiction to issue permit if ordinance constitutional -- (4) ordinance authorizing permit unconstitutional for lack of standards limiting exercise of power delegated to board of appeals -- (5) deleting unconstitutional  [***2] part, ordinance would read "No public parking place shall be constructed in any district"; town still has authority to provide parking places under amendment giving such authority to town board -- (6) even if ordinance deemed void in entirety, same result follows since public parking place never was permitted in district; in absence of proof of need for such places, such construction not unreasonable.

1. Under a building zone ordinance of a town which provides that "no public parking place shall be conducted in any district except as a special exception by the board of appeals", defendant realty corporation applied for a temporary permit to maintain a public parking place on its property located in a "Business" district in the town and after a public hearing, the application was granted by the board of appeals on July 27, 1949. One day prior to that date the town board, following a public hearing, adopted an amendment to the section in question so as to provide that the special exceptionformerly granted by the board of appeals should be granted only by the town board. The amendment was posted by the town clerk and subsequently published in a newspaper. Judgment is directed for [***3] plaintiff in this action to restrain the town and other defendants from constructing and conducting a parking place pursuant to such permit and also for a declaration that the provisions of the zoning ordinance and the determination of said board of appeals are void.

2. The jurisdiction of the board of appeals to issue the permit was not affected by the amendment of the section which gave them power to issue same, for, while the amending ordinance was -- as provided therein -- to become effective immediately on July 26th, both the original ordinance and any amendment thereto, under the terms of the third paragraph of section 269 of the Town Law, must be published twice and posted for ten days before they can become effective.

3. While plaintiff contends that the third paragraph of section 269 has no application here and applies only where there may be some conflict of law between the building zone ordinance and any other statute or ordinance, it is clear that said paragraph has no relation to the other portions of that section dealing with "Conflict with other laws" and was made a portion of said section by mistake of the codifiers. Consequently, the amendment passed on July 26th [***4] did not become effective until August 12th and the board of appeals had jurisdiction to issue the permit if the law granting such authority were constitutional.

4. However, that ordinance was unconstitutional and void because no standards, rules or regulations were adopted to limit the exercise of the discretionary power given therein to the board of appeals.

5. Only that portion of the section should be deleted which reads "except as a special exception by the board of appeals" which would leave the section to read "No public parking place shall be conducted in any district". Such a holding will not leave the town without authority to permit proper public parking places for the reason that before the hearing was had and completed before the board of appeals on this very application the town board proceeded to amend the section so as to make it read "Public Parking Places. No public parking place shall be conducted in any district except as a special exception by the Town Board".

6. Even if the section in its entirety was deemed void because of the unconstitutionality of a part thereof, the result would be the same because under the ordinance a public parking place is not and [***5] never has been a permitted use in a business district. Moreover, in the absence of any proof that there was need for such parking places or that the interest of any person was jeopardized by the absence thereof, such a construction would not be unreasonable.

 

2878 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Longo v. Eilers


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Suffolk County


196 Misc. 909; 93 N.Y.S.2d 517; 1949 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2973



December 2, 1949 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner prospective race track owners (owners) commenced a proceeding pursuant to N.Y. Civ. Practice Act art. 78 against respondent town officials (town) seeking to legalize the completion and operation of the race track.


OVERVIEW: The owners brought an action seeking to legalize the completion and operation of their race track. Their activities were challenged by the town, which argued that the local zoning law proscribed such use. The owners argued that the zoning ordinance had never become effective because it had not been published as required, and that the property was exempt from the ordinance because of its prior nonconforming use as a race track. Upon consideration the court found in favor of the town. The court found that an affidavit of the late town clerk certifying that he had posted copies of the ordinance as required was sufficient to establish its validity. The court then found that the property's prior nonconforming use had been abandoned. The court noted that after the original owner had forfeited the property to the county for nonpayment of taxes racing on the site had been virtually nonexistent, and the track and grandstand had eventually deteriorated. The court found that when the owners' predecessor in interest subsequently purchased the property he took possession with notice of the existing zoning code, and that the limitations were therefore binding on the owners as his grantees.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the owners' proceeding against the town.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- zoning -- (1) procedure for publishing and posting zoning ordinance provided in Town Law ( § 269) must be strictly followed -- (2) former town clerk by affidavit certified that ordinances posted as required by Town Law ( § 269) although affidavit did not state that posting was for ten-day period; court assumes that posted copies remained posted for statutory period, no proof to contrary having been offered -- (3) no racing held between 1936 and 1941 at track built on property in 1927; two races held in 1941; property not used again until 1946; property deeded to county in 1937 upon owner's failure to pay taxes; county sold property to petitioners' predecessor in title in 1946; prior nonconforming use as well as title abandoned; petitioners bought property subject to zoning ordinance -- (4) contentions in brief that property valueless for other purposes and that ordinance confiscatory as to it not alleged in petition and not proven on trial; no finding made with respect thereto -- (5) no estoppel against municipality based on acts of officers in permitting continuance of improvements without objection  [***2] -- (6) municipality not entitled to injunction requiring removal of structures in proceeding under Civil Practice Act (art. 78) -- (7) proceeding dismissed on merits -- (8) since proceeding involved trial of issues of fact, full bill of costs may be taxed.

1. Under section 269 of the Town Law a zoning ordinance of a town does not become effective after adoption until it is published in a newspaper and "shall have been posted in six public places in such town for not less than ten days". Such language is mandatory and a zoning ordinance does not become effective until these requirements of publication and posting are complied with. The procedural and other provisions of the act must be strictly followed.

2. A former town clerk certified by affidavit made at the time a zoning ordinance was passed in 1934, that he had posted the ordinance in eleven public places in the town although the affidavit contained no statement that the ordinance was posted for ten days. Section 371 of the Civil Practice Act makes his affidavit presumptive evidence of that fact which by reason of remaining undisputed throughout the trial has become conclusive evidence. The court will therefore assume,  [***3] since a state of facts once shown to exist is presumed to continue until the contrary is shown, that the copies of the notice posted in at least six public places by the then town clerk remained posted for at least the statutory ten days, no proof to the contrary of any nature having been offered.

3. Petitioners operate an automobile-racing establishment in a residence zone which use may not be permitted unless a prior nonconforming use existed at the time of the adoption of the zoning ordinance. It appears that a race track was built on the property in 1927, and was operated intermittently until 1936. Thereafter no racing was held. From 1936 to 1941 the race track was not used and the grandstand became broken and was partially destroyed by fire. In 1941 one or two automobile races were run and thereafter nothing was done with the property until 1946. The record owners of the property paid no taxes thereon, as a result whereof the property was deeded to the County of Suffolk in 1937. Title remained in the county until 1946 at which time the county conveyed it. The right of the property owners to continue the nonconforming use was here lost through abandonment, not only of the [***4] nonconforming use but also of the title to the property itself. The actual abandonment and loss of title need not be inferred from nonuse of the premises during the war years, for that occurred almost five years before the outbreak of the war. When the county sold the property to petitioners' predecessor in title they took with notice of the existing zoning ordinance and with notice that the property was subject to the provisions thereof. Such limitations are binding on petitioners.

4. The contentions in petitioners' brief that the property has no value for any other purpose and that the ordinance as to the property is unconstitutional and confiscatory were neither alleged in the petition nor proven on the trial. Possibility of other utility of the property was not made the subject of testimony and hence no finding can be made with respect thereto.

5. No estoppel can be invoked against the municipality on the ground that its officers although aware of petitioners' construction and improvement activities permitted the continuance of such activities for a considerable time without objection. The acts of public officials of a town do not constitute an estoppel precluding the enforcement [***5] of existing law by the town.

6. The municipality is not entitled to the affirmative relief of requiring petitioners to remove two existing structures in a proceeding under article 78 of the Civil Practice Act. The municipality is relegated to the customary means of compelling the removal of condemned and illegal buildings.

7. The proceeding is dismissed on the merits and the temporary restraining order is dissolved, with costs to the municipality.

8. Inasmuch as the proceeding involved the trial of issues of fact, a full bill of costs may be taxed (Civ. Prac. Act, § 1301).

 

2879 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Thompson v. Wallin


Case Nos. 1, 2


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Albany and Ulster Counties


196 Misc. 686; 93 N.Y.S.2d 274; 1949 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2951



November 28, 1949 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Motion Denied January 10, 1950; See 276 A.D. 287, 94 N.Y.S.2d 268 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a consolidated action, plaintiffs--members of Communist Party of the State of New York and employees of state school system--initiated a declaratory action against defendants, members of the Board of Regents, seeking to have a law declared invalid.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs sought injunctive relief to restrain the Board from enforcing any provision of the Feinberg Law, N.Y. Elec. Law § 3201, which provided that a person employed in the public schools could have been removed for the utterance of any treasonable or seditious words or for the doing of such acts. Plaintiffs also sought an injunction against the Board from preparing and publishing a list of subversive organizations. The court held that the statute violated the constitutional proscription of bills of attainder and was a legislative finding of guilt of advocating the overthrow of the government by unlawful means, without a judicial trial in violation of U.S. Const. art. I, § 10. The court also determined that, because the statute penalized the free expression of views, it was to receive the closest judicial scrutiny and was in violation of the Due Process Clause. The court found that the statute was also unconstitutionally vague because it failed to establish a definite standard of proscribed conduct, which was a violation of U.S. Const. art. III, § 3. The court granted judgment in favor of plaintiffs in both actions.


OUTCOME: Judgment was granted in favor of plaintiffs in both actions.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- removal of subversive persons from employment in schools -- (1) Feinberg Law (Education Law, § 3022) empowers Board of Regents to adopt rules for removal of employees of schools for subversive activities; board empowered to list organizations deemed subversive, "such as the communist party"; law provides that membership in listed organizations constitutes prima facie evidence of disqualification for appointment in schools; Feinberg Law unconstitutional -- (2) that Education Law (§ 3021) and similar Civil Service Law (§ 12-a) were not challenged constitutionally for many years not determinative here -- (3) Feinberg Law creates penalty for act done prior to its enactment and constitutes bill of attainder -- (4) interest of complaining parties as regards their eligibility to hold school positions sufficient to give them standing to challenge validity of Feinberg Law -- (5) Board of Regents may prescribe qualifications of persons teaching in schools and may require  [***2] scholastic attainment and love of democratic traditions -- (6) Feinberg Law violates due process clause of Federal Constitution -- (7) Feinberg Law raises presumption of guilt based on inference only -- (8) Feinberg Law creates guilt by association and is also unconstitutional on that ground -- (9) law, which destroys guarantees of freedom, is not answer in combating menace of communism.

1. Section 3021 of the Education Law (originally L. 1917, ch. 416, § 3) provides that a person employed in a public school of the State shall be removed from his position for the utterance of any treasonable or seditious word or the doing of any treasonable acts while holding such a position. A somewhat similar provision was later enacted in section 12-a of the Civil Service Law (L. 1939, ch. 547) and applies to all persons in the service of the State or any civil division thereof. Th e so-called Feinberg Law (L. 1949, ch. 360, adding Education Law, § 3022) provides for the elimination of subversive persons from the public school system by the Board of Regents which is required to provide methods and procedure for the enforcement of section 3021 of the Education Law and section 12-a of the [***3] Civil Service Law. It further provides that such board shall list organizations found to be subversive, "such as the communist party", and to provide by rule that membership in any organization included on such list should constitute prima facie evidence of disqualification for appointment to or retention of any position in the public schools of the State. Judgment is granted in favor of plaintiffs, who in Case No.1 are the chairman and secretary of the Communist party and in Case No. 2 are employees of a city school system, declaring the Feinberg Law unconstitutional.

2. The fact that section 3021 of the Education Law and section 12-a of the Civil Service Law have been on the statute books, the former for twenty-two years, and the latter for ten years, without challenge is entitled to little weight. Old or new, no statute is immune when challenged as unconstitutional.

3. The Feinberg Law prevents a person from enjoying public employment in the school service and thus imposes a penalty for an act done prior to the enactment of the Feinberg Law. Such law therefore constitutes an illegal bill of attainder (U. S. Const., art. I, § 10). The law, as enacted, provides that membership [***4] in the Communist party is a seditious act. In the absence of proof it will not be presumed that membership in such party bears any logical relationship to such a member's fitness as a teacher. Lacking such proof, and none is required by the statute, such disqualification is a legislative finding of guilt of advocating the overthrow of Government by unlawful means without a trial and without any of the forms and guards designed to protect the security of the individual.

4. The interest of the complaining parties as regards their eligibility to hold a school position is sufficient to give them standing to question the validity of the Fei nberg Law. Their eligibility to hold a school position enjoys constitutional status and protection. They may not be deprived thereof upon grounds or by procedures which are unconstitutional.

5. Nevertheless, the Board of Regents may prescribe any test bearing a reasonable relationship to the fitness of an applicant or incumbent to teach children and the qualifications which the board may set up may include not only scholastic attainment and technical skill but love of the democratic tradition and heritage.

6. Legislation, such as the Feinberg [***5] Law which penalizes the free expression of views or espousal of doctrines must receive, therefore, the closest judicial scrutiny. The Feinberg Law is vague and fails to establish a definite standard of proscribed conduct. It is a dragnet which may enmesh anyone who agitates for a change of government. The statute violates the due process clause of the Federal Constitution in that it fails to give adequate guidance to those who would be law-abiding, to advise defendants of the nature of the offense with which they are charged, or to guide courts in trying those who are accused. The law does not require the Board of Regents to hold a hearing prior to determining whether an organization is subversive. An applicant for or an incumbent of a school position is given no notice of such hearing, as may be had, of the nature of his organization. He is afforded no opportunity to be present, to confront witnesses, to cross-examine, to adduce evidence of his own or to argue to the Board of Regents the merits of his organization. As applied to him, such determination is given weight not only as evidence but as prima facie evidence of disqualification. Proof of membership in an organization,  [***6] without proof as to the member's embracing a subversive doctrine, is sufficient basis under said law for visiting harsh consequences upon him. The procedure under the Feinberg Law amounts to a denial of due process.

7. When the Feinberg Law directs the Board of Regents to promulgate a rule to the effect that membership in any organization listed as subversive shall constitute prima facie evidence of disqualification, a presumption of guilt is created violating the doctrine that each man is deemed to be innocent until the contrary is proved beyond a reasonable doubt. A presumption to be effective must be based upon some rational connection between the fact proved and the fact inferred so as to provide reasonable basis for the inference. From the mere fact of membership in an organization and the listing of such organization the entire concept of advocacy of the overthrow of Government by violence must be inferred.

8. Under the Feinberg Law the only factual basis for disqualification is the fact of membership in an organization; no proof of illegal purpose or act is required, nor need an unlawful purpose or act of the organization be proved as against him. Membership alone is sufficient.  [***7] This is "guilt by association" with a vengeance. The law is also unconstitutional on that ground.

9. It is no answer to say that this measure is needed to combat the menace of communism. Small service, indeed, to our democracy, is afforded by emulating the tactics of communism, and by destroying the guarantees of freedom. The court finds it hard to believe that it is necessary to resort to witch-hunting in our schools to displace misfits. Necessary or not, the Feinberg Law cannot be the solution, because it is an answer which the Legislature, under the Constitution, is powerless to provide.

 

2880 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Associated Transport v. Syracuse


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Equity Term, Onondaga County


196 Misc. 1031; 93 N.Y.S.2d 423; 1949 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2963



November 21, 1949 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff truckers sought an injunction permanently enjoining defendants, city, village, and their officials, from enforcing ordinances that restricted the truckers' use of city and village streets. Plaintiffs also sought a judgment declaring that the ordinances were invalid. The village and city both alleged that their ordinances were legal and valid. The village sought the same affirmative relief against the city that the truckers demanded.


OVERVIEW: The city ordinance established a truck route that dumped through-freight traffic onto streets that the village had determined were not adequate, safe routes. The combined effect of the ordinances was to prevent through-freight traffic from proceeding beyond the city's boundary with the village. The city argued that its ordinance was reasonable, that the village could not bar trucks from entering at the point required by the city's ordinance, and that the city's only obligation was to provide a safe, suitable route for trucks within the city. The court enjoined the city from enforcing its ordinance. The court held that the village ordinances were reasonable, necessary, and not arbitrary or capricious because the restricted streets were not suitable or safe for truck use, the ordinances did not bar trucks entirely from the village, trucks were permitted to use the restricted streets for the transaction of business thereon, and another safe, suitable route was available. The court held that the city failed to perform its duty as a trustee for the public's benefit by routing truck traffic to its boundaries without regard to the safety and adequacy of the route beyond that boundary.


OUTCOME: The court held that the truckers and the village were entitled to enter a judgment declaring that so much of the city's ordinance that prohibited or attempted to prohibit drivers of vehicles from driving or operating through-freight motor trucks on any public streets of the city except the streets specified in the ordinance was illegal, null, and void. The court held that the truckers' complaint against the village should be dismissed.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- traffic regulations -- ordinance excluding trucks from streets -- (1) village contiguous to city on west enacted ordinances preventing trucks plus load weighing in excess of five tons from using three designated streets in village; in 1946, city enacted ordinance forbidding use of all its streets by trucks except certain designated streets with result that trucks could not proceed in westerly direction further than west line of city; plaintiffs, motor carriers, entitled to injunction against enforcement of city ordinance and, along with village, to judgment decreeing ordinance forbidding use of any streets in city except those designated invalid -- (2) two of three streets banned by village for use by trucks, inadequate and unsafe -- (3) village's ban on use of third street also not arbitrary in view of conditions -- (4) contention of city that having provided safe route within city, it owed plaintiffs no other obligation and that ordinance valid, not sustained in view of its knowledge that continuation of designated route in neighboring village inadequate  [***2] -- (5) under all circumstances, ordinance of city which in effect "dumped" trucks at village's door on street which city knew had been banned to truck traffic was void -- (6) quaere whether city's ordinance forbidding use of streets valid in view of Vehicle and Traffic Law ( § 90, subds. 1, 5) permitting cities to forbid heavy truck traffic from certain designated streets.

1. In an action by motor carriers of freight to enjoin a city and a village from enforcing certain ordinances adopted by each municipality, it appears that the village is contiguous to the city on the west and that in 1940 the village adopted two ordinances forbidding vehicles with a gross weight plus the load of more than five tons from using three designated streets in such village. In 1946, the city enacted an ordinance which forbade the operation of "through freight motor trucks on any of the public streets of this city" except that westbound through-freight trucks could use a street which brought such trucks to the western extremity of the city. The effect of such ordinance of the city in combination with the ordinances of the village is to prevent any westbound through-truck traffic of the kind described [***3] in the ordinances from proceeding further than the west line of the city. Plaintiffs are entitled to a permanent injunction preventing the city from enforcing its 1946 ordinance and plaintiffs and the village are granted judgment decreeing that so much of the ordinance of the city as prohibits through-freight motor trucks from using any public street of the city except those designated is illegal and void. (P. 1053.)

2. Consideration of the evidence reveals that two of the three streets of the village banned by the village for use by trucks weighing more than five tons were not safe or adequate by reason of construction, location, course and surrounding conditions. (P. 1043.)

3. As to the third street banned for use by the village, the preamble to the ordinance shows that the street (which is a continuation of the only street in the adjacent city which through-freight trucks could use) was rapidly deteriorating and would cost the village a large sum of money to repair. The evidence further showed that in enacting the ordinance, the village took into consideration the fact that thousands of persons were employed in industries concentrated on the north side of said street and that [***4] many of them were required to cross said street on foot or in automobiles twice a day and also took into consideration the fact that there was another east to west street running through the village which was adequate for truck traffic. Accordingly, such ordinance prohibiting through traffic from using said third street in the village was reasonable and necessary and was not arbitrary or capricious. (Pp. 1043-1044.)

4. The contention of the city that having provided a safe and suitable route to its west city line, it owed plaintiff no other obligation cannot be sustained in view of the fact that it knew that the continuation of such street outside the city did not provide safe and adequate routes for truck traffic. (P. 1046.)

5. At the time the city adopted its ordinance, it knew that the adjacent village since 1940 had banned trucks of over five tons from traveling over the three streets and knew that such streets were unsuitable for trucks. It also knew that when the trucks were "dumped" at the village's door, there was no other suitable west-bound route which such trucks could take. Since the city did not perform its duty to plaintiffs or to the adjacent village when it enacted [***5] an ordinance which routed through-freight truck traffic to its boundaries without regard to the adequacy of the route beyond such boundary and since the streets are a public trust for use not only by the people of the municipality but of the whole State, the ordinance of the city enacted in 1946 designating through-freight motor routes was unreasonable, arbitrary and capricious. (Pp. 1050, 1051, 1052.)

6. Subdivision 1 of section 90 of the Vehicle and Traffic Law provides that certain municipal corporations having a population in excess of 25,000 are authorized to designate highways within their boundaries from which heavy trucks could be excluded. Quaere, since the ordinance of the city did not designate highways from which heavy trucks should be excluded but instead excluded such traffic from all streets in the city except those designated, whether such ordinance is a valid enactment under subdivisions 1 and 5 of section 90 of the Vehicle and Traffic Law. (P. 1051.)

 

2881 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Board of Education v. Alexander


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Trial Term, Genesee County


197 Misc. 814; 92 N.Y.S.2d 471; 1949 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2843



October 27, 1949 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff taxpayer brought a declaratory action against defendants, village and village officials (collectively village), to prevent an assessment against a school district for the construction of a system of water works for the village's residents.


OVERVIEW: The village taxed its residents, including the school district (district), a special assessment for the construction of a water works system (system). The taxpayer claimed that the district could not have been assessed because it was tax exempt and it was not going to benefit from the system because it had its own wells. The court ruled for the village because the district was liable for the assessment under the village's bond resolution. Although the district was exempt from ad valorem taxes under N.Y. Educ. Law § 1502, it was not exempt from the assessment for the main system and its extensions to the main system in proportion to the benefit that it received. Though the district had its own wells, it still received a benefit. There were insufficient facts to determine whether the assessment was proportional to the benefit of the district's enhancement of its property value. The assessing authority had the jurisdiction to determine the proportionality issue and it was not within the court's jurisdiction to detail the method of assessment. There was no violation of due process regarding the seizure of the district's property because the system benefited the entire public.


OUTCOME: The court ruled for the village in the taxpayer's declaratory action to prevent an assessment against a school district for the construction of a system of water works for the village's residents.


HEADNOTES

Taxation -- taxability of school district for local improvement by village -- (1) declaratory judgment proper remedy to determine power of village to tax property of school district for water system -- (2) school property not subject to ad valorem tax -- (3) despite absence of power to levy assessments in Village Law ( § 223) dealing with establishment of complete water system, village has power to levy assessment for local improvement under Village Law ( § 89, subd. 38) -- (4) central school district's property subject to special assessment for water system as local improvement found to benefit property -- (5) property of school district subject to assessment despite fact that school has private water supply -- (6) special assessment may be levied despite fact that bond resolution did not include provision for assessments on property benefited -- (7) court in action for declaratory judgment cannot prescribe method of apportionment which is for assessing authority, subject to court review -- (8) assessment based on benefit does not involve taking of property without due process of law -- (9) any question of excessive assessment must be  [***2] raised under Tax Law (art. 13).

1. An action for a declaratory judgment is the proper remedy to resolve the power of a village to tax a central school district's property, situate in the village, for the purpose of construction of a water system by the village.

2. School property is not subject to an ad valorem tax to defray the cost of a water system (Education Law, § 1502).

3. Despite the fact that section 223 of the Village Law, which provides for the original establishment of a functioning water system, including a source of supply, distributing mains and distributing pipes, does not confer upon a village any assessing power, a village may nevertheless levy special assessments to defray the cost of improvements under that section by virtue of subdivision 38 of section 89 of the Village Law which grants to a village the power to levy assessments for all local improvements.

4. The property in the village of the central school district is subject to special assessment for the water system, including both the waterworks and the distribution system, which constitutes a local improvement. Since it cannot be said as a matter of law that the central school district's property [***3] can receive no benefits from the water system a special assessment against the school property can be sustained. The school property may receive some benefit in the event its own well becomes inadequate or in the event an addition to the school is made which would make the present supply insufficient. Moreover, the value of the school property will be enhanced by the central water supply, if not as school property then for other possible uses. It cannot be said that the school's property is permanently dedicated to that one use.

5. That the school district has its own private supply of water does not relieve it from the special assessment, for the property is located within the village and within the area to be served by the water system and thus within the assessment area.

6. The special assessment may be levied despite the fact that the bond resolution -- which authorized the establishment of a water system for the village and the issuance of bonds to finance it -- did not include a provision for assessments on the property benefited.

7. The question of how much the school property should pay is not within the power of the court to determine in an action for declaratory judgment.  [***4] The court will not decide what method of apportionment should be adopted by the village. The responsibility for determining the method of assessment rests with the assessing authority, subject however to a review in a proceeding in the nature of certiorari.

8. There is no constitutional question of a taking of property without due process of law in the imposition of a special assessment on the school property provided the assessment can be justified as based on benefit.

9. Any question of excessive assessment must be left for relief by way of article 13 of the Tax Law.

 

2882 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


In re Guaranty Trust Co.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


197 Misc. 462; 93 N.Y.S.2d 790; 1949 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2995



October 25, 1949 


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- impairment of obligation of contract -- where interests of beneficiaries in inter vivos trust created in New York became vested on settlor's death in March, 1948, Florida statute of 1949 imposing taxes on estates of persons dying after January 1, 1948, unconstitutional.

A settlor established a trust fund in New York and died intestate in Florida in 1948. A 1949 law of Florida which imposes estate taxes stated that it applied to estates of persons dying after January 1, 1948, or to payments of estate taxes or death taxes made, or required to be made by a fiduciary after the effective date of the law. Since the interests of the beneficiaries under the trust funds arose out of a contract in the form of a trust agreement which became vested upon settlor's death in March, 1948, the State of Florida cannot by legislation thereafter impair and destroy those rights. The Florida statute is unconstitutional as applying to these trust funds.

 

2883 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People ex rel. Heal v. Foster


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Monroe County


196 Misc. 441; 92 N.Y.S.2d 807; 1949 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2898



October 15, 1949 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner inmate sought a writ of habeas corpus following his conviction and sentence for the crime of robbery in the first degree.


OVERVIEW: The inmate was convicted of first degree robbery and he was sentenced to a prison term of 30 years. He filed a petition for a writ of habeas corpus contending that he was illegally restrained of his liberty because the grand jury that returned the indictment was composed of 24 members instead of 23 under N.Y. Code Crim. Proc. § 224. On review, the court held that the evidence showed that the twenty-fourth juror was excused after one day of service and was not present during any time when defendant was indicted. The court determined 23 good and lawful men deliberated on the indictment, in accordance with the statute.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's petition for habeas corpus.


HEADNOTES

Grand jury -- number constituting -- (1) indictment of relator by Grand Jury of twenty-four instead of maximum of twenty-three jurors, valid -- (2) no challenges were taken to panel when formed and none can be taken now -- (3) affidavits show that twenty-fourth juror had been excused prior to time when relator was indicted; Grand Jury was a legal one.

1. Relator's indictment by a Grand Jury composed of twenty-four members instead of the statutory twenty-three at the most (Code Crim. Pro., § 224) was not thereby rendered invalid. Relator suffered no injustice and was not prejudiced by this imperfection in a matter of form. Moreover, the question is raised many years after trial, conviction and sentencing. It cannot now be said that relator did not receive a fair trial because of the irregularity in the Grand Jury.

2. No challenges to the panel are now allowed except as provided for in section 238 of the Code of Criminal Procedure. None was taken and none can be taken now.

3. Moreover, affidavits show that the twenty-fourth juror was excused after one day of service and he was not present during any of the time when relator was indicted.  [***2] Hence there can be no objection to the indictment since twenty-three members deliberated thereon. It was therefore a legal Grand Jury and the writ of habeas corpus must be dismissed.

 

2884 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People ex rel. Kolm v. Newhauser


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


City Magistrate's Court of New York, Municipal Term, Borough of Queens


197 Misc. 54; 92 N.Y.S.2d 291; 1949 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2814



October 14, 1949 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a motion to dismiss an information that charged him with a violation of a local law governing traffic at a municipal airport on the ground that New York City, N.Y. Admin. Code § 435-16.0 (f) was invalid and unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: New York City, N.Y. Admin. Code § 435-16.0 (f) made it a misdemeanor to operate a plane within city limits in a manner prohibited by a rules of the civil aeronautics administration or the civil aeronautics board. The pilot had operated his plane at a height lower than required by a rule of the civil aeronautics board, 14 Fed. Reg. 479. The local law as promulgated by the city council adopted in its entirety the Air Traffic Rules as a standard of conduct for airplane pilots flying aircraft and making landings at its municipal airports. The necessity for the legislation had been demonstrated by two collisions of aircraft with the tall buildings in this city. The contention of defendant that because some other sovereignty or agency developed a pattern the city council was deprived from sanctioning its application to the needs of its own inhabitants was without merit. To adopt a federal regulation by incorporation or reference did not offend the principle of law concerning delegation of legislative power. The primary purpose of any legislation to control aircraft was to secure the public safety and general health of all the people. The local law was directed to the same objective.


OUTCOME: The court denied the motion to dismiss the information.


HEADNOTES

Aviation -- constitutional law -- adoption in local law of rules of Federal Civil Aeronautics Board governing airports as delegation of legislative power -- (1) New York City local law adopted, for municipal airports, air traffic rules of Civil Aeronautics Board in their entirety; motion of defendant, air pilot, to dismiss information charging him with violation of said local law in failing to conform to traffic pattern prescribed by said rules, denied -- (2) local law was adopted under power conferred on city pursuant to Home Rule Amendment and is subject to same standards as act of Legislature -- (3) adoption of Federal standards within local police power; adoption of Federal regulation by incorporation or reference not contrary to law concerning delegation of legislative power -- (4) local law deemed enforcement measure in aid of Federal legislation to regulate aircraft within municipality -- (5) city's exercise of police power in field occupied by Federal Government does not render act unconstitutional where local law does not interfere with air commerce but merely  [***2] supplements Federal enforcement -- (6) local law not unconstitutional.

1. Section 435-16.0 of chapter 18 of the Administrative Code of the City of New York forbids the navigation of an aircraft within the city limits contrary to the rules and regulations of the Federal Civil Aeronautics Administration or the Federal Civil Aeronautics Board. The local law adopted in their entirety these rules as a standard of conduct for pilots flying aircraft and making landings at its municipal airports. Defendant was charged with a violation of said local law in that he failed to conform to the traffic pattern which was prescribed by the Civil Aeronautics Board in making a landing on a runway of La Guardia airport and required final approach for landing at an altitude of at least 1,200 feet and descent over a park and water insofar as practical. Affidavits of police show that defendant's final approach to said runway was at an altitude of 800 feet and that the pattern was not conformed to, as required by the rules. Defendant moved to dismiss the information on the ground that the section alleged to have been violated is invalid and unconstitutional. The motion is denied.

2. The local law,  [***3] which includes the section in question, was adopted pursuant to the powers conferred upon the city by the State Legislature under the Home Rule Amendment to the Constitution. Local laws so passed are to be judged by the same standards as an act of the Legislature.

3. The adoption of the standards of the Civil Aeronautics Board, a Federal agency, was wholly within the local police power. The contention of defendant that, because some other sovereignty or agency developed a pattern, the city council is deprived of sanctioning its application to the needs of its own inhabitants is without merit. To adopt a Federal regulation by incorporation or reference does not offend the principle of law concerning delegation of legislative power.

4. The local authorities and Federal agencies participated in drafting the air traffic rules which were to apply to the landing and taking off of aircraft at La Guardia Airport. (Darweger v. Staats, 267 N. Y. 290, 196 N.E. 61, distinguished.) The local law involved is in essence nothing more nor less than an enforcement measure in aid of Federal legislation to regulate aircraft within the boundaries of the municipality.

5. Even if it were true [***4] that Congress has exclusive authority to regulate air commerce, it cannot be said that a State or municipality is precluded from enacting legislation which has the effect of enforcing Federal regulations which apply peculiarly within the municipal limits. The contention that the exercise of police power in a field which the Federal Government has occupied renders such act unconstitutional is without merit. The enactment of the local law in no way interferes with nor does it affect air commerce but merely supplements the enforcement of the civil aeronautics regulations. Moreover, the enforcement machinery of the Federal Government is inadequate to police every airport i n every State and proper enforcement by local authorities is necessary.

6. The local law is not unconstitutional since it violates neither the provisions of the New York State Constitution nor the Constitution of the United States.

 

2885 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Sehulster v. Carney


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Trial Term, New York County


196 Misc. 356; 92 N.Y.S.2d 908; 1949 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2923



October 7, 1949 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, an employee of a corporation that existed when which respondent city assumed the operations of a utility department, brought a proceeding under N.Y. Civ. Prac. Act art. 78 to compel the city and respondent commissioner of the department of water, gas, and electricity, to employ him and to assign him as a grade 3 clerk in the department as of a date certain and to pay his salary from that date.


OVERVIEW: The city assumed operation of a utility that supplied water to certain residents. Shortly before that date arrangements were being made for the employees of the corporation to transfer to the city as city employees. The city had to continue to employee all the employees of the corporation who worked at a plant on the day that it assumed operation of the plant. Despite that requirement, the city and the commissioner refused to assign the employee to work or to pay him. The court held that the employee was entitled to the job but that he was not entitled to receive salary retroactive to August 21, 1947. The award of back pay violated N.Y. Const. art. VIII, § 1, art. IX, § 10, which prohibited gifts of public money or property.


OUTCOME: The court granted the reinstatement request but denied the employee's request for back pay.


HEADNOTES

Civil service -- status of employees of water company -- ( 1) city assumed operation of water company and undertook to transfer employees of plant to city in noncompetitive class by local law; under local law, continuity of employment of persons of water company mandatory and required city to employ employees; continuity of employment not made discretionary by resolution of civil service commission -- (2) petitioner, employee of water company, is entitled to order directing city to employ him; petitioner's undertaking for water company of work to close its affairs with permission of city and consequent delay, not laches -- (3) award of retroactive salary cannot be made -- (4) moreover, award of back pay would be unconstitutional -- (5) proceeding not barred by Statute of Limitations.

1. The City of New York assumed operation of the plant of a private water company supplying residents of the city with water. Arrangements were made by the water company and the city for the transfer of employees of such plant to the city and to that end a proposed local law (Local Law No. 62, 1947) was introduced in the city council [***2] which provided that all employees of a water company, the operation of which should be assumed by the city, who were in the employ of such company at the time of such assumption by the city, should be continued in the employment of the city and that the positions should be in the noncompetitive class. Under this local law continuity of employment on the date of assumption of operation by the city was mandatory, except that the municipal civil service commission could exclude a person who was not of good character. The civil service commission could not, by resolution which it adopted and which related to those employees which the city "elects" to continue in employment, make continuity of employment discretionary when such continuity was made mandatory by local law.

2. Petitioner, an employee of the water company, whose good moral character has not been controverted, is entitled to an order compelling the city to employ him although when petitioner received the offer of city employment, he did not signify his willingness to accept the employment. It appears that at that time he was requested to assist the water company in winding up the affairs of the particular plant where he [***3] was doing his work and the city officials gave him a leave of absence without pay. When his temporary work was completed he notified the city that he was prepared to start to work for it and although he kept himself available for work and repeatedly requested an assignment, none was ever made. Under these circumstances petitioner was not guilty of laches. Petitioner had been duly appointed when an assistant commissioner had him execute an oath and acceptance of employment which was approved by the commissioner himself.

3. Petitioner is not entitled to receive salary retroactively. Section 23 of the Civil Service Law is based on reinstatement after a wrongful removal and is inapplicable to the type of employment in this case where the city acquired ownership of a private enterprise and sought to achieve continuity of employment. The parties contemplated that petitioner would be assigned to work and that his salary would begin from the date of such assignment.

4. Moreover, an award of back pay would violate the State Constitution which prohibits gifts of public money or property (art. VIII, § 1; art. IX, § 10).

5. The Statute of Limitations is no bar to the proceeding since [***4] the city did not take final action on petitioner's demands until March 9, 1949.

 

2886 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


G. T. McGovern Trucking Co. v. Moses


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


16 Misc. 2d 72; 92 N.Y.S.2d 550; 1949 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1648



September 26, 1949 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, the motion to dismiss the complaint is granted.

Settle order.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, members and officers of a tunnel and bridge authority, moved to dismiss plaintiff carrier's complaint, which challenged an increase in bridge tolls and rates, on the ground that it did not state facts sufficient to constitute a cause of action.


OVERVIEW: The authority was empowered to establish and collect tolls on bridges under its jurisdiction. The carrier, on behalf of itself and all other persons similarly situated, challenged the authority's decision to increase tolls on the bridges. The court dismissed the carrier's complaint. The court found that, because the injury complained of was a public injury, the carrier could not maintain an action for relief unless it suffered a special injury different from that suffered by the public at large. The carrier did not establish that it suffered a special injury peculiar to it and different from that suffered by the public at large.


OUTCOME: The court granted the authority's motion to dismiss the complaint.


HEADNOTES

Actions -- action against public authority -- no individual, even if suing on behalf of all others similarly situated, can sue to enforce public right -- action for judgment declaring that Triborough Bridge and Tunnel Authority cannot constitutionally increase tolls, dismissed.

1. For the enforcement of a public right, no individual, whether individually or on behalf of all others similarly situated, can maintain an action for an injunction or declaratory judgment or other relief, unless he can show a special individual injury different from the public's.

2. This action by a motor carrier corporation, suing individually and on behalf of all others similarly situated, for a judgment declaring that the Triborough Bridge and Tunnel Authority (Public Authorities Law, §§ 550-571) cannot constitutionally increase toll rates, is therefore dismissed.

 

2887 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Platt v. New York


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Kings County


196 Misc. 360; 92 N.Y.S.2d 138; 1949 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2776



September 26, 1949 



DISPOSITION: Motion denied. Settle order on notice.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant city filed a motion to dismiss plaintiff property owner's complaint for insufficiency.


OVERVIEW: The property owner's complaint alleged that a proposed parkway shown on the city's master plan included real property belonging to her and that the city had directed the building department not to permit any building on the property. The owner alleged that she derived an income from the sale of the property; and that her customers now refused to buy the property. The court held that the city acted in conformance with N.Y. Gen. City Law § 35, and unless the owner properly alleged an impairment of her constitutional rights, the motion to dismiss the complaint should have been granted. The court concluded that the motion had to be denied. Section 35 afforded the owner no remedy. She was in no position to submit plans and apply for a variance under § 35 because she derived her livelihood from the sale of her property. The property owner could have been entitled to equitable relief.


OUTCOME: The court denied the motion to dismiss the complaint.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- establishment of official map as affecting private owner's right to sell land in bed of mapped street -- complaint alleges that plaintiff owns land in bed of proposed parkway and that defendant city has refused to issue permits for building on property, that plaintiff derives income from sale of property, and that customers refuse to buy same; since complaint alleges income derived from sale of property, plaintiff not in position to submit plans for obtaining building permit (General City Law, § 35); statute affords plaintiff no remedy; cause of action for equitable relief stated; complaint sufficient.

A complaint alleges that a proposed parkway shown on the "Master Plan of the City of New York" includes real property belonging to plaintiff; that defendant city has directed its building department not to permit any building on said property; that plaintiff derives an income from the sale of the property, and that plaintiff's customers now refuse to buy the property. The complaint is sufficient. Section 35 of the General City Law authorizes the refusal [***2] of permits for "any building in the bed of any street or highway shown or laid out on such map or plan" and grants authority to the city board of standards and appeals to grant variances "which will as little as practicable increase the cost of opening such street or highway". Since section 35 provides a method for obtaining a building permit and plaintiff alleges that she "derives a major portion of her livelihood from the sale of her real property" plaintiff is thus in no position to submit plans and apply for a variance. The statute affords her no remedy, and defendants' contention that plaintiff has no cause of action because she has not yet exhausted her remedy under the statute is untenable. Whether or not the complaint properly alleges pecuniary damage is immaterial on this motion. Plaintiff may be entitled to equitable relief.

 

2888 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Kraushaar v. Zion


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Nassau County


196 Misc. 437; 94 N.Y.S.2d 449; 1949 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3097



August 29, 1949 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner husband sought review by certiorari of a tax assessment against his property made by respondents mayor and council to pay for sidewalks, curbs, and gutters. He contended that the resolution adopting the assessment was invalid because he did not receive notice of council's action and that the notice provided by the village was ineffective because it was sent to his wife and not him.


OVERVIEW: The council took action by resolution to require adjoining property owners to pay for the cost of improving sidewalks, curbs, and gutters. No notice was provided to adjoining property owners before the action was taken. The court held that none was required by N.Y. Village Law § 161. The statute gave the village an unqualified power to make street improvements and also to place the cost of the improvements, in whole or in part, upon adjoining property owners. The notice required by § 161 was to adjoining property owners that they were being required to pay for the improvement. The wife was the owner of adjoining property when the resolution was passed and transferred it to her husband before the notice was sent. Although the notice to the wife did not comply with § 161's requirement that the notice be sent to the record owner, the court held that the husband had timely, actual knowledge of the facts satisfying the objectives of the statute. He and his wife were in the village office the day after the notice was sent inquiring as to what was required of them by the resolution.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the writ.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- power of village to assess cost of curbing on landowner -- (1) certiorari proceedings to review assessment on petitioner landowner of cost of installing curbing by village pursuant to Village Law (§ 161), dismissed -- (2) mailing of notice of resolution of village to petitioner's wife who was landowner at time resolution adopted, which notice petitioner read, not defective; petitioner's actual knowledge satisfied objectives of statute -- (3) Village Law (§ 161) not unconstitutional because of failure to provide notice and hearing to affected property owner.

1. In certiorari proceedings to review an assessment imposed against petitioner's land by reason of the installation of curbing by a village after a resolution was adopted by the village pursuant to the Village Law (§ 161) requiring the owners listed in the resolution to do such work at their own expense, the petition is dismissed.

2. At the time the resolution of the village was adopted, petitioner's wife was the record owner. At the time that the notice of the resolution was mailed petitioner had become the owner. By section 161 of [***2] the Village Law a village is clothed with authority to construct and repair sidewalks, curbs and gutters at its own cost or to place the cost in whole or in part upon adjoining landowners. That is an unqualified grant of power which may be exercised at any time and without advance notice to the affected property owners, especially if the village is to absorb the costs. If the adjoining landowners are to have the cost placed upon them, the village is required to give notice to them of the specified time, place and manner of the proposed construction. The notice simply informs the landowners of the action which the authorities have taken. It does not imply that the recipient may attend at some time or place to object. Since petitioner was aware of the notice mailed to his wife and read it, and also called at the office of the village to inquire about the work, petitioner lost nothing because he received the notification indirectly. Petitioner's objection to the notice is one in form only and may not be sustained. His timely, actual knowledge of the facts satisfied the objectives of the statute.

3. The omission in the statute to provide notice and a hearing to the affected property [***3] owners in advance of the official action which burdened them with the cost of the improvement did not render section 161 of the Village Law unconstitutional. The right to own land obliges the owner to pay for public improvements incidental to his ownership and to submit to the right of constituted authority to determine the improvements needed and to decide upon whom the cost thereof shall fall.

 

2889 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Dilliard v. North Hills


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Nassau County


195 Misc. 875; 91 N.Y.S.2d 542; 1949 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2648



August 12, 1949 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff landowners filed an action to declare invalid a zoning ordinance of defendant village.


OVERVIEW: The zoning ordinance prescribed that areas for residential districts was two acres. The landowners claimed that the ordinance was unconstitutional and void in that it deprived them of their property without due process of law, and such circumstances in effect took their property for public use without just compensation, and that the restrictions bore no substantial relation to public health, safety, and morals. The court found that only the question of police power arose in the case. The court held that the ordinance was unconstitutional and void. The court found that exercise of police power was restricted to prohibitions necessary to guard public health, morals, and safety, and to conserve public peace, order, and the general welfare. The court found no relation of the zoning ordinance to any of those concerns, and found that it was at most an attempt to protect large estates in the village.


OUTCOME: The court declared the ordinance unconstitutional and invalid.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- zoning -- (1) zoning ordinance of village requiring area of two acres for house in residence A district unrelated to public health, welfare or morals and is unconstitutional -- (2) part of action attacking ordinance on ground no public hearings held dismissed.

1. A zoning ordinance of a village requiring that a house in a residence A district be built upon two acres of land has no relation to the public health, the public welfare or the public morals, but seems to have been designed to protect the large estates in the village. Such provision of the zoning ordinance is unconstitutional.

2. That part of the action attacking the ordinance on the ground that no public hearings were held is dismissed.

 

2890 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Queens Farms Dairy, Inc.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


City Magistrate's Court of New York, Borough of Queens, Municipal Term


195 Misc. 510; 90 N.Y.S.2d 817; 1949 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2499



July 18, 1949 



DISPOSITION: The motion to dismiss for failure of proof beyond a reasonable doubt is denied and the defendant corporation is found guilty as charged.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In defendant's prosecution for violating New York City, N.Y., Sanitary Code § 139 by selling adulterated or misbranded egg nog, defendant filed the motion to dismiss for failure of proof beyond a reasonable doubt.


OVERVIEW: The egg nog produced by defendant included tartrazine, which was a harmless coal tar substance that enhanced the yellow hue of the egg nog. The tartrazine was a component of the rum flavor manufactured by another firm and defendant was not aware of its existence. Defendant was prosecuted for violating § 139, which prohibited the sale of adulterated or misbranded food. After trial, defendant filed the motion to dismiss the matter for failure of proof beyond a reasonable doubt. The court denied the motion, finding that consumers purchasing egg nog presumed that the coloring emanated from the eggs, rather than a coloring matter that was not a normal ingredient featured on the label. Applying the definition of "adulterated" contained in New York City, N.Y., Sanitary Code § 139(5), the court concluded that the addition of tartrazine constituted a coloring improperly added to make the egg nog better than it really was. Therefore, the proof supported the conclusion that defendant violated § 139. The fact that defendant was unaware of the tartrazine in the rum flavoring was not a defense to the charge. Intent was not a required element under the provision.


OUTCOME: The court denied the motion to dismiss and rendered judgment finding defendant guilty.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- possession and sale of adulterated food -- (1) defendant convicted of having for sale adulterated food in that "egg nog" contained tartrazinegiving product yellow color; food deemed adulterated if it is colored so that it is "made to appear better than it really is" (Sanitary Code of City of New York, § 139) -- (2) immaterial that defendant had no knowledge that tartrazine was ingredient of flavoring used by it and manufactured by another.

1. Section 139 of the Sanitary Code of the City of New York provides that no person shall have, sell or offer for sale any food which is adulterated, and that food shall be deemed adulterated if "it is colored * * * whereby * * * it is made to appear better than it really is." Defendant corporation is found guilty of having for sale or offering for sale a milk product labeled as "egg nog", containing a harmless coal tar product or [***2] dye known as tartrazine, which is yellow in color and nonnutritive. The "egg nog" was made to appear more yellow in color as a result of this ingredient which was part of a rum flavor not manufactured by the defendant. People purchased the "egg nog" presuming that the color emanated from the eggs.

2. It is no defense that the defendant had no knowledge of the tartrazine in the rum flavoring, and had no intention of including it in the final product. As against innocent purchasers, defendant had a duty, for the protection of the public, to determine whether tartrazine was an ingredient of the rum flavoring it used.

 

2891 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Leven v. Birrell


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court, Special Term, New York County, Part I


91 N.Y.S.2d 729; 1949 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2703



June 20, 1949 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, corporate officers and directors, filed a motion to dismiss the representative action by plaintiff stockholders on the ground that there was another action pending in the court by the same parties for the same relief.


OVERVIEW: Prior to the institution of the stockholders' representative action, different stockholders brought such an action against defendants. The prior action was stayed because the stockholders refused on constitutional grounds to post security to pay defendants' expenses as required by N.Y. Gen. Corp. Law §§ 61-b and 64. The court considered the motion in this action to have been brought under N.Y. R. Civ. Prac. Rule 107(4). The court found hat the stockholders were not the same persons as those who brought the prior action even though both sets of stockholders were suing in their capacity as stockholders and asserted the rights of the same corporation and the relief was predicated on the same alleged transactions. The court held that the two actions were distinct from one another because the prior action sought rescission of certain transactions plus damages while this action sought damages only and was against defendants in their individual capacity. The court held that the reference in N.Y. R. Civ. Prac. 107(4) of another action pending "for the same cause" did not mean the same cause of action but substantially the same set of facts upon which relief was requested.


OUTCOME: The court granted defendants' motion to dismiss unless the stockholders, within 30 days from the date thereof, stipulated to consolidate their action with a pending action against the defendants that was brought by other stockholders.

 

2892 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Zorach v. Clauson


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Kings County


195 Misc. 531; 90 N.Y.S.2d 750; 1949 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2477



June 13, 1949 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner committee filed a motion for an order permitting it to intervene and become a party respondent in a N.Y. Civ. Prac. Act art. 78 proceeding directed against respondents, Commissioner of Education of the State of New York and the board of education of the City of New York.


OVERVIEW: The relief sought in the underlying action was the termination and discontinuance of a "released time" program in which public school pupils were permitted to be released not more than one hour weekly upon the request of their parents to attend classes in religious observance and instruction. The committee was organized for the purpose of furthering the released time program. The court found that the committee, therefore, had a direct and immediate concern in the proceeding and was substantially and beneficially interested in upholding the refusal of respondents to discontinue the program. The court granted the committee's application and admitted the committee as a party respondent.


OUTCOME: The court granted the committee's application in all respects and admitted it as a party respondent in the proceeding with leave to serve an answer within 10 days after service of a copy of the order.


HEADNOTES

Parties -- intervention -- (1) motion by private committee for permission to intervene in proceeding brought under article 78 of Civil Practice Act to terminate "released time" program in city of New York for religious instruction, granted; intervener, organized to further program of released time, "specially and beneficially interested" (Civ. Prac.
