Act, § 1298) -- (2) trial will not be delayed -- (3) immaterial that committee could protect rights as amicus curiae -- (4) application will not be denied because others might seek to intervene -- (5) right of intervention under statute not limited to proceedings brought to review determination, but extends to all remedies under article 78.

1. A motion by the Greater New York Co-ordinating Committee on Released Time of Jews, Protestants and Roman Catholics for an order permitting it to intervene and to become a party respondent in a proceeding under article 78 of the Civil Practice Act, wherein petitioner seeks the termination of the "released time" program in the city of New York whereby public school pupils are released to attend classes in religious instruction is granted. Section 1298 [***2] of the Civil Practice Act provides for the joining of a party upon the application "of a person specially and beneficially interested in upholding or annulling a determination to be reviewed". The intervener was organized for the purpose of furthering the program of "released time". It has a direct and immediate concern in the subject of the proceeding and is substantially and beneficially interested in upholding the refusal of the board of education of the city of New York and the Commissioner of Education of the State of New York to discontinue the program. The petition itself states that the released-time program in New York City is under the supervision of the committee and describes the activities of the committee in the conduct of the released-time program.

2. The intervention will not delay the trial and disposition of the proceeding.

3. It is immaterial that the committee could also protect its rights as amicus curiae.

4. The possibility that others might also seek to intervene does not justify the denial of the present application.

5. Section 1298 of the Civil Practice Act was not intended to limit the allowing of intervention only to cases where the proceeding [***3] is brought to review a determination. The words "upholding or annulling a determination to be reviewed" must be interpreted in their generic sense so as to allow intervention in all proceedings under article 78 of the Civil Practice Act, such as here, a proceeding to compel the performance of a duty specifically enjoined by law.

 

2893 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Bristol-Myers Co. v. Picker


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, TRIAL TERM, NEW YORK COUNTY


195 Misc. 151; 89 N.Y.S.2d 215; 1949 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2251



May 24, 1949 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff manufacturer filed an action against defendant merchants seeking a permanent injunction to restrain the merchants from issuing cash register receipts to customers who purchased the manufacturer's products.


OVERVIEW: The manufacturer marketed various drug and cosmetic articles, and they fixed minimum retail prices for its products by contracts made with their distributors. The merchants owned a drug store where they sold many drugs and cosmetics, including those of the manufacturer. They belonged to an organization of merchants that gave dividends to customers in the form of cash register receipts that represented a percentage of the amount of each purchase. A customer who purchased $ 10 worth of merchandise could redeem the receipts for 25 cents worth of merchandise. The manufacturer filed an action against the merchants seeking a permanent injunction to restrain them from issuing the receipts. The court awarded the injunction, holding that the merchants were not entitled to issue the receipts unless they sold them at that same percentage of prices above the fixed minimum contract prices. The law permitted contracts fixing minimum prices and forbade the sale of any such commodities at less than those stipulated prices. The customer was deliberately given a discount from the purchase price through the issuance of the receipts, just as effectively as if he received a cash rebate.


OUTCOME: The court granted the permanent injunction.

Mellman v. Metropolitan Jockey Club, Inc.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court, Special Term, New York County, Part I


195 Misc. 121; 89 N.Y.S.2d 64; 1949 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2220



May 13, 1949 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner filed an application to review a determination by respondents, certain jockey clubs, racing associations, and the New York State Racing Commission, which allowed and permitted at racetracks owned, maintained, and operated by them in New York daily double betting and wagering. Petitioner sought an order directing respondents to desist from permitting such wagering and an order revoking licenses to operate tracks and to bet therein.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner alleged that the conduct pursued was not "pari-mutuel betting" but was in effect a lottery, bookmaking, pool selling, and gambling. Petitioner alleged that tickets were not vended from tote machines and that wagering on the daily double was not immediately and electrically and mechanically displayed on a totalizer board as required by pari-mutuel law. The court found an insufficient basis in law and in fact to grant the relief sought. N.Y. Const. art. I, § 9 allowed pari-mutuel betting on horse races as prescribed by the legislature and from which the state of New York derived reasonable revenue for the support of government. The constitution made no mention of mechanically operated machines. By enacting N.Y. Unconsol. Law § 7569, the legislature provided that tickets sold for daily doubles did not have to be vended by a mechanical or electrical device nor was it required that the totals of the pool be computed and determined by a totalizer. The court held that respondents' conduct was not unreasonable, arbitrary, and capricious.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the petition.

 

2895 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People ex rel. Lawrence v. Kraushaar


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


District Court of New York, Second District, Nassau County


195 Misc. 487; 89 N.Y.S.2d 685; 1949 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2320



May 6, 1949 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with violating Lawrence, N.Y. General Ordinances of the Village §§ 361.1, 361.2, 361.3, 312(2), by parking a motor vehicle in a space, which required a particular license, without having obtained such license.


OVERVIEW: Defendant did not deny that he did not have a license or that he parked his car in a spot designated for driver's with such licenses. However, defendant attacked the validity of the ordinance, contending that its provisions were repugnant to and in direct violation of N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 54 and that they were repugnant to and in direct violation of N.Y. Const. art. I, § 11, and U.S. Const. amend. XIV as violating equal protection. While the court rejected the former argument, it accepted the latter. The court agreed that the ordinance violated equal protection. The court held that because the ordinance discriminated within a class of nonresidents of the village, namely in providing for the issuance of permits for one dollar per year to the residents of two localities, both communities outside of the village, and charged other nonresidents $ 10 per year, the ordinance was discriminatory, unconstitutional and void. Accordingly, the court discharged defendant.


OUTCOME: The court found defendant not guilty and discharged him. The court further determined that the ordinance in question was unconstitutional.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- discrimination in license fees for parking -- (1) village ordinance providing for parking license, fees for which are higher for some nonresidents than for other nonresidents, unconstitutional -- (2) park not changed to public street by use of space for parking automobiles -- (3) amount charged, $ 10 for nonresidents, normal for purpose, is not tax -- (4) General Municipal Law (§ 80), relating to discrimination in business against nonresidents, not applicable.

1. An ordinance of a village provides that a license must be obtained for parking a motor vehicle in a space, part of a park, that the fee for a parking license for the residents of the village is $ 1 per annum, that the fee for residents of specified communities outside the village is also $ 1 per annum, and that the fee for all others is $ 10 per annum. The ordinance is unconstitutional in that it discriminates within the class of nonresidents of the village. The village might grant a preference to residents over nonresidents, but is discriminatory when it provides for a charge for some nonresidents higher than that [***2] for other nonresidents. Defendant is found not guilty of violating the ordinance and an information charging that he parked his motor vehicle without a license to do so is dismissed.

2. The village did not change the character of the park to a village street when it set aside a part of the park as parking space for automobiles.

3. The license fee is not a tax. The amount of $ 10 charged per year for nonresidents is a normal amount for the purpose.

4. Section 80 of the General Municipal Law relating to discrimination against nonresidents when carrying on business within the limits of a municipal corporation is not pertinent.

 

2896 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


East Coast Lumber Terminal, Inc. v. Babylon


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Nassau County


195 Misc. 516; 89 N.Y.S.2d 253; 1949 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2256



May 4, 1949 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff landowner, citing U.S. Const. amend. XIV, N.Y. Const. art. I, §§ 6, 11, sought an injunction pendente lite to restrain defendant Town of Babylon (New York) from enforcing Babylon, N.Y., Ordinance Licensing and Otherwise Regulating Sand, Gravel and Clay Pits, Quarries, Removal of Topsoil, and Other Excavations (Dec. 6, 1943) during pendency of the landowner's action to enjoin enforcement of the Ordinance and implementing regulations.


OVERVIEW: An interlocutory federal court judgment denying relief until state issues were passed upon by the state courts had been affirmed on appeal. The landowner contended that the Ordinance was unreasonable and denied the landowner equal protection of the laws. The court granted the motion. The court found that the Ordinance and implementing regulations seemed so unreasonable as applied to the landowner that it was proper to grant temporary relief to maintain the status quo pending the final outcome of the litigation. The court observed that the federal appellate court had stated that the meaning of the ordinance was not plain as to the conditions of which the landowner complained and that such uncertainties were often treated as controlling. The court found that there was some proof of unequal and discriminatory administration of the Ordinance and regulations so far as the landowner was concerned. The court stated that the injury that the landowner would suffer from a denial of relief was far greater than that which the Town could sustain by a temporary restraint.


OUTCOME: The court granted the landowner's motion for an injunction pendente lite in its action against the Town seeking to enjoin enforcement of an ordinance and regulations.


HEADNOTES

Injunctions -- temporary injunction -- town restrained from enforcing ordinance during pendency of action for permanent injunction; ordinance governing conditions for removal of sand and gravel, so unreasonable that status quo should be maintained until final determination of meaning of statute and its constitutionality.

A motion for an order restraining a town from enforcing an ordinance during the pendency of an action to enjoin its enforcement is granted. The ordinance and regulations seem so unreasonable that it is proper that temporary relief should be granted to maintain the status quo until a final determination of the question so that plaintiff's business will not be destroyed. The ordinance deals with the licensing of and otherwise regulating sand, gravel and clay pits. It requires that a license for the removal of material must be renewed monthly. It is possible under the ordinance that a bond of over $ 175,000 would have to be furnished by plaintiff at a time when it has been unable to persuade its surety to continue a much smaller bond. The ordinance requires that all excavation should be refilled [***2] and covered with four inches of topsoil. The court must determine what the true meaning of the ordinance is, and, having determined its meaning, whether it is constitutional. There is some proof that there has been unequal and discriminatory administration of the ordinance as against plaintiff (N. Y. Const., art. I, § 11). The injury which plaintiff will suffer from a denial of the temporary relief is far greater than that which the town can sustain as a result of granting such relief.

 

2897 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Gold Sound, Inc. v. New York


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court, Special Term, New York County, Part III


195 Misc. 291; 89 N.Y.S.2d 860; 1949 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2356



May 3, 1949 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, sound equipment owners and sellers, filed an action against defendant city to enjoin the enforcement of New York City, N.Y., Local Laws No. 64 (1948), New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code § 435-6.0, which regulated the use of mechanically amplified sound upon and adjacent to public streets, parks, and places. The city filed a motion to dismiss the complaint.


OVERVIEW: The complaint asserted that the local law was unconstitutional because it prohibited the use of sound amplification for commercial purposes, and it violated the constitutional protection to the right of free speech. The city argued that the local law was enacted to insure safety and to protect the public health and welfare as well as the peace and comfort of neighboring inhabitants. The owners and sellers maintained that the local law placed the right to be heard in the uncontrolled discretion of a city official and that that constituted a previous restraint upon the right of freedom of speech. The court determined that the owners had set forth sufficient factual averments in the complaint to withstand the motion to dismiss. However, the allegations in the complaint as to the sellers did not meet the requirement of showing that the sellers' rights or privileges were restricted, curtailed, destroyed, or directly affected by the provisions of the local law.


OUTCOME: The court denied the motion to dismiss as to the owners but granted the motion to dismiss as to the sellers.

 

2898 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People ex rel. Provident Loan Soc. v. Chambers


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


196 Misc. 367; 88 N.Y.S.2d 459; 1949 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2120



March 17, 1949 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Relator loan society created by statute brought a certiorari proceeding against respondent Tax Commission of the City of New York, seeking an order adjudging that the society was a corporation organized exclusively for charitable purposes and devoted solely to the carrying out of those purposes, and that the real property occupied as its principal office was therefore exempt from taxation for the year 1948-1949.


OVERVIEW: N.Y. Laws ch. 295, §2 (1894) created the loan society to aid those needing financial assistance. The court concluded that the society was organized exclusively for charitable purposes, and was therefore entitled to have its real property exempt from taxation pursuant to N.Y. Tax Law § 4(6). The court held that the lending of money to those applying for pecuniary assistance upon the security of pledged articles of personalty, at rates less than those charged by other available sources for such loans, and in amounts and on terms not generally procurable from other sources, constituted the doing of charity where no private gain could result and where the net income merely augmented the funds employed in making the loans from time to time. Although the society had an undistributed surplus of over ten million dollars, it had an average net return of less that one percent on the loans it had made. The exclusively charitable character of relator's activities was not affected by the fact that some loans made were large in amount or by the fact that many of the borrowers may not have been poor people in a strict sense.


OUTCOME: The court granted the society's request for an order declaring that the society was a corporation organized exclusively for charitable purposes and devoted solely to the carrying out of those purposes, and that the real property occupied as its principal office was therefore exempt from taxation for the year 1948-1949.


HEADNOTES

Taxation -- exemption -- (1) real property of corporation organized for purpose of loaning money to those in need of pecuniary assistance on pledge of personal property entitled to exemption from tax under Tax Law (§ 4, subd. 6) -- (2) relator's charitable purpose not cut down by act of subsequent Legislature using similar language to that in relator's charter in providing for incorporation of pawnbrokers -- (3) that relator charges interest for services not reason for denial of exemption -- (4) relator's purpose relieves Government of assuming burden of lending money -- (5) evidence that relator charged less interest than pawnbrokers may be considered to establish relator's charitable object -- (6) profits above costs not used for private gain of members, but used in augmenting fund for lending -- (7) occasional lending of large sum to individual not noncharitable act -- (8) storage of furs in cold storage vault incidental to charitable purposes -- (9) advertisement of loan facilities does not change charitable character of activities -- (10) evidence lacking that  [***2] any officer or employee entitled to pecuniary profit -- (11) relator's right to exemption so clear that principle of practical construction based on nonclaiming of tax exemption for over fifty years inapplicable -- (12) granting of exemption from taxation to relator under other statutes immaterial.

1. Relator was incorporated in 1894 by a special act of the Legislature. The purpose for which relator was organized is declared in the act as being "aiding such persons as said Society shall deem in need of pecuniary assistance, by loans of money at interest, upon the pledge or mortgage of personal property." The purpose of the society is not to lend money at interest but rather to aid those needing financial assistance. Consequently relator is entitled to the exemption from taxation of its real property (occupied as its principal office) as a corporation organized exclusively for charitable purposes within subdivision 6 of section 4 of the Tax Law. Such lending of money was not to result in private gain, for the act of incorporation expressly provided that no member of the society should receive any compensation or any profit other than lawful interest. The act of incorporation [***3] omitted a provision limiting the rate of interest which could be charged because the State Constitution prohibited a private act "regulating the rate of interest". Such a limitation was subsequently placed in the constitution of the society and has remained there since. The omission from the act of incorporation of a restriction upon the amount of interest which could be charged should not require a holding that relator was not organized for a charitable purpose.

2. The charitable character of relator's purpose to give "aid" without profit to persons "in need of pecuniary assistance" cannot be negatived by the fact that a subsequent Legislature used similar language in providing for the incorporation of ordinary pawnbrokers.

3. Relator's activities in lending money at interest are charitable in nature, notwithstanding the fact that the services are not rendered gratuitously or limited exclusively to the poor.

4. If the underlying concept of tax-exemption statutes is, as the city claims, that the exemption is granted in exchange for the assumption of burdens of a public nature which the Government itself should discharge, then relator has assumed a burden of a public nature which [***4] the Government itself could properly discharge, and might conceivably be forced to discharge in the absence of the existence of a lending agency, such as relator.

5. Evidence that since its incorporation relator has never charged more than 12% interest per annum, a rate lower than that generally obtainable elsewhere, may properly be considered for the purpose of establishing that the object for which relator was formed was to make loans at less than the rates charged by pawnbrokers and loan sharks.

6. The evidence establishes that to the extent that the charges made by relator exceed the cost of its lending services the difference does not redound to the private gain of its members or trustees, but goes to augment the fund available for employment in the performance of the relator's lending functions.

7. Relator's property has not been used for the lending of large individual sums to an extent to indicate that its purpose is not charitable. Only an insignificant portion has thus been given and the word "exclusively" in the tax-exemption statute is not to be given an interpretation so literal as to prevent an occasional use of relator's property for such purpose.

8. Nor has relator [***5] entered into the business of fur storage by its installation of cold storage vaults. Relator does not accept furs for storage except where the furs are pledged as security for loans. The storage is then incidental to the loan for the purpose of preventing deterioration of the pledge.

9. That relator advertises its loan facilities and solicits borrowers does not alter the charitable character of its activities. Such advertising seeks to extend relator's facilities to the greatest possible number of those members of the public in need of such facilities.

10. There is no such possibility of pecuniary profit to any of relator's officers and employees, for there is no evidence whatsoever from which it may be inferred that any officer or employee may lawfully be entitled to receive any pecuniary profit except reasonable compensation for services.

11. That relator did not apply for exemption until recently despite its consent to taxation over a period of more than fifty years is insufficient in and of itself to bar it from claiming exemption for future years. Relator felt that it was exempt but that it would assume some of the burdens of government as long as it had funds for carrying [***6] out its purposes. In recent years its viewpoint has changed because of an operating deficit. Since the taxing authorities would not grant exemption in the absence of a claim therefor, there has not been such practical construction in favor of taxation by the taxing authorities in the usual sense of the term as to deprive relator of that privilege. Moreover, the principle of practical construction applies only when there is ambiguity and here relator's right to exemption appears to be free from doubt.

12. Exemptions granted to relator by administrative agencies under different tax statutes are irrelevant in the determination of relator's present application.

 

2899 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


S. S. Kresge Co. v. New York


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


194 Misc. 645; 87 N.Y.S.2d 313; 1949 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1919



March 11, 1949 



DISPOSITION: The motion is granted. Settle order.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant city filed a motion for judgment on the pleadings in plaintiff tenant's action for a judgment declaring unconstitutional and void a resolution and map change adopted by the city, as well as N.Y. General City Law §§ 29 and 35, and New York City, N.Y., Charter, § 199.


OVERVIEW: The tenant operated a retail store under leases, pursuant to which it was authorized to demolish existing buildings and erect new buildings. The complaint alleged that following the adoption of the resolution, the tenant's application for a permit to erect a building on the street as it was before the mapped change was denied as contrary to N.Y. General City Law § 35. The court held that the tenant had the same rights and privileges as the owner of the premises; therefore, it had the right to appeal the denial of the permit under § 35. Therefore, the court found that unless and until the tenant applied to the board of standards and appeals for a permit and the application was denied, the court was without jurisdiction to determine whether there was a taking without just compensation.


OUTCOME: The court granted the city's motion for judgment on the pleadings.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- permit for building in bed of mapped street -- (1) lessee of property with power to demolish existing buildings and erect new ones is "owner" within General City Law (§ 35) and entitled to apply to board of standards and appeals for permit for building in street whose bed widened by resolution of board of estimate -- (2) whether resolution of city widening street by nine feet is unconstitutional taking of property not determinable until after plaintiff applies to board of standards and appeals for permit to erect building thereon and its application is denied.

1. A resolution of the Board of Estimate of the City of New York widened by nine feet the mapped widths of certain streets in the city of New York. Section 35 of the General City Law provides that for the purpose of preserving the integrity of the official map adopted by the board of estimate, no permit shall be issued for any building in the bed of any street shown on such map, provided, however, that if the land within such mapped street "is not yielding a fair return on its value to the [***2] owner" the board of standards and appeals shall have power to grant a permit for a building in such street. Although in words such section entitles only an "owner" to appeal to the board of standards and appeals, it was the intention of the Legislature to afford the remedy therein not only to persons who actually own the property involved, but also to those who have a substantial interest in it -- such as plaintiff who holds leases on certain property on such mapped streets which do not expire until 1986, and has authority to demolish the existing buildings and erect new buildings thereon -- and will sustain loss or damage if the property is taken without just compensation. Plaintiff, if not the owner, stands in the shoes of, and is entitled to the same rights and privileges as the owner.

2. The official map serves a useful purpose and does not, in and of itself, constitute an unconstitutional taking of property included within the mapped streets. Whether there is a taking of property depends upon the circumstances. Unless and until plaintiff applies to the board of standards and appeals for a permit to erect a building in the mapped streets as provided in section 35, and its [***3] application is denied, this court is without jurisdiction to determine, in an action for judgment declaring such resolution and map change unconstitutional, whether under the circumstances there has been a taking of the nine-foot strip without just compensation. Defendant's motion for judgment on the pleadings dismissing the complaint is granted.

 

2900 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People ex rel. Hollander v. Britt


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Erie County


195 Misc. 722; 92 N.Y.S.2d 662; 1949 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2874



March 7, 1949 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a habeas corpus proceeding brought on the relation of relator detainee to test the sufficiency of a warrant of extradition under which the detainee was taken into custody, the detainee claimed that the papers upon which the warrant was issued by the governor were insufficient in that the affidavit of the complaining witness was sworn to before the clerk of the circuit court and not before a judge or justice of the court.


OVERVIEW: The detainee was charged with the felony in Indiana. The issue in the habeas corpus proceeding was whether the clerk of the circuit court before whom the affidavit was sworn to was a magistrate in the sense in which that term was used in the federal extradition statute, 18 U.S.C.S. § 3182. The court dismissed the writ, holding that, in view of the powers and duties of the clerk of the circuit court under Indiana statutes, the clerk was a magistrate within the meaning of the federal statute and that affidavits made before him were sufficient as the basis of an extradition proceeding. The clerk was clothed with power as a public civil officer in connection with the issuance of warrants in criminal prosecutions. Because the prosecution was by affidavit in Indiana, the prosecuting attorney was empowered to determine whether to file the charge, and the clerk was authorized to issue the warrant thereon, in the absence of any direction by a court or judge. Under that procedure, the clerk functioned as the magistrate, issuing the warrant of arrest. The clerk was the only public official to whom the matter was presented by the prosecuting attorney in order to initiate the prosecution.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the writ of habeas corpus.


HEADNOTES

Extradition -- sufficiency of papers -- (1) in habeas corpus proceeding to test sufficiency of warrant of extradition, court may not consider matters relating to merits of criminal charge -- (2) affidavit in support of extradition papers, sworn to before "clerk of a Circuit Court" of Indiana, was sworn to before "magistrate" as required by Federal extradition statute -- (3) affidavit indorsed by prosecuting attorney was equivalent to information; extradition proceeding sustained on ground that felony prosecution instituted against relator in Indiana by information and requisition supported by copy of information; writ of habeas corpus dismissed -- (4) Code of Criminal Procedure (§ 830) providing for acceptance of information as equivalent of indictment constitutional.

1. In a habeas corpus proceeding wherein relator is seeking to test the sufficiency of a warrant of extradition charging him with having committed the crime of failing to support his minor children in the State of Indiana, the court has no power to consider matters relating to the merits of the criminal charge and the possible defenses. The court's jurisdiction is limited to [***2] the determination of whether the relator is charged with the commission of a crime under the law of the demanding State and whether he was present in that State at the time when the crime is alleged to have been committed there.

2. Section 3182 of title 18 of the United States Code provides that a requisition for the extradition of a person charged with being a fugitive from justice, must be accompanied by "a copy of an indictment found or an affidavit made before a magistrate". The term "magistrate" therein refers to any public officer who is regarded as a "magistrate" under the law of the demanding State. Upon an examination of the applicable Indiana law, it appears that the affidavit of the complaining witness on the basis of which extradition was sought, which was sworn to before the "clerk of a Circuit Court" and had indorsed thereon by the prosecuting attorney the words "approved by me" as required by the Indiana statute, was sworn to before a "magistrate" within the meaning of section 3182 of title 18 of the United States Code.

3. Moreover, under the applicable Indiana law the affidavit in question, since indorsed with the prosecuting attorney's approval, was in effect an [***3] information filed by the prosecuting attorney in lieu of an indictment by a grand jury and was the equivalent of an indictment. Accordingly, the extradition proceeding may be sustained upon the ground that a felony prosecution had been duly instituted against relator in Indiana by information and that the requisition was supported by a copy of the information. The writ of habeas corpus should be dismissed.

4. Section 830 of the Code of Criminal Procedure in providing for the acceptance of an information as the equivalent of an indictment is constitutional.

 

2901 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Consolidated Edison Co. v. Maltbie


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Albany County


193 Misc. 1015; 86 N.Y.S.2d 356; 1949 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1768



February 11, 1949 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner utility company filed a motion for a stay of the order sought to be reviewed pending a final determination of the matter under a N.Y. Civ. Prac. Act art. 78 proceeding to review the temporary rate fixed by respondent Public Service Commission of the State of New York (Commission) which would reduce the utility company's revenues from electric service.


OVERVIEW: The utility company filed a motion seeking the correction of the order of a temporary rate fixed by the Commission after a review, or an order annulling the order sought to be reviewed. The utility company also filed a motion for a stay of the order sought to be reviewed pending a final determination of the controversy. The Commission opposed the application for a temporary stay and asserted that the court was without authority to grant a stay. On appeal, the court denied the application for a stay because the court could not make a finding of "great and irreparable damage" as required by N.Y. Pub. Serv. Law § 23. The court found that the utility company had no permanent right to the rates in existence at the time that the order fixing new temporary rates went into effect. The Commission had the right to regulate the utility company's rates from time to time. The court reasoned that if the temporary rates were erroneous and the utility company had the right to recoupment of any loss due to error, in the fixing of the permanent rate, then the utility company could not suffer "great and irreparable damage" even if the order of the Commission was annulled.


OUTCOME: The court denied the application by the utility company for a stay of the order sought to be reviewed of a temporary rate fixed by the Commission pending a final determination of the controversy.


HEADNOTES

Public utilities -- stay of temporary rates -- (1) proceeding to review temporary rate for electric service fixed by Public Service Commission transferred to Appellate Division for determination on merits since temporary rate had genesis in hearing -- (2) Special Term may grant stay pending final order if finding made that "great and irreparable damage" would result (Public Service Law, § 23) -- (3) temporary stay cannot be granted in proceeding under Civil Practice Act (art. 78) solely on ground of alleged confiscatory rate -- (4) no damages to petitioner's financing resulted from temporary rate for period such rate in effect -- (5) since Special Term cannot make finding of "great and irreparable damage", application for stay denied.

1. A proceeding under article 78 of the Civil Practice Act to review a temporary rate fixed by the Public Service Commission which it is claimed will reduce the revenues of petitioner utility [***2] company from electric service must be transferred, pursuant to section 1296 of the Civil Practice Act, to the Appellate Division for a determination on the merits since the order sought to be reviewed had its genesis in a hearing on the question of rates.

2. Special Term has discretionary power to grant a stay pending a final order in this proceeding (Civ. Prac. Act, § 1299), but within the limitations of subdivision 2 of section 23 of the Public Service Law which provides that an order staying an order of the commission fixing any rate shall contain a specific finding that "great and irreparable damage" would otherwise result to petitioner.

3. Under the rule in this State which precludes a temporary injunction against enforcement of a temporary rate on the ground of confiscation, a temporary stay cannot be granted in a proceeding under article 78 of the Civil Practice Act for review, solely on the ground of an alleged confiscatory temporary rate. The law is settled that if a temporary rate order under review were to be annulled by the Appellate Division upon the merits, petitioner utility company would be entitled to, and it would be mandatory upon, respondent Public Service [***3] Commission to accord to petitioner, all of the benefits of the recoupment provisions of sect ion 114 of the Public Service Law for the entire period during which the temporary rates would have actually been in effect. Under such circumstances, there can be no "great and irreparable damage" to petitioner.

4. The contention that an adverse effect will be had on petitioner's financing during the period of the temporary stay which amounts to "great and irreparable damage" is overcome by the record of the sale of petitioner's securities since the temporary rate became effective.

5. Since Special Term cannot make a finding of "great and irreparable damage" under section 23 of the Public Service Law, the application for a stay must be denied.

 

2902 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Heller v. Department of Health


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


194 Misc. 96; 86 N.Y.S.2d 419; 1949 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1777



February 11, 1949 



DISPOSITION: The motion is granted. Settle order.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff brought an action against defendant city health department to declare that New York City, N.Y., Sanitary Code § 118-b, -c, -e were inconsistent with N.Y. Educ. Law § 6814 and were unconstitutional, void, and unenforceable. The department filed a motion for judgment on the pleadings.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff argued that the field covered by the provisions in New York City, N.Y., Sanitary Code § 118-b, -c, -e were fully covered in N.Y. Educ. Law § 6814 in such a manner as to produce an inconsistency and were an invalid exercise of the city's legislative power. The department moved for a judgment on the pleadings, contending that the conditions of health hazard that distinguished New York City, N.Y., Sanitary Code § 118-b, -c, -e from those provided in N.Y. Educ. § 6814 were established in the pleadings. The court granted the motion, holding that the department was entitled to a judgment on the pleadings because the city was empowered to adopt additional health regulations than those provided for by the State of New York where there was a real distinction between the city and other parts of the State and where the pleadings had established those distinctions.


OUTCOME: The court granted the department's motion.


HEADNOTES

Local laws -- inconsistency with statute -- (1) judgment declaring Sanitary Code of City of New York (§§ 118-b, 118-c and 118-e) not inconsistent with Education Law (§ 6814) granted in favor of defendant health department -- (2) moreover, pleadings established different conditions in municipality justifying adoption of additional regulations.

1. In an action to declare sections 118-b, 118-c and 118-e of the Sanitary Code of the City of New York inconsistent with section 6814 of the State Education Law [entitled "Barbiturate and other hypnotic and somnifacient drugs"] and void, judgment on the pleadings is granted in favor of defendant department of health. The field covered by the code provisions, though apparently fully covered by the statute, is not inconsistent with it in the light of the serious health hazards which the code is intended to ameliorate. The code provisions provide for successive prescriptions to enable the procurement of that which the Education Law permits.

2. Moreover, the pleadings establish a distinction between conditions in the city and other [***2] parts of the State, empowering the municipality to adopt additional health regulations in spite of general regulations by the State.

 

2903 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Robinson v. Broome County


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Trial Term, Broome County


195 Misc. 24; 87 N.Y.S.2d 501; 1949 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1951



February 5, 1949 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff taxpayers filed a complaint against defendant county in order to enjoin the county from acting pursuant to 1930 N.Y. Laws ch. 197, 1936 N.Y. Laws ch. 595, 1940 N.Y. Laws ch. 619 or 1946 N.Y. Laws ch. 200, which gave a town within the county the permission to withdraw from a county public welfare unit.


OVERVIEW: Legislation was passed on a non-local basis permitting towns of a certain size to withdraw from a county public welfare unit. The taxpayers filed a complaint to enjoin action by the county pursuant to such legislation. The court dismissed the complaint. The court first held that the fact that the legislation was only applicable to one town by the town size definition in the legislation did not make the legislation purely a local law and therefore the law was not invalid for failure to pursue a local enactment. The court held that the law was still general. The court concluded that a later amendment of the original law by re-enactment did not constitute a new law but was a continuation of the existence of the former law under the general construction rules of N.Y. Gen. Constr. Law § 95.


OUTCOME: The court entered judgment in favor of the county, dismissing the complaint.

 

2904 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Larkin v. Consolidated Tel. & Electrical Subway Co.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


193 Misc. 1001; 85 N.Y.S.2d 631; 1949 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1683



January 10, 1949 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff employee filed a motion to strike out five separate defenses that defendant employer brought against the complaint that was filed by the employee to recover back wages pursuant to New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code § 83-4.0.


OVERVIEW: The employee claimed that the employer had not lived up to its agreement to pay him the prevailing scale of union wages. The employee brought a complaint against the employer to recover back wages pursuant to New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code § 83-4.0. The employer responded to the complaint by asserting five defenses. The employee filed a motion to strike out the five defenses upon the ground that the defenses were insufficient in law. The employee attacked the first defense because he argued that § 83-4.0 was invalid for vagueness and for lack of definiteness as to what was a prevailing scale of wages. The court found that the first defense was sufficient at law because the employer paid the employee the prevailing scale of union wages within the letter and spirit of § 83-4.0. Finally, the court found that the remaining four defenses were also sufficient in law. The court issued a judgment that denied the employee's motion to strike.


OUTCOME: The court denied the employee's motion to strike out five separate defenses that the employer brought against the complaint that was filed by the employee to recover back wages.


HEADNOTES

Master and servant -- prevailing rate of wages -- (1) Administrative Code of City of New York (§ 83-4.0) provides that as condition to issuance of permit to open street, permittee must agree to employ only skilled workmen to whom "prevailing scale of union wages" shall be paid; in action by workman to recover difference between wage paid him, as fixed by union bargaining contract, and "prevailing scale of union wages" for six years prior to commencement of action, complaint grounded on statute and third party beneficiary agreement between city and permittee -- (2) defense that collective bargaining contract between plaintiff's union and defendant was method of defining "prevailing scale of union wages" under statute and payment of such wages was compliance with section 83-4.0, sufficient -- (3) defense that plaintiff waived benefit of statute by acceptance of wages under collective bargaining agreement without protest, sufficient -- (4) defense that, insofar as section 83-4.0 and permits thereunder require higher scale than scale fixed by collective bargaining contracts, statute and permits are unconstitutional, sufficient  [***2] -- (5) defense that section 83-4.0 if so construed is void as vague, sufficient -- (6) defense that construction of section 83-4.0 which would give plaintiff right to higher wages than other employees would unjustly discriminate in his favor sufficient.

1. Section 83-4.0 of the Administrative Code of the City of New York provides, as a condition to the issuance of a permit to open a street, that the person seeking the permit agree with the city to employ only skilled and competent workmen to whom shall be paid "the prevailing scale of union wages". In an action by a workman to recover the difference between the wages paid him under a union's collective bargaining contract with defendant employer and such "prevailing scale of union wages", for a period of six years prior to the commencement of the action, it is found that the statute and the agreement between the permittee and the city make the employee the beneficiary thereof and that the complaint is grounded on the statute and this third party beneficiary agreement.

2. The statute does not define what constitutes the "prevailing scale of union wages" and no method is provided to ascertain and determine it. Since vague, the statute [***3] is subject to attack, but in view of the purpose of the statute to aid labor, the Legislature must have intended that the parties affected would themselves employ such legal methods as would provide a standard to define the term. Here defendant and the union of which plaintiff was a member, which union was certified by the National Labor Relations Board as the sole bargaining agent, entered into a contract fixing a union scale. Arbitration awards growing out of such contracts were approved by governmental agencies. Such collective bargaining contracts, arbitration awards, decisions and directive orders of the governmental agencies, have established the union scale of wages. Payment there of constituted payment by defendant of the "prevailing scale of union wages" within section 83-4.0 of the Administrative Code and was compliance therewith. The first defense in defendant's answer which alleged the making of the collective bargaining contracts and payment in accordance therewith, is sufficient in law.

3. A defense which alleged that plaintiff's acceptance during the six-year period, of the wages paid him pursuant to the collective bargaining contracts without protest constituted a [***4] waiver of plaintiff's right to additional compensation is sufficient in law. Plaintiff could voluntarily waive the benefit of the statute if he chose to do so and accept a lesser compensation than provided thereby. Section 83-4.0 does not prohibit the employee from exercising his right of waiver. Whether by his acts and conduct plaintiff intended to waive any right to extra compensation presents a mixed question of law and fact for determination on the trial.

4. A defense states that, insofar as section 83-4.0 and permits issued thereunder require the payment of a scale of wages that is higher than the scale fixed by the collective bargaining contract, the statute and the permits are invalid as contravening the public policy expressed by section 17 of article I of the State Constitution, which recognizes the right of collective bargaining, and that they are inconsistent with the National and State Labor Relations Acts. Such defense is sufficient. The right of collective bargaining is recognized by the State Constitution, by article 20 of the State Labor Law and by title 29 of the United States Code (§§ 151-159). Section 83-4.0 of the Administrative Code may not be interpreted to override [***5] the wages fixed by the collective bargaining contract as the proper and lawful wages.

5. A defense that section 83-4.0 and permits issued thereunder are unenforcible if interpreted to require the payment of higher wages than that fixed by the collective bargaining agreements since the statute and permits are too vague to be intelligible, is sufficient.

6. A defense that the statute, and permits issued thereunder are arbitrary and discriminatory, if interpreted to require defendant to pay higher wages than that fixed by the collective bargaining contracts made by the defendant and plaintiff's union, is sufficient. To interpret section 83-4.0 as overriding the wage determined by the collective bargaining contracts is to confer upon plaintiff and others similarly situated a preferred status and to deprive the other employees of collective bargaining rights granted to them by the Constitution and the labor relations acts.

 

2905 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Leighton v. Roper


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Monroe County


194 Misc. 893; 87 N.Y.S.2d 527; 1948 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3940



December 29, 1948 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff passenger filed an action against defendants, her husband and a driver's administrator, to recover damages for injuries she sustained in an automobile accident. The administrator filed a motion to dismiss the complaint on the ground that a personal action in the New York courts could not be maintained against an administrator appointed by a court of another state in the absence of property in New York.


OVERVIEW: The passenger was riding in an automobile driven by her husband. The car collided with the driver's vehicle. The driver was a resident of another state. The wife claimed that he injuries were caused by the negligence of the husband and driver. She filed an action against them to recover damages. The driver died, and his administrator was substituted as a defendant. He filed a motion to dismiss the complaint. The court held that N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 52 removed the case from the application of the rule that a personal action in the New York courts could be maintained against the administrator, who was appointed by a court of another state, in the absence of property in New York. A nonresident driver who had no property in the State consented to the rendering of a personal judgment against him pursuant to § 52 by entering the State with his motor vehicle. Under § 52, the operation of a motor vehicle within New York by a nonresident was deemed a signification of his agreement that any summons served upon the secretary of state would be of the same legal force and validity as if served on him personally within the State.


OUTCOME: The court denied the administrator's motion to dismiss the complaint against him.

 

2906 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People ex rel. Pinello v. Leadbitter


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Dutchess County


194 Misc. 481; 85 N.Y.S.2d 287; 1948 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3766



December 21, 1948 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Relator barber filed an habeas corpus proceeding, contending that Poughkeepsie, N.Y. Ordinance ch. 48, § 9(10) was violative of both the federal and New York constitutional prohibitions against depriving a person of liberty and property without due process of law.


OVERVIEW: The barber was charged with violating Poughkeepsie, N.Y. Ordinance ch. 48, § 9(10) by carrying on his trade before 8:30 o'clock in the morning, which was the time set for the opening of business in the ordinance. The barber claimed that the ordinance violated the constitutional prohibitions against depriving a person of liberty and property without due process of law. The court held that § 9(10) was arbitrary, unreasonable, unconstitutional, and invalid. The regulation was not designed as a health measure to protect the public, proprietors, or employees engaged in the barbering trade, or to facilitate sanitary inspections. It was designed to protect certain members of the trade from lawful competition. The ordinance did not tend to promote or preserve public health, morals, comfort or welfare but was an arbitrary and unwarranted interference with the barber's business. Hours of opening and closing of barbershops did not have a reasonable relation to the public health and safety, and it did not facilitate adequate inspection of the barbershops. Opportunity existed without absolute closing of barbershops for reasonable inspection.


OUTCOME: The court sustained the writ of habeas corpus and ordered the barber released from custody.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- ordinance regulating hours for barbering -- (1) ordinance restricting hours between which barbering might be carried on unconstitutional -- (2) habeas corpus proper to test constitutionality -- (3) presumption of constitutionality overcome by adequate proof.

1. An ordinance of a city which seeks to regulate the hours during which barbers might carry on their trade (not earlier than 8:30 A.M. or later than 6:30 P.M.) is an unconstitutional invasion of the right to earn a living and a denial of due process. It has no relation to the public health. The public will be just as healthy whether a shop opens or closes at the hours specified in the regulation or at some other reasonable time convenient to the trade. The regulation does not facilitate adequate inspection of barbershops thus inuring to the public health. Any regulation compelling the opening or closing of barbershops between certain hours because it will be inconvenient for the authorities to inspect them -- when they are open at other hours amply sufficient for such inspection -- is an unnecessary and unreasonable interference with the operation of a lawful [***2] trade.

2. Since relator was arrested and charged with a violation of the ordinance, habeas corpus may be resorted to in advance of trial and judgment to determine the question of the constitutionality of the ordinance.

3. The presumption of constitutionality of the ordinance was overcome by proof that it did not contribute to the public welfare, that professional, business and traveling men and tourists required barbering service outside the restricted hours and that it resulted in a financial loss to certain barbers.

 

2907 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Lewis v. Spaulding


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Albany County


193 Misc. 66; 85 N.Y.S.2d 682; 1948 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3842



November 12, 1948 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner resident brought a proceeding under N.Y. Civ. Prac. Act art. 78 against respondents, Commissioner of Education of the State of New York and a city board of education, for order directing discontinuance of the practice of releasing children from school attendance for purpose of religious instruction.


OVERVIEW: The resident sought discontinuance of the practice popularly known as the "Released Time", whereby public school students in the New York were released from regular attendance for the purpose of religious instruction. The resident argued that the practice violated the First Amendment requirement of separation of church and State. The court concluded that (1) the constitutionality of a released time program was to be tested by a consideration of the factual aspects of the particular program under scrutiny; (2) the released time program as operated by the city entailed no use of the school buildings for the religious instruction, nor was there any expenditure of public funds for that purpose; (3) the programs were not in aid of religion.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the resident's petition against the Commissioner of Education of the State of New York and a city board of education, seeking an order directing discontinuance of the practice of releasing children from school attendance for purpose of religious instruction.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- released time from public school for religious instruction -- (1) public school programs of released time for religious instruction of pupils as practiced in New York State not in aid of religion and not unconstitutional -- (2) constitutionality tested by factual aspects of program under scrutiny -- (3) prior action challenging legality of similar program not res judicata.

1. Pursuant to statutory authority (now Education Law, § 3210, subd. 1, par. b), the State Commissioner of Education adopted regulations permitting the absence of pupils from school during school hours for religious observance and education. Thereafter the board of education of the city of New York promulgated rules governing so-called "released time" for religious education providing in part that there would be no announcement in the public schools relative to any program for religious instruction initiated by any religious group; that any religious organization might [***2] issue to its enrolled pupils a card countersigned by the parent requesting the release of the pupil from school for religious instruction at a specific location; that such card should be filed in the office of the public school; that the religious organization, in co-operation with the parents, would assume responsibility for attendance at the religious center and would file with the school principal, weekly, a card attendance record; that upon presentation of a proper request pupils might be dismissed from the public school for the last hour of the day's session on one day of each week; that the dismissal at that time would be in the usual way and school authorities would have no responsibility beyond that assumed in regular dismissals and that there should be no comment by principal or teacher on the attendance or nonattendance of any pupil upon religious instruction. This released time program as practiced in the city of New York is generally typical of plans throughout the State -- no use is made of school buildings for religious instruction and there is no expenditure of public funds for that purpose. This is not a program in aid of religion in violation of the First Amendment [***3] to the United States Constitution (as made applicable to the States by the Fourteenth Amendment) prohibiting the making of any law respecting an establishment of religion.

2. "Released time" is not per se unconstitutional. The constitutionality of such a program must be tested by the factual aspects of the particular programunder scrutiny (see Illinois ex rel. McCollum v. Board of Education of School Dist. No. 71 Champaign Co., 333 U.S. 203, 92 L. Ed. 649, 68 S. Ct. 461).

3. Plaintiff, who in the present action demands an order directing the school officials to discontinue the practice of releasing children from regular school attendance to permit them to receive religious instruction, had instituted a prior action against the Commissioner of Education of the State of New York challenging the constitutionality of a released time program as operated in the city of White Plains in 1926, but his claim was therein rejected (People ex rel. Lewis v. Graves, 245 N. Y. 195, 156 N.E. 663). The matter is not res judicata as to the Commissioner of Education of the State of New York.

 

2908 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Potter v. Emerol Mfg. Co.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court, Special Term, New York County, Part III


84 N.Y.S.2d 356; 1948 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3577



November 10, 1948 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff employees sued defendant company to recover bonuses and commissions that they claim were due to them under an incentive sales program allegedly adopted by the company. The company moved to dismiss the complaint.


OVERVIEW: The company contended that complaint did not state a cause of action, that the contract upon which it was founded was void and unenforceable under the statute of frauds, and that the action was barred by a judgment in a prior action. The employees had brought an action upon the same bonuses and commissions that was dismissed upon the company's demurrer under N.Y. R. Civ. Prac. 107 because they could not produce a writing sufficient to satisfy the statute of frauds. The court found that the complaint at issue was sufficient upon its face because the employees produced a written contract. It concluded that the judgment dismissing the prior action did not bar the employees' new action. The prior decision upon the company's motion to dismiss was not a final adjudication and did not support its plea of res judicata.


OUTCOME: The court denied the motion.

 

2909 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Wong v. Finkelstein


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Queens County


193 Misc. 289; 84 N.Y.S.2d 631; 1948 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3641



November 10, 1948 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner landlord applied pursuant to N.Y. Civ. Prac. Act § 78 for the court's review of the decision by respondents, the members of a temporary city housing rent commission of the City of New York. Respondents refused to grant the landlord a certificate of eviction for her rental unit based on her good faith desire to withdraw from the rental market the unit that her current tenant occupied.


OVERVIEW: The landlord sought review of respondents' denial of her application for a certificate of eviction to allow her to withdraw her rental unit from the rental market. The landlord's application was based on the federal Housing and Rent Act of 1948 § 204(8), which permitted the petitioned withdrawal. Respondents contended that they lacked the authority to grant the certificate under New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code § U41-7.0, that provided that no tenant could be evicted unless under one of the grounds specified in § U41-7.0. The court granted the landlord's application based on its determination that the local law conflicted with the Act and prohibited the landlord's right to withdraw his property from the rental market, a right that the Act permitted. The court found that the local law was also invalid because it compelled landlords to rent against their will, while the Act provided that they were not required to do so. The court found that the clear and plain direction of Congress that property could not be withdrawn form the rental market could not be nullified by respondents as local authorities, who were required to recognize the Act as the supreme law.


OUTCOME: The court granted the landlord's application.


HEADNOTES

Landlord and tenant -- rent regulation -- municipal regulation restricting evictions -- New York City local law invalid insofar as landlord denied right to certificate of eviction for purpose of withdrawing space from rental market as permitted by Federal rent act.

Subdivision (a) of section 209 of the Federal Housing and Rent Act of 1947, as amended in 1948, permits a landlord to withdraw housing accommodations from the rental market. New York City Local Law No. 66 of 1947 (Administrative Code, § U41-7.0) declares that except for nonpayment of rent, no tenant shall be evicted unless the temporary city housing rent commission shall certify the existence of at least one of four grounds specified in the local law. Withdrawal of housing accommodations from the rental market is not such a ground. Institution of an eviction proceeding without first obtaining a certificate pursuant to such local law is a misdemeanor. Such local law is invalid. It conflicts with the Federal statute insofar as it omits the right to withdraw property from the rental market and with the express policy of Congress contained in section 302 [***2] of the Housing and Rent Act of 1948 that "Nothing in this Act * * * shall be construed to require any person to offer any housing accommodations for rent". The local law does not supplement the Federal policy but conflicts with it. It compels landlords to rent against their will, while the Federal law says they need not do so. The act of Congress is the law of the land and must be recognized by the temporary city housing rent commission. Accordingly, petitioner's application to compel the commission to issue a certificate of eviction to petitioner who seeks to evict a tenant for the purpose of withdrawing the space from the rental market is granted.

 

2910 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


M & M Transp. Co. v. New York


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court, Special Term, New York County, Part III


84 N.Y.S.2d 128; 1948 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3520



November 4, 1948 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant city moved pursuant to N.Y. R. Civ. Prac. 212 for an order to dismiss plaintiff taxpayer's complaint in its declaratory judgment action in which it sought a determination that it was not subject to taxation under the New York City Business Tax Law (New York) and the regulations promulgated under it.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayer was incorporated in a foreign state and was engaged in the business of transporting commodities in interstate commerce. The taxpayer maintained terminals in several locations and states, including one located in the city. The taxpayer did not engage in intrastate commerce. The city sought a determination that the taxpayer was subject to taxation under the New York City Business Tax Law and regulations promulgated by the city's comptroller. The court found that the taxpayer was subject to taxation and granted the city's motion to dismiss the complaint. The portion of the taxpayer's business in interstate commerce that bore a special relation to the city was subject to the tax. The tax was not repugnant to the Commerce Clause or the Due Process Clause of the United States Constitution because no burden was placed on interstate commerce and the taxing scheme provided for the apportionment of tax attributable to the taxpayer's activities within the city. The taxpayer was entitled to seek declaratory relief despite the remedies in the taxing statute based on its constitutional challenges to the statute and its challenge to the applicability of the statute.


OUTCOME: The court determined that the taxpayer was subject to the city's tax law and granted the city's motion to dismiss the taxpayer's complaint.

 

2911 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Sinclair v. Byrne


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Municipal Court of the City of New York, Borough of The Bronx


193 Misc. 1; 83 N.Y.S.2d 629; 1948 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3399



October 28, 1948 



DISPOSITION: The petition is therefore dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner landlord brought a summary proceeding to recover possession of the first floor apartment of a two-family dwelling. The landlord questioned the constitutionality of a local law in the light of the Federal Housing and Rent Act of 1947, 50 U.S.C.S. app. § 1881 et seq. The attorney for the temporary city housing rent commission was invited by the court to appear as amicus curiae, and he so appeared.


OVERVIEW: The landlord desired the apartment for himself. The landlord contended that, in a proceeding under § 209(a)(5) of the Federal Housing and Rent Act of 1947, New York City, N.Y., Local Law No. 66 (1947) and the rules issued thereunder by the temporary city housing rent commission were inapplicable, and if applicable, were unconstitutional. The landlord asserted that he was not required to obtain a certificate under the local laws because the federal law had pre-empted the field where the basis of the eviction was the landlord's intent to withdraw the housing accommodations from the rental market. The landlord urged that the local law, in failing to give the landlord a right comparable to that given by the Federal act permitting a landlord to withdraw property from the rental market, conflicted with and, therefore, had to defer to the supreme law of the land. The court held that the legislation, insofar as it regulated evictions sought on the ground of personal use, came within the permissible local legislation concurrent with and supplementary to the federal law, and was not in conflict with the latter nor in a field of action pre-empted by the Congress.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the landlord's petition.


HEADNOTES

Landlord and tenant -- rent regulation -- municipal regulation restricting evictions -- (1) since landlord testified that he sought apartment for personal use, case not proper to test constitutionality of local law restricting evictions, in light of provision of Federal Housing and Rent Act of 1947 (§ 209, subd. [a], par. [5], as amd.) permitting landlord to withdraw space from market; contemplated personal use of apartment not withdrawal of space from market within Federal act -- (2) local law requiring landlord to obtain certificate prior to eviction proceeding if he seeks possession for himself not in conflict with Federal law -- (3) failure of landlord to obtain such certificate not reviewable by Municipal Court of City of New York.

1. Landlord alleges in his petition that he seeks possession of tenant's apartment in good faith to withdraw it from the rental market pursuant to paragraph (5) of subdivision (a) of section 209 of the Federal Housing and Rent Act of 1947 (as amd. in 1948). Landlord further alleges that he sought a certificate of eviction from [***2] the temporary city housing rent commission on that ground which was denied because not based on any of the grounds set forth in Local Law No. 66 of 1947 of the City of New York for the issuance of such certificate. On the trial, landlord testified that he desired tenant's apartment for himself because he intended to marry. Because of such testimony, this is not a proper test case to declare said local law -- which requires landlord to obtain a certificate, before instituting an eviction proceeding, from the temporary city housing rent commission where he seeks possession for personal use -- invalid and unconstitutional insofar as a proceeding under paragraph (5) of subdivision (a) of section 209 of the Federal Housing and Rent Act of 1947 (as amd.) is concerned. Although when a landlord occupies his own property, it is not rented and it is thereby withdrawn from the rental market, such a situation may not be considered an instance where landlord has withdrawn the premises from the rental market within the meaning of the act. The petition in summary proceedings to recover possession of the apartment is dismissed.

2. The local legislation insofar as it regulates evictions sought [***3] on the ground of personal use comes within permissible local legislation concurrent with and supplementary to the Federal Law, is not in conflict with the latter, and is not in a field of action pre-empted by Congress.

3. Since landlord has not succeeded in obtaining a certificate from the temporary city housing rent commission pursuant to the local law, the Municipal Court of the City of New York may not review its determination.

 

2912 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Old Westbury v. Foster


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Nassau County


193 Misc. 47; 83 N.Y.S.2d 148; 1948 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3308



October 11, 1948 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff village sought an injunction to restrain defendant, a professional dog handler, from carrying on a business in a portion of the village zoned for residential purposes. The dog handler alleged that the ordinance as applied to him was unreasonable and confiscatory and, therefore, unconstitutional and that the zoning commission appointed by the board of trustees failed to comply with the provisions of N.Y. Village Law § 179-a.


OVERVIEW: The dog handler's principal occupation was that of showing pedigreed dogs at dog shows. As a part of his business, the dog handler received dogs into his kennels, maintained them and conditioned them for show purposes. In addition, he owned one or more male dogs which he used as a stud at his kennels. The dog handler attacked the constitutionality of the ordinance and questioned the legality of the enactment, attacking the regularity of the proceedings taken preliminary to the adoption of the ordinance. The court found that the village endeavored to maintain a rural atmosphere in the development of the village and that the ordinance imposed to carry out such purpose was not unreasonable or arbitrary. The court concluded that any depreciation in value of the dog handler's property by reason of the ordinance did not render the ordinance invalid nor did the fact that the property could be more profitably used for business purposes make the ordinance confiscatory and unconstitutional. The court also found that the ordinance was legally adopted, that all preliminary requirements in connection with its adoption were complied with, and that the ordinance was in full force and effect.


OUTCOME: The court entered judgment for the village.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- zoning -- (1) zoning ordinance prohibiting business in section of village zoned residential valid where character of section mainly residential; village entitled to enjoin professional dog handler from continuing his business in such zone -- (2) court will not substitute its judgment for that of legislative body even where determination is debatable -- (3) depreciation in value of property by reason of zoning restrictions does not render ordinance invalid -- (4) ordinance legally adopted.

1. A zoning ordinance enacted by a village prohibiting the carrying on of a business in a section of the village zoned for residential purposes is not arbitrary, unreasonable or confiscatory where it appears that the property along the highway -- on which the property of defendant, a professional dog handler who maintains kennels on his premises, is located -- is rather rural and its character mainly residential, and that there is no appreciable demand among the residents of the village for defendant's services as a professional dog handler. Plaintiff village is entitled to an injunction restraining [***2] defendant from carrying on his business of a professional dog handler in such residential zone.

2. Even where a determination on the part of the board of trustees to keep the village practically free from business is fairly debatable, the court will not substitute its judgment for that of the legislative body charged with the primary duty of determining the question.

3. Depreciation in value of the property of an individual by reason of the restrictions placed upon it by zoning regulations does not render an ordinance invalid. Nor does the fact that the property might be more profitably used for industrial or business purposes, make the ordinance confiscatory and unconstitutional.

4. The ordinance was legally adopted where the proceedings taken by the zoning commission were regular and in conformity with section 179-a of the Village Law as to notice of public hearings and the filing of a preliminary and final report. In the absence of a definite requirement of the Village Law as to what notice of the public hearings shall be given, the minutes of the board of trustees noting that two hearings were held sufficiently show a compliance by the zoning commission with the statutory [***3] requirement for the holding of such hearings.

 

2913 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Aldrich


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Queens County


192 Misc. 971; 83 N.Y.S.2d 129; 1948 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3303



October 6, 1948 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After defendant was convicted of attempted robbery in the third degree committed as a second offense upon his guilty plea, the State filed a motion to increase the duration of defendant's sentence.


OVERVIEW: Defendant pled guilty to attempted robbery in the third degree committed as a second offense. Upon his conviction, the court imposed a minimum sentence of two and one-half years. The State filed a motion to increase the minimum duration of the sentence to five years, claiming that the court's minimum sentence was illegal. The court held that: (1) N.Y. Penal Law § 1941 imposed no mandatory maximum sentence, only a limitation on the discretion of the sentencing judge in fixing the term of an indefinite sentence; and (2) insofar as § 1941 purported to, as claimed by the State, impose a harsher penalty on offenders who merely attempted their crimes than on those who actually consummated them, the section was meaningless and indefinite.


OUTCOME: The court denied the State's motion.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- sentence -- (1) defendant, found guilty of attempted robbery in third degree committed as second felony, properly sentenced to term of from two and one-half years to five years -- (2) portion of Penal Law (§ 1941) providing that minimum sentence for second felony offender shall in no case be less than five years unconstitutional.

1. Section 1941 of the Penal Law provides that a second felony offender shall be sentenced "to imprisonment for an indeterminate term, the minimum of which shall be not less than one-half of the longest term prescribed upon a first conviction, and the maximum of which shall be not longer than twice such longest term; provided,  [***2] however, that the minimum sentence imposed hereunder upon such second or third felony offender shall in no case be less than five years". Section 2192 of the Penal Law provides that when a crime is declared punishable "by not more than" a specified number of years, the court is authorized to pronounce judgment upon conviction in its discretion for any time less than that prescribed by the provisions of the Penal Law. Defendant, who was convicted on his plea of guilty to attempted robbery in the third degree committed as a second offense, could have been given a maximum sentence of five years (Penal Law, § 2129, § 261, subd. 2), and under section 1941 of the Penal Law, the court properly imposed a sentence of two and one-half years to five years. Such sentence is a legal sentence for the reason that the maximum sentence, which is only a discretionary term and not a fixed term, could be five years and, therefore, one half of this as a minimum sentence could be two and one-half years.

2. Section 1941 of the Penal Law in respect to the following words: "provided, however, that the minimum sentence imposed hereunder upon such second or third felony offender shall in no case be less [***3] than five years" is unconstitutional when read with the balance of the section. The words are meaningless and indefinite.

 

2914 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Hirsch v. Albany Sav. Bank


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


City Court of New York, Albany


192 Misc. 505; 81 N.Y.S.2d 253; 1948 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2748



July 26, 1948 



DISPOSITION: Motion of the third party defendants is granted, with $ 3 costs, and the third party summons set aside.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Third party defendants filed a motion to set aside the service of a third party summons by third party plaintiff.


OVERVIEW: The third party plaintiff had previously been given the right to plead over upon service of proper summons and complaint. In their motion, the third party defendants argued that N.Y. Civ. Prac. Act § 193-a and N.Y. R. Civ. Prac. 54 did not apply to actions in the court. Further, if they were so construed, they would have been in contravention of N.Y. Const. art. VI, § 18 because this would be an attempt to confer equity jurisdiction upon an inferior court. Reluctantly, the court granted the motion, holding that the New York Legislature did not intend to extend N.Y. Civ. Prac. Act § 193-a governing third party practice to actions in the inferior courts.


OUTCOME: The motion was granted.


HEADNOTES

Parties -- third party practice -- third party practice provision of Civil Practice Act (§ 193-a) inapplicable to actions in Albany City Court.
