The Legislature did not intend to extend the third party practice provisions of the Civil Practice Act (§ 193-a) to actions in the Albany City Court because the provisions of rule 54 of the Rules of Civil Practice and section 193-a of the Civil Practice Act with regard to twenty days for an answer are in conflict with the six to twelve days allowed in City Court, because of the absence of any express provision making the section applicable to lower courts, and because the Legislature omitted from section 193-a the clause which was inserted in section 193 that the provisions of the section shall be applicable to all actions whether formerly denominated legal or equitable. (Hirsch v. Albany Savings  [***2] Bank, 191 Misc. 415, 81 N.Y.S.2d 250, overruled.)

 

2915 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Krantz v. Amherst


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Trial Term, Erie County


192 Misc. 912; 80 N.Y.S.2d 812; 1948 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2667



July 23, 1948 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff owners filed an action against defendant town seeking a judgment enjoining their arrest or prosecution or any interference with them in respect to excavation or removal of topsoil from their real property or interference with their business under the authority of Amherst, N.Y., Building Code § XXII (1943).


OVERVIEW: The owners were engaged in removing topsoil from their lands. Amherst, N.Y., Zoning Ordinance § 9(2) provided that farmland could not be used for any other purpose than agriculture. The owners claimed that by the enforcement of the ordinance they were prevented from fulfilling contracts for delivery of topsoil. They claimed that the ordinance was invalid as confiscatory and without due process of law. The court held that § 9(2) was constitutional, reasonable, and valid. The ordinance, while it regulated strictly, was not confiscatory. It provided a formula that would permit the owners' business activities to the point where they invaded or seriously threatened to invade the welfare rights of the community as to health, safety, and general values. The inability of the owners to fulfill contracts was not a compelling objection. There was no convincing showing that contracts could not be fulfilled. The ordinance was a valid exercise of the police power as specifically amplified by 1941 N.Y. Laws 674. It was general in its terms, it was applicable to all alike, and it was directed to a valid purpose to serve broadly public welfare. The ordinance was in no sense capricious.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the complaint.


HEADNOTES

Injunctions -- removal of topsoil -- (1) Zoning Ordinance of Town of Amherst provides that farm land may not be used for purpose other than agriculture; plaintiffs' business of removing topsoil from farm lands for sale violates ordinance -- (2) Building Code (§ XXII) regulates removal of topsoil; such regulation not confiscatory -- (3) esthetic considerations support such legislation -- (4) inability to fulfill contracts not objection; only cost of performance increased by section XXII -- (5) ordinance is valid exercise of police power -- (6) ordinance not capricious or discriminatory -- (7) since town showed no damage, counterclaim dismissed.

1. Subdivision 2 of section 9 of the Zoning Ordinance of the Town of Amherst provides that farm land may not be used for any other purpose than agriculture. The business of certain plaintiffs of removing topsoil from their farm lands for resale is not farming and is in violation of such zoning ordinance.

2. Section XXII of the Building Code of said town regulates the removal of topsoil. Such ordinance, while it regulates strictly, is not confiscatory. It does not constitute a general and complete prohibition [***2] of the use of property. It provides a formula, the observance of which, within minimum standards, will permit the business activities of the plaintiffs to the point where they invade or seriously threaten to invade the welfare rights of the community as to health, safety and general values.

3. Esthetic considerations are a proper factor and support the legislation.

4. The inability of plaintiffs to fulfill contracts is not a compelling objection. There is no convincing showing that contracts cannot be fulfilled, only that the profit therefrom may be lessened or eliminated. An ordinance does not become unreasonable by reason of the fact solely that it increases the expense of a business and consequently increases the cost of the product of a business. This ordinance of necessity increases the cost of the topsoil business and where permits are granted, it will increase the price of topsoil produced thereunder. This increase, however, is not such as to constitute confiscation and does not exceed a reasonable charge for regulation and enforcement.

5. The ordinance is a valid exercise of the police power. Unless expressly restricted, the police power may be held to include any [***3] reasonably prudent legislative program indicated as essential to the growing needs of a forward looking community. The police power is widely embracing, and constitutionality is to be presumed. The complaint in this action to enjoin defendants from interfering with plaintiffs' businesses is dismissed.

6. The ordinance is not capricious and discriminatory. The legislation is general in its terms and is applicable to all alike.

7. Since the town has offered no adequate proof of damage from the operations of plaintiffs to support the counterclaim of the answer, the counterclaim is dismissed.

 

2916 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People ex rel. Ackalitis v. Foster


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Cayuga County


193 Misc. 634; 83 N.Y.S.2d 898; 1948 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3472



July 21, 1948 



DISPOSITION: Writ dismissed. Let order enter accordingly.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Relator inmate was convicted of feloniously possessing a machine gun, a violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 1897, subd. 1-a, and was sentenced to a term of imprisonment of not less than seven years, nor more than 14 years. His conviction and sentence were affirmed on appeal. The inmate then filed a petition for a writ of habeas corpus.


OVERVIEW: The police raided a building and found four machine guns therein. The inmate was in the building at the time of the raid. He was subsequently convicted of possessing a machine gun in violation of § 1897, subd. 1-a, which provided, inter alia, that the presence of a machine gun in any room, dwelling, structure, or vehicle constituted presumptive evidence of its illegal possession by all persons occupying the place where the gun was found. In his petition for habeas relief, the inmate claimed that the presumption created by § 1897, subd. 1-a, deprived him of due process of the law and was thus violative of U.S. Const. amend. XIV. The court denied the petition, noting that the New York Court of Appeals had determined in a prior case that the presumption did not violate the Fourteenth Amendment. The court also noted that it did not have jurisdiction in a habeas corpus proceeding to determine the question of whether the inmate's due process rights had been violated. Rather, the court found that the inmate's remedy for such a violation was to file a motion to vacate the judgment of conviction in the court that entered it.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the writ.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- possession of machine gun -- (1) subdivision 1-a of section 1897 of Penal Law which provides that presence of machine gun in any place is presumptive evidence of illegal possession thereof by person occupying such place is constitutional -- (2) habeas corpus not proper remedy to attack conviction on ground of denial of due process; motion to vacate judgment proper.

1. Subdivision 1-a of section 1897 of the Penal Law describes a machine gun and provides that possession of one is a felony and that the presence of a machine gun in any place shall be presumptive evidence of its illegal possession by all the persons occupying the place where found. Such statute is constitutional and does not deny an accused due process of law. The Legislature may enact that when certain facts have been proved they shall be prima facie evidence of the existence of the main fact in question. The fact upon which the presumption is to rest must have some fair relation to the main fact. The inference of the existence of the main fact because of the existence of the fact actually proved must not be arbitrary,  [***2] and the accused must have a fair opportunity to make his defense and submit the whole case to the jury for decision after weighing the evidence. Such provision does not take away the right of trial by jury nor does it change the burden of proof.

2. Moreover, relator who was convicted of violating the section because he was in a building where a machine gun was found cannot use the writ of habeas corpus to attack his conviction on the ground that there was a denial of due process. Controverted questions of fact as to whether one imprisoned pursuant to a judgment is deprived of his liberty without due process in violation of the Federal Constitution can only be determined by a motion to vacate the judgment made in the court which granted it. The Supreme Court has no jurisdiction to determine that question upon a writ of habeas corpus.

 

2917 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


In re City of New York


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Bronx County


195 Misc. 842; 82 N.Y.S.2d 55; 1948 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3021



July 20, 1948 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Condemnation proceedings were held for a determination of the value of claimants' land that was taken by the city for a cross-borough express highway.


OVERVIEW: The claimants owned property, which was acquired by the city to provide a cross-borough express highway. Proceedings were held before the court for determination of the claimant's property. The court determined that generally the value of property that was subject to eminent domain proceedings was determined by the property's fair market value and that if there was a partial taking, the value was determined by the difference of the value of the whole property before the taking and the value of the remainder after the taking. Additionally, the court held that neither damages incurred to a business because it was required to move to another location as a result of the taking nor expenses incurred in removing personal property from property that was condemned were proper items of damages in a condemnation proceeding. The court found that its receipt of evidence, which showed lost profit, was harmless because the damages awarded were in accordance with the proper standards. Further, the court also considered improvements made by the city in advance of the condemnation in ascertaining just compensation to the claimants.


OUTCOME: The court adjusted the damage claim of one of the claimants and approved their settlement, ordered the repayment of taxes paid by some of the claimants, and ordered payment of the award to the mortgagees upon submission of proper proof.


HEADNOTES

Eminent domain -- compensation -- (1) in condemnation proceeding owner entitled to just compensation -- (2) owner entitled to fair market value -- (3) on partial taking, owner entitled to award for land appropriated and consequential damages to remaining land -- (4) lessee entitled to compensation for value of unexpired term unless lease terminates upon condemnation -- (5) option to buy property contained in lease gives tenants no right to award unless option exercised at date of condemnation -- (6) clause agreeing that award be paid to lessor without deduction effects waiver of claim by lessee -- (7) lessee erecting building on land pursuant to agreement entitled to award -- (8) where there is change of grade and lease has unexpired term of at least three years both lessor and lessee have separate right to award -- (9) mortgagee entitled to amount of mortgage plus interest payable out of award -- (10) evidence of sales to prove just compensation admissible on direct examination as well as on cross or redirect examination -- (11) view of premises by trier of facts and other  [***2] factors may result in value different from that fixed by expert opinion -- (12) incidental losses of owner excluded as speculative -- (13) proper to show that property peculiarly adapted for particular business -- (14) no award given for damage to owner's business caused by removal to another location -- (15) expenses of removal to other location not recoverable -- (16) evidence of profits, if not speculative, admissible -- (17) usable value of property for specific business admissible to fix market value -- (18) improvements enhancing value made by condemnor prior to condemnation, admissible -- (19) cost or structural value of improvements are factors -- (20) actual earnings of improved properties form factual basis for economic values -- (21) in determining value of parcels in common use, consideration given to any increment of value reflected to less valuable parcel from more valuable parcel -- (22) actual value always depends on use to which property may be put and its marketability.

1. In a condemnation proceeding for the determination of the value of lands taken for a cross-borough express highway which involves many unusually shaped parcels and numerous partial takings,  [***3] the owner of private property taken for a public purpose is entitled to just compensation judicially determined. Just compensation is an award of money for the value of the property as of the time of taking to indemnify the owner for the loss.

2. The owner of land condemned is entitled to the fair market value.

3. A partial taking of an owner's real property by condemnation, leaving the remaining land damaged in value due to the taking and to the resultant size, shape or serious sales impairment of the portion left, is an exception to the constitutional requirement of compensation only for the value of the property taken. When the property taken is physically an integral part of a larger plot and what is left is substantially deteriorated by reason of the relationship in value of that taken to the entire property, the resulting consequential damages to the remaining part are allowed as part of the sum awarded therefor and for the value of the property taken.

4. Upon condemnation, a lessee under a lease is entitled to compensation for the value of the unexpired term based upon the difference for such period between the fair annual rental value of the demised premises and the rent [***4] reserved. However, if the lease contains a clause that it shall cease upon the taking of the property for condemnation, the lessee is not entitled to any compensation or to share in the award to the owner-lessor.

5. An option to buy the property contained in a lease gives the tenants no right to the award or a share of it unless the option has been exercised at the date of the condemnation.

6. Any claim by a lessee is waived by a clause agreeing that the award will be paid to the lessor without deduction.

7. A lessee who under agreement with the owner has erected a building on the land is entitled to an award unless he has also limited or waived his right thereto.

8. Where there is a change of grade and the lease has an unexpired term of at least three years, both lessor and lessee have a separate right to an award.

9. A mortgagee who proves his claim is entitled to be paid the amount of the mortgage and interest at 4% out of the award (since the enactment of section 3-a of the General Municipal Law). While extinguished on the vesting of title, the mortgage if proved is a lien on the owner's award.

10. Evidence of sales prices to determine just compensation is admissible on [***5] direct examination and is not limited to cross or redirect examination. Sales are also admissible when included in a notice that they will be offered in evidence upon the trial.

11. While expert opinion is still the usual method of proving market value, the view of the property by the trier of the facts and his or their general knowledge of value gained from practical experience and analyses of the opinions by known appraisal checks may lead to a conclusion at variance with such testimony.

12. As real property alone is taken and it alone requires compensation, incidental losses sustained by the owner are excluded as too speculative.

13. On the question of value an owner may show any fact which he would naturally and properly bring to the attention of a buyer with whom he was negotiating a sale, but care must be exercised that the mere hope of the owner that the property might be used for some special purpose is not substituted for reality. Thus while it is proper to show that the property is particularly adapted for a particular business, the success of an enterprise does not depend upon its location alone.

14. Damage occasioned to an owner's business because he is compelled [***6] to move to another location is not an item which can be considered in a condemnation proceeding.

15. An owner cannot recover the expenses incurred in removing personal property from lands condemned to another location.

16. Profits may be recognized as an element of value and are only excluded where the evidence is too speculative.

17. The usable value of the property for any suitable legitimate business is relevant in fixing its market value. The prospective use of the property and its potential value for such use may be considered.

18. Improvements made by the taker in advance of authority in condemnation are considered in ascertaining just compensation to the owner, but not the cost of such improvements, nor their intrinsic, replacement or sound value, nor the value of their use to the taker, but only the amount of the enhancement in the value of the land made by such improvements.

19. The cost or structural value of improvements while not the measure, is a factor in determining value.

20. Except under unusual circumstances, the actual earnings of multiple dwellings and other improved properties form the factual basis for economic value.

21. In determining the value of parcels [***7] in common use, consideration was given here to the increment of value, if any, which may be reflected to the less valuable parcel from the more valuable one by reason of such use.

22. The standard practices and principles of law as well as rules and tables in common use are all entitled to due consideration. None, however, is controlling, and actual value must always depend on such factors as the use to which a property may be put and its marketability.

 

2918 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Lubin v. Finkelstein


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court, Special Term, New York County, Part I


82 N.Y.S.2d 329; 1948 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3104



July 13, 1948 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner tenants brought a proceeding to review and annul an order of the Temporary City Housing Rent Commission of the City of New York that granted defendant hotel operator the right to increase the rents payable by the tenants.


OVERVIEW: The tenants argued that in treating a return of less than six percent on the value of the hotel property as an inequity the commission exceeded its statutory authority and was arbitrary. The court disagreed. The court found that the Commission's formula was reasonable. The remaining question was whether there was evidence to sustain its findings as to values. As to the value of the land and buildings, the court held that the Commission properly accepted the last judicially reviewed city assessment for taxation as prima facie evidence of fair value. City assessments, even those not judicially reviewed, were competent evidence on the question of value. The court concluded that evidence sustained the Commission's finding of value for furniture and fixtures, notwithstanding a lower appraisal. Working capital was an item to be included, and the Commission's act in fixing it at the difference between the current assets and the current liabilities was not arbitrary or unreasonable as against the tenants.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the petition to annul the Commission's order.

 

2919 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People ex rel. Barker v. Elkin


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


City Court of New York, Mount Vernon, Sitting as Court of Special Sessions County


196 Misc. 188; 80 N.Y.S.2d 525; 1948 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2606



July 10, 1948 



DISPOSITION: The motions to dismiss the information, upon which decisions were reserved, are, therefore, denied, and the defendant is adjudged guilty of the offenses with which he is charged.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged in an information by the State of New York with keeping empty packing boxes on his premises in violation of Mount Vernon, N.Y., Bldg. Code art. 5, § 5.27. Defendant filed motions to dismiss the information.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was accused of storing empty packing boxes on his premises in violation of § 5.27. Defendant claimed that § 5.27 was a zoning regulation that was enacted without notice, that § 5.27 was unconstitutional, and that the State's information was required to be dismissed. The court convicted defendant. The court held that a city had the power to prevent fires and to protect its inhabitants, that § 5.27 was not intended as a zoning restriction, and that § 5.27 was intended as a measure of fire prevention and was constitutional. The court also held that defendant piled a huge quantity of boxes on his premises that reached a height of six feet, that there was ample proof supporting the charges, and that defendant was guilty of the offenses with which he was charged.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motions to dismiss and convicted defendant of violating the ordinance.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- storage of inflammable material -- (1) motion to dismiss information charging defendant stored empty packing boxes within area where such storage prohibited by ordinance denied and defendant found guilty -- (2) ordinance intended for fire prevention and not zoning -- (3) ordinance presumptively valid; no showing of invalid enactment -- (4) assuming ordinance intended for zoning, ordinance not rendered invalid by failure to hold public hearing and give notice -- (5) power to issue permit not delegated by common council to building commissioner -- (6) court required to take judicial notice of location of premises involved.

1. Section 5.27 of article 5 of the Building Code of the City of Mount Vernon provides that no building within the fire limits shall be used as a lumber yard, cooperage or place for storage of new or second-hand lumber, empty packing boxes or similar inflammable [***2] material, as a sawmill, feed, flour or grain mill, "except by special permission of the Common Council after approval by the Commissioner." A motion to dismiss an information charging that defendant stored empty packing boxes on premises concededly within the fire limits is denied and defendant is adjudged guilty of a violation of the building code provision.

2. The ordinance is not a zoning regulation and as a consequence it was not necessary that the provisions as to public notice and hearing provided in the Zoning Ordinance of the City of Mount Vernon (§ 25) be followed. The ordinance was intended rather as a measure of fire prevention.

3. The act is presumptively valid and the presumption attachesto the process of enactment. There is no evidence that the ordinance was not correctly adopted.

4. Assuming that the ordinance were a zoning ordinance, it would not be void for want of public notice and hearing. The provision for notice and hearing in the zoning ordinance is a self-prescribed limitation enacted by the body charged with having violated it. It cannot serve to limit the greater power vested in the legislative body by the State.

5. The ordinance is not invalid for [***3] failure to prescribe a uniform rule for the issuance of a permit. The common council may act without the approval of the commissioner and the provision for his approval in the ordinance may be disregarded in considering the validity of the ordinance. The ordinance was not intended to divest the common council of its governmental function and to delegate that function to the building commissioner.

6. The court is required to take judicial notice of the location of the premises involved (Mount Vernon City Charter, § 187-a).

 

2920 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Atkins v. Stichman


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


193 Misc. 986; 81 N.Y.S.2d 499; 1948 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2835



June 25, 1948 



DISPOSITION: The application is accordingly denied in all respects. The petition is dismissed, without costs, however, and the provision heretofore made with reference to the separate maintenance of the funds constituting the rent increase is hereby vacated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner tenants sought review of an order of respondent State Commissioner of Housing granting an increase in rent for apartments to be charged by respondent limited dividend housing corporation. An order was made that the corporation segregate the funds constituting the increased rentals.


OVERVIEW: After 1947 N.Y. Laws 341 amended N.Y. Pub. Hous. Law § 179 of the Public Housing Law so as to permit a maximum monthly rental of $ 15 per room, the maximum rent chargeable by the corporation was increased to $ 14 a room by the Commissioner. In dismissing the tenant's petition for review of that order, the court noted that there were limitations on the rates of interest limited dividend housing corporations could pay on mortgages or other debts and on the right of mortgage without the consent of the Commissioner. The formula fixing the return that such corporations could obtain was statutory. The corporation's stockholders and debenture holders had never received any dividends or interest. A hearing for the tenants would not have changed the result in light of the Commissioner's diligence, increased costs, and the absence of any interest or dividends to the corporation's investors.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the petition and vacated the order concerning segregation of the funds constituting the rent increase.


HEADNOTES

Public housing -- validity of increase in rent ordered by State Commissioner of Housing -- (1) application to set aside order of State Commissioner of Housing granting rent increase to be charged by limited dividend housing corporation denied -- (2) tenants not entitled to hearing -- (3) commissioner diligent, prudent and not remiss in discharge of duties -- (4) commissioner's action not dependent on action of Federal Office of Housing Expediter -- (5) Legislature had power to set up formula for fixing rents and formula adopted not unreasonable -- (6) rental increase of 12% proper under existing conditions.

1. Pursuant to authority contained in chapter 341 of the Laws of 1947 , amending section 179 of the Public [***2] Housing Law so as to permit a maximum monthly rental of $ 15 per room, the State Commissioner of Housing increased the maximum rent to $ 14 per room for tenants in an apartment project owned and operated by a limited dividend housing corporation. In a proceeding to review the action of the commissioner, an application to set aside the rent increase order on the ground that the tenants had no hearing is denied and the petition is dismissed.

2. There is no statutory provision for any hearing in such situation. The commissioner was not exercising a quasi-judicial function and accordingly no hearing was necessary. The powers of the commissioner were granted to avoid rental rate hearings and obviate the necessity of the tenants bearing the burden of such hearing.

3. The return made in the present proceeding indicates that the commissioner has been diligent, prudent and careful and there is no basis for a claim that he has been remiss in the discharge of his duties. The purpose of the housing legislation was to allow the commissioner to protect tenants and prevent their exploitation but it was not intended that in exercising this power the investments of others would be destroyed.

4.  [***3] The commissioner's order was not dependent upon the action of the Federal Office of Housing Expediter, and no consideration is given to the proceedings of that agency.

5. The Legislature had the power to set up a formula for the fixing of maximum rents on projects built by limited dividend corporations and the formula adopted is not unreasonable or violative of any constitutional right.

6. Considering the matter objectively and in its entirety there is no basis upon which a rental increase of 12% could be set aside in the light of existing conditions.

 

2921 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Little v. Young


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Nassau County


82 N.Y.S.2d 909; 1948 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3262



June 22, 1948 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Order Affirmed December 20, 1948


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner property owner sought an order of mandamus to compel respondent building inspector to issue to the property owner a permit for the erection upon his property of 100 bathhouses and 56 cabanas.


OVERVIEW: The property owner's ocean front property had been given a zoning permit for 12 bathhouses and 2 showers. The property owner sought to build 100 bathhouses and 56 cabanas, which was denied by the building inspector until the property owner obtained special permission from the board of appeals. The court held that the ordinance that forbade such uses of the property owner's property was unconstitutional because it failed to provide the necessary standards to have been met by applicants and was void and ordered the building inspector to issue the permit. The court found that the local legislative body was without authority to delegate its power to zone. The court stated that no discretion was given to the employee who administered the ordinance. He merely determined if the standards had been met by the applicant and the standards were to have been reasonable, clear, understandable. The court held that nullifying the special permit section had the effect of restoring the ordinance to the form it took before the amendment, which form provided that a "place of amusement" might have been established in a business district as a matter of right.


OUTCOME: The court entered judgment for the property owner and entered an order compelling the building inspector to issue the permit applied for by the property owner.

 

2922 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Garvey


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


City Magistrate's Court of New York


6 Misc. 2d 266; 79 N.Y.S.2d 456; 1948 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2022; 15 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P64,775



May 27, 1948 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, the defendant is acquitted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with disorderly conduct in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 722(2).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was arrested during a public demonstration. The arresting officer testified that he warned defendant to move on, but that she kept "pushing and shoving" toward him all the time. Defendant denied that she had been warned to move on by the officer. She stated that the police had pushed her into the gutter and that the crowd had pushed her back against the officer. She also asserted that she could not have moved if she had wanted to, because of the pressure of the crowd. The officer placed her under arrest and charged her with disorderly conduct. The court found that the police had the right to take reasonable steps to break up the demonstration. The sidewalk was blocked and traffic could not move freely as a result of the demonstration. The police officer, therefore, had the right to order defendant to move on. From all the evidence, there was doubt as to whether the police officer did order defendant to move on. Even if the order was given it was doubtful that defendant heard it. Furthermore, even if defendant wanted to move it was doubtful she could have because of the pressure of the crowd.


OUTCOME: Defendant was acquitted of the charges of disorderly conduct.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- disorderly conduct -- police may order massed pickets on busy street to move on (Penal Law, § 722; New York City Charter, § 435) -- but defendant acquitted; doubtful that she was told to move on, or heard such order, or could have moved against pressure of crowds.

1. Mass picketing on a busy street -- in this case, by 150 pickets -- even if without violence, is subject to the power of the police to order the pickets to move on (Penal Law, § 722; New York City Charter, § 435).

2. However, the defendant, who was a waitress and a member of the picketing union, is acquitted. From all the evidence, it is doubtful whether the policeman did order her to move on, or, if such order were given that defendant heard it, or, even if she heard it, that she could have moved against the pressure of the crowd.

 

2923 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People ex rel. Kahan v. Creative Age Press, Inc.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


City Magistrate's Court of New York, Borough of Manhattan, Lower Manhattan Court


192 Misc. 188; 79 N.Y.S.2d 198; 1948 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2380



May 19, 1948 



DISPOSITION: Defendant's motion to dismiss the summons is granted and the defendant is discharged.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a prosecution for the selling of an alleged obscene book, defendant filed a motion to dismiss.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with having committed a misdemeanor under N.Y. Penal Law § 1141(1) by reason of having sold a book that he published. The People alleged that the book was obscene, lewd, lascivious, filthy, indecent, and disgusting. Defendant's counsel had offered as evidence a collection of letters that were claimed by him to be written by prominent members of the community and stated their opinions of the book. In granting the motion to dismiss, the court found that it could not find by any prevailing judicial standard that the book would have a sexually demoralizing effect upon its readers. The court found that the book was not obscene or otherwise within the condemnation of N.Y. Penal Law § 1141(1). Factors that had to be considered included the theme of the book, the degree of sincerity of purpose evidence in it, its literary worth, the channels used in its distribution, contemporary attitudes toward the literary treatment of sexual behavior, and the types of readers reasonably expected to secure it for perusal. The court also sustained the objection of the People's counsel to the reception in evidence of defendant's collection of solicited letters.


OUTCOME: The court granted the motion to dismiss and discharged defendant.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- obscenity -- (1) in prosecution charging sale of obscene book (Penal Law, § 1141, subd. 1) letters from prominent members of community stating their views of book not admissible -- (2) but reviews by literary critics disseminated in ordinary course of employment admissible -- (3) book allegedly showing condition of national morality reflected by individual morality not obscene -- (4) subdivision 1 of section 1141 of Penal Law not void for uncertainty.

1. In a prosecution for the sale of an allegedly obscene book (Penal Law, § 1141, subd. 1), letters solicited by defendant publisher from allegedly prominent members of the community stating their opinions of the book may not be received in evidence. ( People v. Viking Press, 147 Misc. 813, not followed.) There is no opportunity to test the qualifications of the writers or their position in the community or the nature of the persuasion used to obtain their views. The writers should be subject to the same scrutiny as are other witnesses.

2. On the other hand,  [***2] opinions of professional literary critics publicly disseminated in the ordinary course of their employment are proper aids to the court in weighing an author's sincerity of purpose and the book's literary worth. A collection of clippings established to have been taken from newspapers and periodicals of general circulation containing reviews of the book by literary critics for those publications is received in evidence.

3. The author testified that his primary purpose was to express the view that, at any given time, the condition of national morality is reflected by the then current condition of individual morality and that national policies of compromise at the expense of principle occur when it has become common practice for individual moral standards to be disregarded for the sake of material advantage. The critics generally were impressed by his sincerity of purpose. Every passage in the book seems to have been included to express the author's theme. Considering the theme of the book, the degree of sincerity of purpose evident in it, its literary worth, the channels used in its distribution, contemporary attitudes toward the literary treatment of sexual behavior and the types [***3] of readers reasonably to be expected to secure it for perusal, as factors in an evaluation of the extent to which the book as a whole would have a demoralizing effect on its readers, the book is not obscene or otherwise condemned by the statute.

4. Subdivision 1 of section 1141 of the Penal Law is not void for uncertainty.

 

2924 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Andrews v. State


Claim No. 28516


Court of Claims of New York


192 Misc. 429; 79 N.Y.S.2d 479; 1948 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2423



May 19, 1948 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant state made a motion to dismiss a claim by claimant Native American for her share of funds paid to the Onondaga Nation for land purchased by the state.


OVERVIEW: The Native American brought an action as an enrolled member of the Onondaga Nation, not residing on the reservation, alleging certain rights in communal lands. Upon consideration, the court granted the state's motion to dismiss. The court held that the Onondaga Nation did not have a relationship as such with the state but that the tribe was a "ward" of the federal government. Although the tribe had retained its autonomy in many ways, the court found that the federal government had the sole power to legislate as to the distribution of their lands pursuant to the United States Constitution. The court also noted that statutory authority was required before a tribe could sue or be sued or an individual in the name of the tribe could bring an action. Therefore, the court held that it had no jurisdiction as a state court regarding the Native American's claim. The court also held that the Native American did not have title or a vendible interest in the communal property. The court also found that the moneys paid for the property were not annuities but a lump sum paid pursuant to agreement with the Council of Chiefs, which was equitably distributed to those residing on the reservation.


OUTCOME: The court granted the state's motion to dismiss the Native American's claim for funds regarding the state's purchase of communal property.


HEADNOTES

Indians -- jurisdiction of State courts -- (1) claim against State by Onondaga Indian for her share of money paid by State to Onondaga Nation for land; claimant, nonresident on reservation, who had no title or enforcible right to communal property, claims State liable as guardian of Indian wards; claim dismissed for lack of jurisdiction -- (2) status of Indian tribes discussed; self government of Onondaga Nation noted; land disputes customarily settled by Council of Chiefs -- (3) no obligation by State shown; judgment against State would affect property rights of Onondaga Nation and would deny inherent sovereignty of tribe with no showing of failure to follow applicable custom.

1. Claimant, as an enrolled member of the Onondaga Nation not residing on the reservation, alleges that she was the possessor of certain rights in the communal lands of said Nation which have been abridged and she demands judgment against the State because it purchased some of said land and paid the agreed price to the Council of Chiefs who distributed the proceeds equally among the enrolled [***2] members of the tribe living on the reservation. The lands involved are communal lands and are not lands allotted to, possessed, or occupied by claimant. They were acquired by agreement not by appropriation. In determining who are the communal owners entitled to share in the distribution of a fund under a treaty, the court will follow Indian laws and customs so far as they do not conflict with the laws of the United States or the purposes of the treaty or natural law and justice. Claimant had no title or enforcible right to the communal property nor did she have a vendible interest in same. She does not state whether she is a full-blooded Onondaga, whether she was born on the reservation, whether she has ever lived there or whether she at any time was an active member of the tribe. She demands judgment on the theory that the State paid the Council of Chiefs without insuring her participation in the distribution. She alleges that by reason of an existing fiduciary relationship of State guardianship over its Indian wards, the State is liable although no claim of fraud is made. The action is dismissed for lack of jurisdiction. ( Seminole Indians v. United States, 316 U.S. 286, 62 [***3] S. Ct. 1049, 86 L. Ed. 1480, distinguished.)

2. The status of Indian tribes, particularly that of the Six Nations which includes the Onondaga Nation, with respect to the United States Government and the State of New York, is discussed and it is noted that the rights which belong to self government are vested in the Onondagas, and that their Indian laws and customs control in all international affairs of the tribe when not expressly implied by statute. It is noted also that the Onondaga Nation, unlike others, has never accepted or relied upon the State courts but has retained its tribal integrity and its sovereignty by Council of Chiefs which is a tribunal with original and general jurisdiction over local affairs of reservation members. Land disputes are settled by the chiefs.

3. Moreover, claimant has sought to disregard completely the organization of which she claims to be a member. She has provided the court with no statute which recognizes an obligation running from the State to any and every individual Indian belonging to the Nation nor has she pointed to any treaty or agreement where such an obligation was required or mentioned. These moneys were not annuities but the total [***4] amount was paid at once to the tribe and no complaint is made that the Council of Chiefs failed to disburse it equitably to all members residing on the reservation. Property rights of the Onondaga tribe and legal and political relations affecting it would seem to be involved in the proceeding. The Nation is not a party. The ultimate result sought, namely a judgment against the State, would affect the property rights and relations of the tribe. In the absence of legislative action bestowing on individual Indians the right to litigate internal questions relating to their tribal property in the Court of Claims and conferring jurisdiction to determine such controversies, this court should not assume jurisdiction.

 

2925 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Suomen Pankki v. Bell


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court, Special Term, New York County, Part III


80 N.Y.S.2d 821; 1948 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2668



May 17, 1948 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, Superintendent of Banks for the State of New York, as liquidator of a Japanese bank licensed to do limited banking business in the state through a local agency, moved to dismiss the third cause of action filed by plaintiff, a Finnish banking corporation seeking the return of certain funds transferred to the agency, on the basis that defendant's action was not commenced within the time period prescribed under N.Y. Banking Law § 625.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, Superintendent of Banks for the State of New York, as liquidator of a Japanese bank, moved to dismiss plaintiff's third cause of action seeking return of certain funds transferred to the bank on the ground that it was not commenced within the prescribed time period under N.Y. Banking Law § 625. Plaintiff was a Finnish banking corporation. The insolvent Japanese bank was licensed to transact limited banking business in the state through an agency. Plaintiff alleged that § 625 was inapplicable because the third cause of action sought return of a particular, earmarked fund, not part of the agency's assets, to which plaintiff alone was entitled when liquidation began. Plaintiff claimed that under the circumstances, the statute of limitations in § 625 had been tolled. Defendant's motion was granted because the Federal government had never "occupied the field" in such a manner or to such an extent that the State of New York was without power to liquidate the New York agency of the Japanese bank in accordance with state banking laws. Further, the six-month statute of limitations in § 625 was mandatory and could not be tolled.


OUTCOME: Defendant's motion to dismiss was granted. Plaintiff's allegations established nothing more than a debtor-creditor relationship with the agency with respect to the funds sought to be recovered. The federal government did not occupy the field precluding the State of New York from liquidating the New York agency of a foreign bank, and the applicable statute of limitations was mandatory and could not be tolled.

 

2926 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Neufeld v. O'Dwyer


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


192 Misc. 538; 79 N.Y.S.2d 53; 1948 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2361



April 12, 1948 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff resident filed suit against defendant city agencies and officials, seeking to enjoin them from proceeding with a housing project. In response, the city agencies and officials filed a motion to dismiss.


OVERVIEW: The resident's action sought to block the city agencies and officials from developing a housing project involving four separate development sites and from other acts related to the project, including the sale of notes or certificates of indebtedness and bonds, the payment of moneys pursuant to a subsidy-guaranty contract, and the condemnation of land in the areas selected for the project. The court held dismissal of the action was appropriate in part because the complaint failed to allege facts sufficient to make out a good case against the city agencies and officials on any of the grounds mentioned in the complaint. In particular, the court found the resident's allegation that families with annual incomes of more than $ 4,500 per year were eligible for tenancy in the buildings proposed was insufficient without more to overcome a presumption that the eligibility limits ultimately established for the project would be fixed in conformity with the requirements of N.Y. Pub. Hous. Law § 3.


OUTCOME: The court granted the motion to dismiss by the city agencies and officials in the resident's action for injunctive relief.


HEADNOTES

Public housing -- validity of project -- (1) in action to enjoin New York City housing authority from proceeding with housing project, motion to dismiss complaint for insufficiency granted -- (2) claim that project is not "low rent housing" without merit; under authority of Constitution therefor, Legislature defines "families of low income" as those "who cannot afford to pay enough to cause private enterprise * * * to build a sufficient supply of * * * dwellings" -- (3) claim that families with incomes of more than $ 4,500 will be eligible for tenancy conclusory; even if true, such families are of low income if private enterprise does not build dwellings for them; public housing projects not limited to lowest rents and lowest income limits as previously set -- (4) charge that majority of tenants in private properties pay less per room unit than that planned for project immaterial -- (5) legality of project not affected by fact that slum clearance not involved (Public Housing Law, § 3, subd. 14) -- (6) authorization of project by Constitution  [***2] answers charges of class discrimination, loss of potential tax income and depreciation of adjacent real estate values -- (7) charge of violation of zoning laws rejected; charge of illegality of donation of city's services without merit -- (8) subsidy-guaranty for project not illegal because approved by board of estimate only and not by city council also -- (9) location of site as affected by tax arrears and relative cost of acquisition matter solely for administrative judgment of authorities -- (10) cost of acquisition will be properly set in condemnation proceedings -- (11) inclusion of four separate developments in project not improper -- (12) charge that city's debt limitations will be exceeded mere conclusion.

1. In this taxpayer's action to enjoin the Mayor of the City of New York, other members of the board of estimate and the New York City housing authority from proceeding with a housing project, a motion to dismiss the complaint is granted on the ground that the complaint fails to allege facts sufficient to make out a cause of action.

2. Plaintiff alleges that the project is not "low rent housing" within the meaning of section 1 of article XVIII of the Constitution and [***3] subdivision 23 of section 3 of the Public Housing Law since eligibility to tenancy in the proposed project will be increased to include families with annual incomes of $ 4,500 or more per year, and a monthly rental of $ 12.50 per room is contemplated, as against former rentals of $ 6.05 to $ 7.00. Under article XVIII the Legislature may provide for low rent housing for persons of low income "as defined by law" (§ 1), and the Legislature has defined low rent housing to mean dwellings within the financial reach of "families of low income" (Public Housing Law, § 3, subd. 23), and the latter have been defined as families who are in the low income groups and "who cannot afford to pay enough to cause private enterprise in their municipality to build a sufficient supply of adequate, safe and sanitary dwellings" (Public Housing Law, § 3, subd. 18). The Legislature has thus fixed the criterion of what constitutes low rent housing or what constitutes families of low income without reference to a precise amount in dollars and cents.

3. The allegation as to families with incomes of more than $ 4,500 per year is made upon information and belief and is a conclusory statement. It is insufficient [***4] without more to overcome the presumption that when the eligibility limits are established they will be fixed in conformity with law. Moreover, assuming the truth thereof, it does not necessarily follow that the project is illegal for such families are "families of low income" if they cannot afford to pay enough to cause private enterprise to build a sufficient supply of adequate, safe and sanitary dwellings. Furthermore, there is no legal requirement that public housing projects must be limited to the very lowest rents and the very lowest income limits that have been previously set, or that the needs of all families of the very lowest incomes be satisfied before public housing may be constructed for the benefit of other low income groups.

4. Nor does the allegation that rentals paid by the majority of tenants of privately owned multiple dwellings in the city of New York are less than $ 12.50 per room per month, avail plaintiff, for families of low income, as defined by the statute, are those which cannot afford to pay enough to cause private enterprise to build a sufficient supply of adequate dwellings, and no facts have been pleaded to show at what rental private enterprise could [***5] or would build adequate dwellings.

5. The Constitution authorizes the Legislature to provide low rent housing for persons of low income "or" for the clearance and rehabilitation of insanitary areas "or for both such purposes", and the Legislature has so provided (Public Housing Law, § 3, subd. 14). Consequently, the fact that slum clearance is not called for here does not impair the legality of the project.

6. The fact that construction will be pursuant to the authorization contained in the Constitution is sufficient answer to the charge of illegality based on class discrimination and to a charge of loss of potential tax income from competition with private housing developments. The same is true with respect to the claim that the construction will produce overcrowding and will depreciate adjacent real estate values.

7. The charge that the project will violate zoning laws is a conclusion without supporting facts and the claim that the city is illegally agreeing to donate sewage, drainage and sanitation, as well as beds of streets to be closed, is without merit in view of sections 99, 100 and 124 of the Public Housing Law which expressly authorize such donations.

8. The subsidy-guaranty [***6] contract for the project is not illegal because it has not received the approval of the city council, but only that of the board of estimate (Public Housing Law, § 97; Administrative Code of City of New York, § 223-3.0; N.Y. City Charter, §§ 295, 300).

9. The attack on the location of one of the sites, based upon a comparison of the tax arrears thereon and those of other areas considered and upon a comparison of the relative cost of acquisition, goes solely to the administrative judgment of the city housing authority, the board of estimate, the city planning commission and other agencies legally empowered to determine the sites.

10. The charge that it is intended to acquire one of the sites at a price greater than its assessed valuation, which is claimed to be the fair market value, must also be rejected since the property will be acquired by eminent domain and the amount to be paid will be that which the court in charge of the condemnation proceedings fixes.

11. There is nothing in the law indicating that it is illegal to combine four separate developments into a single project for financing purposes.

12. The charges that the subsidy-guaranty contract and the approving resolution [***7] of the board of estimate create a city indebtedness in excess of the city's debt limitations are nothing more than conclusions, and insufficient facts are set forth to support them.

 

2927 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Smith v. Buffalo


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Erie County


191 Misc. 439; 78 N.Y.S.2d 540; 1948 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2277



March 30, 1948 



DISPOSITION: The motion is hereby denied, with costs to the respondents in the amount of $ 10. The stay granted by the Hon. Leo J. Hagerty is hereby vacated and set aside. Prepare order.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff taxpayer filed an action against defendants, a police commissioner, a city, and a mayor, for an injunction restraining them from destroying slot machines. The taxpayer also sought to restrain defendants from enforcing an order of destruction directing them to return the machines from the premises from which they were seized or post security for their safe return.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayer alleged that the commissioner and his subordinates threatened to seize machines from various citizens and places in the city and have them destroyed. The taxpayer claimed that she did not have an adequate remedy at law for the relief demanded. However, the taxpayer did not claim ownership of or interest in the machines and did not claim that she had any other interest in the proceedings other than as a taxpayer anxious to prevent waste. The court found that there was no urgency in the matter. The statutes that were questioned by the taxpayer had been on the statute books for almost 50 years. Further, the supreme court did not have jurisdiction in equity to grant to the taxpayer an injunction against defendants as prayed for in her amended complaint. Moreover, because the taxpayer did not claim any interest in any of the machines or in the proceedings, she could not maintain an action for an injunction.


OUTCOME: The court denied the motion for an injunction filed by the taxpayer. The stay in proceedings was vacated and set aside.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- taxpayer's action -- (1) court has no power to grant temporary injunction in action by taxpayer (General Municipal Law, § 51) to enjoin destruction of slot machines by city on ground that if Penal Law (§§ 982-985) unconstitutional city will be subjected to damage suits by owners of machines, thus wasting city funds -- (2) court has no inherent power to declare statute unconstitutional except where litigant's personal rights affected -- (3) matter not so urgent that court should ignore exact procedure -- (4) although plaintiff disclaims ownership of slot machines, she is seeking to lay foundation for damage claims; court will not permit true owners to protect their unlawful property and activities through a "front".

1. In an action by a taxpayer under section 51 of the General Municipal Law to restrain certain officials and the police department of a city from destroying slot machines seized by the police on the grounds that sections [***2] 982, 983, 984 and 985 of the Penal Law, which authorize the seizure and destruction of such machines, are unconstitutional, and that if defendants should deprive the rightful owners of the machines and should destroy said machines, defendants would, if the statutes be unconstitutional, subject the city to damage claims thereby causing waste of the property and funds of the city, the taxpayer's motion for a temporary injunction is denied. The Supreme Court has no jurisdiction in equity to grant said taxpayer a temporary injunction as prayed for in the complaint.

2. There is no inherent power in a court of equity to set aside a statute as unconstitutional or to right a wrong, except in a controversy between litigants where it is sought to enforce rights or to enjoin, redress or punish wrongs affecting the individual life, liberty or property of one or more of the litigants. Special, peculiar personal rights of the complaining litigant must be affected. A citizen and taxpayer has no right to bring before a court for review the acts of a department of government simply because he is one of many such citizens and taxpayers.

3. Where a matter is of urgency and of transcending public [***3] importance, the court should not permit any delay and it would be justified in not overly concerning itself with exact procedure. This matter is not such a one. The statutes have been upon the statute books of this State in nearly their present form for almost fifty years and were last amended in 1934.

4. Moreover, plaintiff taxpayer comes before the court here seeking to lay the foundation for a flood of litigation against the municipality for destruction of the slot machines. She escapes the "unclean hands" doctrine by her disavowal of any interest in the machines. Nowhere have the names of the true owners appeared. A court of equity ought not permit itself to be used as a forum wherein persons engaged in activities contrary to law may use a "front" in an effort to protect their contraband property and in an effort to permit themselves to continue their unlawful activities secure in the half-light of anonymity.

 

2928 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People ex rel. Pellegrino v. 70 Realty Corp.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


City Magistrate's Court of New York, Borough of Manhattan, Municipal Term Court


192 Misc. 122; 78 N.Y.S.2d 265; 1948 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2244



March 29, 1948 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with a violation of an order of a fire commissioner, under New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code § 487e-2.0(d), that directed the installation of an interior fire alarm system and the provision of a watchman time detector system as described in § 487e-2.0. Defendant sought to challenge the validity of the fire commissioner's order.


OVERVIEW: An action was brought against defendant for the failure to comply with the fire commissioner's direction, which required defendant to install certain fire safety devices. Defendant admitted that no effort was made to comply with the direction, but contended that there was no obligation on its part to do so, inasmuch as his building was not a hotel within the contemplation of the § 487e-2.0(d). The defendant has mistaken its remedy. The court held that it had no jurisdiction to review the facts for the purpose of determining whether the fire commissioner's order, which represented the exercised discretion of that official, was reasonable or necessary. The only question, which the court could consider was whether defendant did in fact comply with the fire commissioner's direction. Inasmuch as there was no such compliance, defendant was found guilty as charged. No valid reason was presented to justify taking the matter outside the jurisdiction of the New York City Board of Standards and Appeals. Having failed to exhaust its statutory remedy, defendant could not evade its responsibility by asking the court to presume the powers of the Board.


OUTCOME: The court convicted defendant.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- violation of order of fire commissioner -- (1) defendant owner of apartment hotel found guilty of failureto comply with order of fire commissioner to install fire alarm system and watchman time detector system in building (Administrative Code of City of New York, § 487e-2.0, subd. d) -- (2) court may not determine whether order reasonable and necessary where owner of premises failed to exhaust statutory remedy by appeal to Board of Standards and Appeals (New York City Charter, § 666, subd. 6).

1. The fire commissioner directed defendant corporation to install an interior fire alarm system and to provide a watchman time detector system, as described in section 487e-2.0 of the Administrative Code of the City of New York, in a fifteen-story apartment hotel owned and operated by defendant and containing seventy-three units rented on a yearly or monthly basis, twenty-four units rented by day or week, and a restaurant. That section authorized the commissioner to order such installations, within his discretion, in [***2] hotels and certain other types of structures. Having failed to comply with this direction, defendant is found guilty under subdivision d of that section of the Administrative Code.

2. The court has no jurisdiction to review the facts to determine whether the order is reasonable or necessary. Subdivision 6 of section 666 of the New York City Charter provides for review of the order by the Board of Standards and Appeals. Having failed to exhaust its statutory remedy, defendant may not place the responsibility for the functions of the Board of Standards and Appeals upon the court.

 

2929 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Curtis v. McWilliams Dredging Co.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


City Court of the City of New York, New York County


191 Misc. 1022; 78 N.Y.S.2d 317; 1948 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2245; 14 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P64,352



February 24, 1948 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Amended April 2, 1948


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff former employees filed their action to recover overtime wages, an equal amount as liquidated damages, and attorney's fees under the Federal Fair Labor Standards Act of 1938 (Act), 29 U.S.C.S. § 201 et seq., from defendant former employer.


OVERVIEW: Three former employees sought to recover overtime wages withheld by the former employer. The employer claimed that the former employees were not entitled to the benefits of the Act because neither the employer nor the employees were engaged in work essential to interstate commerce. The court ruled that the former employees' work was sufficiently closely related to the movement of commerce to justify a holding that they were "engaged in commerce." The court held that the former employer was not relieved of its obligations to pay overtime wages under the Act by the passage of the Portal-to-Portal Act of 1947, 29 U.S.C.S. § 251 et seq., because the statute was retroactive in its scope and was an attempt to exonerate employers from the consequences of having failed in the past to comply with the terms of the Act. The court determined that the former employees had a vested right to their wages for services already rendered and that application of the Portal-to-Portal Act would violate ex post facto principals. The court held that the former employees were entitled to recover the unpaid overtime wages, as Congress was without power to deprive them of the wages they had already earned.


OUTCOME: The court entered judgment in favor of the former employees in their action to recover unpaid overtime wages. The court awarded prejudgment interest on the unpaid wages. The court ordered the former employer to pay the costs of suit.


HEADNOTES

Master and servant -- wages -- Fair Labor Standards Act of 1938 -- (1) defendants, constructing airbase for United States War Department in Greenland, maintained office in New York where plaintiffs were employed as bookkeeper and watchmen respectively; in action for overtime wages, liquidated damages and attorney's fees under Federal Fair Labor Standards Act, plaintiffs entitled to recover for overtime alone -- (2) plaintiffs engaged in interstate commerce and so within terms of Fair Labor Standards Act -- (3) Federal Portal-to-Portal Act, passed while actions pending, exonerated employers for failure to comply with Fair Labor Standards Act where omission was in good faith and inreliance on administrative regulation and defendants here were covered thereby since evidence shows their reliance in good faith on orders of War Department -- (4) however, Portal-to-Portal Act unconstitutional when applied to plaintiffs with respect to overtime wages since, contrary to terms of Fifth Amendment, it deprives plaintiffs of earnings acquired  [***2] in past -- (5) plaintiffs' claim for liquidated damages under Fair Labor Standards Act not entitled to same protection as claim for overtime wages; judgment of Congress that damage granted under Fair Labor Standards Act be disallowed under certain circumstances not subject to inquiry -- (6) same principle applies to allowance for attorney's fees -- (7) plaintiffs not entitled to interest under section 480 of Civil Practice Act -- (8) plaintiffs entitled to costs under New York statute.

1. Defendants, under a cost-plus contract with the War Department, were constructing an airbase for the Government in Greenland and, for administrative and executive purposes, they maintained a New York office in which plaintiffs were employed and which was responsible for obtaining supplies and materials and sending them to Greenland. At this office were kept all the records of materials, supplies, equipment and the like. Plaintiff bookkeeper-accountant and plaintiff watchmen sue under the Federal Fair Labor Standards Act of 1938 to recover additional compensation for overtime work, an equal amount as liquidated damages, and attorney's fees. The period of employment was from 1942 to 1944. Plaintiffs [***3] are entitled to recover only the overtime wages.

2. Employers' contention that plaintiffs are not entitled to the benefits of the act because they were not engaged in interstate commerce or in any work essential thereto lacks validity in view of the constant stream of men and materials from the United States to Greenland and return. Plaintiff bookkeeper-accountant was responsible for the recording of data relating to the interstate and foreign transportation of men and materials and plaintiff watchmen at the office not only guarded the documents but protected the supplies destined for Greenland. All three employees come clearly within the Act.

3. Section 9 of the Federal Portal-to-Portal Act provides that no employer shall be subject to liability on account of failure to pay minimum wages or overtime compensation under the Fair Labor Standards Act of 1938 if he pleads and proves that the act or omission complained of was in good faith and in reliance on any administrative regulation and defendants contend that their failure to pay was in good faith and in reliance upon orders and rulings of the War Department. It is asserted that it was the view of the War Department that the [***4] Fair Labor Standards Act did not apply to firms doing work similar to theirs and that, even if such views were invalid, they were, nevertheless, relied on in good faith. From an analysis of the testimony and the records it does not appear that the Secretary of War at any time recognized the applicability of the statute to employees like the plaintiffs. So far as defendants are concerned, their negotiations and dealings were entirely with the War Department. In no event could defendants be reimbursed by the Government for any wages disbursed unless their payments were in strict accordance with the rulings from the department. It is plain that there were orders and rulings relating to the payment of wages directed to defendants and that defendants complied therewith. As between the Wage and Hour Administrator and the War Department, the defendants had to abide by the rulings of the latter with which it was dealing. It could not with impunity act in defiance of those rulings.

4. Since the defense by the statute is a bar to the action, plaintiffs urge that the statute is not constitutional when applied to them. The actions were pending when the Portal-to-Portal Act was passed and [***5] it is said to be invalid under the Fifth Amendment to the United States Constitution because, reaching back into the past, it deprives plaintiffs of property -- earnings acquired in the past. While retroactive legislation is not beyond the power of a Legislature to enact, it calls for justification and no justification was expressed in the Congressional statement of findings and policy in part I of the act. For services already rendered, the employee had a vested right to his wages. The Fifth Amendment looks to the substance of owners' rights and these have been destroyed. An obstacle has been interposed to plaintiffs' right to recovery where none had previously existed. Congress adopted the invalidity of the rulings of the Secretary of War as the basis, not for isolating him or the Government from liability, but for outlawing the plaintiffs' claims against their employers. In the absence of findings of an overwhelming public need to deny compensation for work done, courts ought not to rationalize Congressional action in an effort to support that denial. Congress was without power to deprive plaintiffs of the wages they had already earned whether the power was attempted to be [***6] exercised by direct repeal or, as in this instance, by reference to a standard erected by the War Department.

5. As to plaintiffs' claim for liquidated damages, different considerations apply. The Fair Labor Standards Act does not authorize payment of interest as such but it has provided a substitute, in effect, 100% interest which is deemed to be damages for withholding the wages. The computation of damage for withholding sums due has always been a subject within legislative control even when the Legislature has acted retroactively. It was the judgment of Congress that the item of damage heretofore allowed a plaintiff in a suit under the Fair Labor Standards Act ought, under certain circumstances, be disallowed and the soundness of its judgment in this matter cannot be inquired into. The two branches of recovery must be treated separately from a constitutional standpoint. It cannot be said it would be arbitrary to deny a recovery beyond the strict overtime, where overtime payment was withheld, not willfully or deliberately or in defiance of the statute but pursuant to the mature and considered judgment of a department of the Government with which the employer had his dealings.

 [***7] 6. The principle relating to "double payment" applies with equal force to an allowance for attorney's fees for it is a rule of the common law that the expense of carrying on a law suit is not an item of compensation to the prevailing party. The power to deny interest or damages by way of interest from a recovery includes the power to deny attorney's fees as an item of recovery, even retroactively.

7. Normally the plaintiffs suing for unpaid wages would be entitled to interest under the New York statute (Civ. Prac. Act, § 480) but, since the Portal-to-Portal Act of 1947 governs and Congress intended to relieve defendants from all liability, except where it was beyond its power to do so, there can be no interest.

8. Plaintiffs, however, may include costs under the New York statute.

 

2930 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


La Placa v. Hutcheson


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Monroe County


191 Misc. 27; 79 N.Y.S.2d 355; 1948 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2404



February 20, 1948 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a motion to dismiss plaintiff's personal injury action against him on the ground that he was not a resident of New York and the court did not have jurisdiction over his person as a result. Service of the summons was made upon defendant pursuant to N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 52, as amended by 1937 N.Y. Laws 490. Defendant claimed that § 52 violated N.Y. Const. art. VI, § 11, relating to the powers and jurisdiction of county courts.


OVERVIEW: The enactment of N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 52 was caused by the legislature in order to give New York residents a remedy against non-resident users of New York highways. The legislation was based on a legal theory that when a court acquired jurisdiction of the subject matter, it also acquired jurisdiction over the person of a nonresident involved in an accident upon the New York highways. Under § 52 it was presumed that a defendant, by such operation of a motor vehicle, knew the law and gave his consent that a summons served upon the Secretary of State had the same force and effect as if personally served on him within the state. 1937 N.Y. Laws 490 added the following language: "and within the territorial jurisdiction of the court from which the summons issued." The court held that, by implication, the legislature had conferred jurisdiction upon all courts that had jurisdiction of the subject matter, jurisdiction over the person of a defendant when service was made as provided for in N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 52. Applying the rule of implied consent, the court found no conflict between provisions of the New York State Constitution conferring jurisdiction upon county courts and § 52.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motion to dismiss.


HEADNOTES

Process -- service of summons -- County Court -- jurisdiction -- (1) motion to dismiss complaint in action in County Court to recover damages for personal injuries resulting from alleged negligent use of highways by nonresident, where summons served by mail on Secretary of State (Vehicle and Traffic Law, § 52), denied -- (2) although State Constitution (art. VI, § 11) limits jurisdiction of County Courts to residents, jurisdiction may be conferred by consent.

1. Service of the summons, in this action for damages for personal injuries arising out of the alleged negligent use of the highways by nonresidents, has been made pursuant to section 52 of the Vehicle and Traffic Law. That section provides for service by mail upon the Secretary of State and further provides that the operation of a motor vehicle in this State shall signify an agreement that any summons so served shall be of the same force and validity as if served personally within the State and within the territorial jurisdiction of the court from which the summons issues. By such service the County Court acquired jurisdiction, and a motion to dismiss the complaint is denied.

 [***2] 2. Section 11 of article VI of the State Constitution provides that jurisdiction of County Courts shall not be extended to authorize an action for the recovery of a sum in excess of $ 3,000 or in which any person not a resident of the county is the defendant unless such defendant have an office for the transaction of business within the county and the cause of action arose therein. There is no conflict between this constitutional provision and section 52 of the Vehicle and Traffic Law. Concededly the court had jurisdiction of the subject matter. Jurisdiction over the parties may be conferred by consent.

 

2931 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Amalgamated Housing Corp. v. Kelly


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Bronx County


193 Misc. 961; 82 N.Y.S.2d 577; 1948 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3170



February 18, 1948 



DISPOSITION: The judgment is, therefore, granted for the relief requested in the petition. Settle judgment.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a proceeding by plaintiff limited dividend housing company to condemn a parcel of real property for low-rent housing, defendants opposed the condemnation on the ground of the unconstitutionality of the provision in N.Y. Pub. Housing Law § 135 which made a certificate "conclusive evidence" that property was necessary for public use. The matter was before the trial court for decision.


OVERVIEW: The housing company sought to condemn a parcel of real property for low-rent housing for persons of low income. Defendants urged that § 135 was unconstitutional because it provided that a certificate issued by the State Housing Commissioner was "conclusive evidence" that a property had been acquired for a public use. The trial court construed the term "conclusive evidence" as used in § 135 in light of the presumption favoring a statute's constitutionality. It concluded that the legislature had intended to give great weight to the certificate and to regard the matters certified as incontrovertible and conclusive, provided the certificate had been issued according to law. The trial court found no basis for nullifying the determination of the commissioner. Further, the trial court found that a preference to be given in the housing to members of the garment trade had not made the taking for a private use. The taking of property for a housing project could be for a public use, despite preferences in housing matters, as long as the persons benefitted satisfied the statutory definition of low income and there was no discrimination.


OUTCOME: The trial court granted the relief requested in the petition.


HEADNOTES

Public housing -- condemnation -- commissioner's certificate as "conclusive evidence" of necessity -- (1) Public Housing Law (§§ 134, 135) providing that certificate of State Housing Commissioner shall be "conclusive evidence" that acquisition of property in public interest and necessary for public use does not bar review of commissioner's determination; certificate "conclusive" only if issued in manner prescribed by law and with reasonable basis; so construed, statute not unconstitutional -- (2) statute presumed constitutional in construing words "conclusive evidence" -- (3) project of limited dividend housing company to condemn real property for low-rent housing for persons of low income not for private use although sponsored by trade union of which 30% of tenant-stockholders are members -- (4) judgment granted for relief requested in petition in proceeding to condemn real property for low-rent housing.

1.
